Linguistic Custom vs. Metaphor: The Crucial Condition
Distinguishing true linguistic custom from metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between genuine linguistic custom and metaphorical usage by identifying the presence or absence of external indicators.

Core Idea
A meaning that requires a clue or mental link is metaphor, not custom; true custom is self-evident from the word.

The passage warns that many common expressions are actually metaphors, not custom. For a mufti, this distinction is critical: when interpreting contracts or oaths, only meanings that are clearly conventional without need for context can be treated as custom. This lesson sharpens that diagnostic skill.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of linguistic custom
Linguistic custom is a conventional meaning understood from the bare wording without any external clues. Example: 'bayt' meaning 'room'.
This is a haqiqa 'urfiyya.
2
Understand the condition of self-evidence
If understanding the intended meaning requires a contextual indicator (qarina) or an intellectual relation ('alaqa 'aqliyya), it is not custom but metaphor.
The key is whether the meaning is immediately clear without extra information.
3
Recognize a contextual indicator (qarina)
A qarina is an external clue present in the speech or situation. Example: carrying a small stick while swearing to kill indicates painful beating, not literal death.
This indicator is textual or situational.
4
Recognize an intellectual relation ('alaqa 'aqliyya)
An intellectual relation is a mental link between agent and place, such as 'the court ruled' meaning the judge ruled. This is metaphor because the act is attributed to the place, not the agent.
This relation is cognitive, not textual.
5
Apply the test to examples
Given an expression, ask: Can the intended meaning be understood from the word alone in this community? If yes, it is linguistic custom. If it requires a qarina or 'alaqa, it is metaphor.
The burden is on the mufti to prove the custom.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
كان نوعها وقيمتها حتى الورق النقدي اليوم مع نقد فضي مسكوك بوزن معين، وقيمة محددة.
أن الدراهم في الأصل
وكذلك استعمال لفظ «البيت» في بعض البلدان بمعنى «الغرفة» وفي بعضها بمعنى «الدار» بجملتها .
وهذا النوع من العرف هو في الحقيقة من قبيل اللغة الخاصة لأصحابه، فإذا احتاج فهم المعنى المقصود إلى قرينة أو علاقة عقلية لم يكن ذلك عرفاً بل هو من قبيل المجاز .
مثال القرينة : ما لو حمل إنسان عصا صغيرة وحلف ليقتلن فلاناً بها، فإنه يفهم بهذه القرينة أنَّ مراده بالقتل الضرب المؤلم. وكذا لو قال لآخر: «وهبتك هذا الشيء بعشرة دنانير». فإنه يفهم بقرينة البدل أن مراده بالهبة معنى البيع مجازاً.
ومثال العلاقة العقلية قولنا : حكمت المحكمة على فلان فإنَّ المراد أنه قد حكم الحاكم، إذ المحكمة المكان. ويعتبر هذا الإسناد سائغاً مقبولاً بعلاقة أو رابطة يلحظها العقل بين الحال والمحل يمكن أن يعتبر معها الفعل كأنه صادر عن المحل بقطع النظر عن الفاعل الحال فيه، كما تقول : حالف الأمير البلد الفلاني أو حاربه والمراد أهل البلد وسكانه . فهذه الأساليب الفنية في طرائق التعبير هي من صور المجاز في البيان لأنها تقوم على أساس وجود القرينة أو العلاقة، وليست في شيء من العرف اللفظي الذي يعتبر كلغة وضعية خاصة تصبح معانيها حقائق عرفية تستفاد من مجرد اللفظ .
٣/٦٨ - ب - العرف العملي : وأما العرف العملي فهو اعتياد الناس على شيء من الأفعال العادية أو المعاملات المدنية .
والمراد بالأفعال العادية : أفعال الناس الشخصية في شؤونهم الحيوية مما لا يقوم على تبادل المصالح وإنشاء الحقوق، وذلك كالأكل والشرب واللبس والركب والحرث والزرع ونحو ذلك .
والمراد بالمعاملات المدنية التصرفات التي يقصد منها إنشاء الحقوق بين الناس، أو تصفيتها وإسقاطها، سواء أكانت تلك التصرفات عقوداً أم غيرها كالنكاح والبيع والإبراء، وكالغصب والقبض والأداء (ر: ف ١/٤٨ و ١٣/٥٩ الحاشية).
فمن أمثلة العرف في الأفعال اعتياد الناس تعطيل بعض أيام الأسبوع عن العمل. وكذا اعتيادهم في بعض الأماكن أكل نوع خاص من اللحوم كالضأن أو المعز أو البقر، أو استعمال نوع من الملابس والأدوات، ونحو ذلك
-
أمثلته ومن في المعاملات :
اعتياد الناس في بيع بعض الأشياء الثقيلة مثل الحطب والفحم والقمح أن تكون على البائع حمولتها إلى بيت المشتري.
- واعتيادهم تقسيط الأجور السنوية للعقارات إلى أقساط معدودة.
- وتعارفهم في الأنكحة تعجيل جانب معين من مهور النساء كالنصف أو الثلثين، وتأجيل الباقي إلى ما بعد الوفاة أو الطلاق.
-
-
-
وتعاملهم فيما بينهم ببعض أنواع النقود دون سواها.
وتعارفهم في بعض المهن والصنائع أن يأخذ الصانع من رب العمل أجراً، وفي بعضها الآخر أن يأخذ رب العمل من الصانع أجراً، كما في الخياطة عندما يكون المقصود تعليم الصانع تلك الصناعة لا مجرد استخدامه إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى من أعراف الناس العملية الممكن جريانها في أفانين معاملاتهم وتفاريع التزاماتهم فيها.
ثانياً : العرف العام، والعرف الخاص
٤/٦٨ ـ أ ـ العرف العام :
فالعرف العام هو الذي يكون فاشياً في جميع البلاد بين جميع الناس
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. ...and its type and value, even paper currency today with silver coin minted at a specific weight and specific value. That the dirhams originally... And likewise the use of the word 'al-bayt' in some countries to mean 'the room' and in others to mean 'the house' in its entirety. This type of custom is in reality of the nature of a special language for its people. If understanding the intended meaning requires a contextual indicator (qarina) or an intellectual relationship, then it is not custom but is rather of the nature of metaphor (majaz). Example of a contextual indicator: If a person carries a small stick and swears to kill so-and-so with it, one understands by this contextual indicator that he means by 'killing' painful beating. Likewise, if he says to another: 'I have gifted you this thing for ten dinars,' one understands by the contextual indicator of the compensation that he means by 'gift' the meaning of sale metaphorically. Example of an intellectual relationship: Our saying 'the court ruled against so-and-so' means that the judge ruled, since the court is the place. This attribution is considered permissible and acceptable due to a relationship or connection that the mind perceives between the state and the place, by which the act can be considered as if it emanated from the place regardless of the actual agent acting in it, just as you say: 'The governor made an alliance with such-and-such country' or 'fought it,' meaning the people and inhabitants of the country. These artistic methods in modes of expression are forms of metaphor in eloquence because they are based on the existence of the indicator or relationship, and they have nothing to do with linguistic custom, which is considered a special conventional language whose meanings become customary realities understood from the mere wording. 3/68 - B - Practical custom: As for practical custom, it is people's habituation to something from ordinary actions or civil transactions. By ordinary actions is meant people's personal actions in their vital affairs that are not based on exchange of interests and creation of rights, such as eating, drinking, dressing, riding, plowing, planting, and the like. By civil transactions is meant dispositions intended to create rights between people, or to liquidate and drop them, whether those dispositions are contracts or otherwise, such as marriage, sale, discharge, and also usurpation, possession, and payment (see: F 1/48 and 13/59 footnote). Examples of custom in actions: people's habit of suspending work on some days of the week, and likewise their custom in some places of eating a specific type of meat such as sheep, goat, or cow, or using a type of clothing and tools, and so on. Its examples from transactions: People's custom in selling heavy items such as firewood, coal, and wheat that their transportation to the buyer's house is upon the seller. - Their custom of splitting annual rents for properties into fixed installments. - Their custom in marriages of hastening a certain portion of women's dowers, such as half or two-thirds, and deferring the remainder until after death or divorce. - Their dealing among themselves with some types of currency to the exclusion of others. - Their custom in some professions and crafts that the craftsman takes wages from the employer, and in others that the employer takes wages from the craftsman, as in tailoring when the purpose is to teach the craftsman that trade, not merely employing him, and other countless practical customs of people that can flow in the forms of their transactions and the branches of their obligations therein. Secondly: General custom and specific custom. 4/68 - A - General custom: General custom is that which is widespread in all countries among all people.
Source pages p. 820

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies custom ('urf), a key source of Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by noting that even paper currency today is treated like silver coin of specified weight and value by custom. He then distinguishes linguistic custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) from practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). Linguistic custom is a special conventional language where words take on specific meanings in a community, such as 'bayt' meaning room in some areas and house in others. He stresses that if a meaning requires a contextual indicator or intellectual relation, it is metaphor (majaz), not custom. Practical custom is people's habituation in actions or civil transactions. He gives examples: suspending work on certain days, eating specific meats, selling heavy items with delivery by seller, splitting rents, customizing dower payments, using certain currencies, and payment customs in crafts like tailoring. Finally, he introduces the second classification: general custom (widespread in all countries) versus specific custom (in a particular region or group). This classification is foundational for a mufti: a general custom can override a qiyas, while a specific custom may be restricted.

Key Terms
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator in the speech or situation that clarifies the intended meaning, distinguishing metaphor from literal or customary meaning.
العلاقة العقلية (al-'alaqa al-'aqliyya): An intellectual relationship between the word and its intended meaning, such as attributing an action to a place instead of the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example where an intellectual relationship makes the expression metaphor and not custom?
A) 'I gifted you this for ten dinars'
B) 'The court ruled against the defendant'
C) 'I swear to kill you while holding a stick'
D) 'The word 'bayt' meaning 'room' in some countries'

Key Takeaway
Linguistic custom requires self-evidence from the wording; any meaning needing a contextual clue or mental link is metaphor, not custom.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
5 min
Custom: General and Specific
Introducing general custom and implying specific custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a custom as general or specific and explain why general custom carries more authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
General custom prevails over specific custom and can even override analogy.

Beyond the division by subject, custom is also classified by scope: general customs are widespread across all countries and people, while specific customs are limited to a region, profession, or sector. This lesson introduces the classification and its legal weight.

Guided Path
1
Define general custom
General custom is that which is widespread in all countries among all people. Example: the use of silver coins for monetary transactions (historically).
Widespread geographical and demographic distribution.
2
Define specific custom
Specific custom is limited to a particular country, region, profession, or group. Example: a profession's custom of taking payment in kind rather than cash.
Opposite of general; context-bound.
3
Compare authority
In Hanafi methodology, general custom is given more weight. It can even abrogate a clear analogy (qiyas jali), while specific custom is more restricted and cannot override general custom or a strong text.
General custom is closer to a universal norm.

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies custom ('urf), a key source of Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by noting that even paper currency today is treated like silver coin of specified weight and value by custom. He then distinguishes linguistic custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) from practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). Key Terms
العرف العام (al-'urf al-'amm): General custom: custom widespread in all countries among all people.
العرف الخاص (al-'urf al-khass): Specific custom: custom limited to a particular country, region, profession, or group.

Division of Custom: Actions and Transactions
Classifies custom and provides examples from actions and transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'urf in actions and 'urf in transactions, and identify examples of each from the source.

Core Idea
Custom (al-'urf) is a subsidiary source of law that can govern both daily actions and commercial contracts.

This passage from al-Zarqa's introduction to jurisprudence classifies custom into two categories: custom in actions (e.g., work habits, dress) and custom in transactions (e.g., trade practices, dowry splitting). As mufti trainees, understanding this division is crucial for applying 'urf correctly in fatwa — you need to know whether a custom relates to an action or a transaction, because different rules of evidence and reliance apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Scan the passage for the explicit division of 'urf. The author separates custom into two broad types.
Look for the phrases 'العرف في الأفعال' and 'العرف في المعاملات'.
2
Differentiate the legal nature
Consider how each type functions in fiqh. Custom in actions often relates to daily habits; custom in transactions directly shapes contractual rights and obligations.
Transaction customs are more likely to be legally binding if widespread.
3
Extract examples per category
List the concrete examples given for actions (e.g., weekend breaks, dietary habits) and for transactions (e.g., transport costs, installment payments, dowry splitting).
Notice that transaction examples involve legal effects like payment terms.
4
Reflect on conditions for reliance
From your knowledge of Hanafi usul, think about when a custom can be used as evidence. The passage implies customs must be consistent and not contradict a primary text.
Refer to the maxim 'al-‘ādatu muḥakkamah' but note its limitations.
5
Consider practical implications
Why does al-Zarqa emphasize that these customs are 'practical' and 'countless'? This shows that 'urf is a flexible tool for muftis to address evolving situations.
Think of modern applications like cryptocurrency usage or remote work customs.

الفصل (٦٨) تقسيم العرف
واللبس والركب والحرث والزرع ونحو ذلك .
والمراد بالمعاملات المدنية التصرفات التي يقصد منها إنشاء الحقوق بين الناس، أو تصفيتها وإسقاطها، سواء أكانت تلك التصرفات عقوداً أم ،غيرها كالنكاح والبيع والإبراء، وكالغصب والقبض والأداء (ر: ف ١/٤٨ و ١٣/٥٩ الحاشية).
فمن أمثلة العرف في الأفعال اعتياد الناس تعطيل بعض أيام الأسبوع عن العمل. وكذا اعتيادهم في بعض الأماكن أكل نوع خاص من اللحوم كالضأن أو المعز أو البقر، أو استعمال نوع من الملابس والأدوات، ونحو ذلك

أمثلته ومن في المعاملات :
اعتياد الناس في بيع بعض الأشياء الثقيلة مثل الحطب والفحم
والقمح أن تكون على البائع حمولتها إلى بيت المشتري.
- واعتيادهم تقسيط الأجور السنوية للعقارات إلى أقساط معدودة. وتعارفهم في الأنكحة تعجيل جانب معين من مهور النساء كالنصف أو الثلثين، وتأجيل الباقي إلى ما بعد الوفاة أو الطلاق.
وتعاملهم فيما بينهم ببعض أنواع النقود دون سواها. وتعارفهم في بعض المهن والصنائع أن يأخذ الصانع من رب العمل أجراً، وفي بعضها الآخر أن يأخذ رب العمل من الصانع أجراً، كما في الخياطة عندما يكون المقصود تعليم الصانع تلك الصناعة لا مجرد استخدامه إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى من أعراف الناس العملية الممكن جريانها في أفانين معاملاتهم وتفاريع التزاماتهم فيها.
Chapter (68) Division of Custom: And clothing, riding, plowing, cultivation, and the like. And by civil transactions is meant dispositions intended to establish rights among people, or to settle and drop them, whether those dispositions are contracts or otherwise, such as marriage, sale, release, and usurpation, possession, and payment. From examples of custom in actions: the habit of people suspending work on certain days of the week. Also their habit in some places of eating a specific type of meat like sheep, goats, or cattle, or using a type of clothing and tools, etc. Its examples in transactions: the habit of people in selling some heavy items like firewood, charcoal, and wheat that their transportation to the buyer's house is upon the seller. And their habit of splitting annual rents for real estate into a fixed number of installments. And their mutual custom in marriages of advancing a certain portion of women's dowries like half or two-thirds, and deferring the rest to after death or divorce. And their dealing among themselves with certain types of currency to the exclusion of others. And their custom in some professions and crafts that the artisan takes wages from the employer, and in others that the employer takes wages from the artisan, as in tailoring when the intention is to teach the artisan that craft not merely to employ him, and so on from countless practical customs of people that can be applied in various aspects of their dealings and details of their obligations.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am discusses the division of custom ('urf) into two categories: custom in actions and custom in transactions. The author first defines civil transactions as legal acts intended to create, settle, or drop rights, whether they are contracts (like sale, marriage, release) or other acts (like usurpation, possession, payment). He then provides illustrative examples. For custom in actions, he mentions the habit of taking weekends off work, and regional dietary and clothing customs. For custom in transactions, he lists market practices: the seller bearing transport costs for bulky goods, installment payment of rent, splitting dowry into prompt and deferred portions, using specific currencies in trade, and varying payment norms in apprenticeships. The passage emphasizes that these customs are practical and numerous, and they can be applied to regulate people's dealings and obligations. The author does not here derive rulings but lays the groundwork for the legal theory of 'urf as a subsidiary source of fiqh.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom: a recurring practice among people that becomes a norm in their actions or transactions, recognized as a subsidiary source of law.
المعاملات المدنية (al-mu'amalat al-madaniyya): Civil transactions: legal acts intended to create, settle, or drop rights, including contracts and other dispositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of 'urf in transactions?
A) Wearing a specific type of clothing
B) Paying rent in installments
C) Eating lamb on holidays
D) Taking weekends off

Key Takeaway
Custom (al-'urf) is divided into custom in actions and custom in transactions; both can serve as subsidiary sources of law in the Hanafi school when they do not contradict primary texts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

General and Specific Custom (Urf)
Defining general and specific custom with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general custom (al-urf al-amm) and specific custom (al-urf al-khas), and apply the Hanafi rule that general custom can override qiyas in mu'amalat.

Core Idea
General custom is a binding source in Hanafi fiqh; specific custom is valid only within its scope.

Custom ('urf) plays a vital role in Hanafi jurisprudence, especially in transactions (mu'amalat). This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm introduces the crucial classification into general and specific custom. Understanding this distinction is key for a mufti when deriving rulings for contemporary issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of custom
Read the passage and note how the author defines general custom (al-urf al-amm) and specific custom (al-urf al-khas). General custom is widespread across the Muslim world; specific custom is limited to a region or group.
2
Examine the examples of general custom
The author gives two examples: the manufacturing contract (istisna') and the division of dower (mahr) into prompt and deferred. Both are universally practiced in Islamic lands.
Istisna' is a contract not explicitly detailed in the primary texts but validated by general custom.
3
Examine the examples of specific custom
Specific custom examples include merchants' customs regarding defects, payment terms for wholesale goods, and lawyers' contingency fees. These are restricted to certain professions or regions.
4
Determine the legal weight of each category
In Hanafi usul, general custom carries more weight and can override analogical reasoning (qiyas). Specific custom is authoritative only within its domain and cannot contradict general custom or nass.
This hierarchy is important for fatwa.
5
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Consider a modern example: the custom of using electronic payment apps. Is it general or specific? How would a Hanafi mufti use it?
This step prepares you for the practice tasks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
في أمر من الأمور وذلك كالاستصناع في كثير من الحاجات واللوازم من أحذية وألبسة وأدوات وغيرها. فإن الناس قد احتاجوا إليه ودرجوا عليه من قديم الزمان ولا يخلو اليوم من التعامل به مكان، وقد أصبح جارياً في جميع الحاجات حتى استصناع المعامل بكاملها والبواخر والأبنية ..
ومثله في العموم اليوم تأجيل جانب من مهور النساء في البلاد الإسلامية. فقد تعورف تقسيم المهر إلى معجل ومؤجل، بنسبة تختلف مقداراً بين بلد وآخر. وقد تكون هذه النسبة ثابتة بحيث أنه متى ذكر بين الطرفين مبلغ المعجل عرف المؤجل، كما في بلادنا الشامية اليوم، فإنَّ العرف لدينا مطرد على أن المؤجل من المهر نصف المعجل مالم يصرّح في العقد بخلافه .
٥/٦٨ - ب - العرف الخاص :
أما العرف الخاص فهو الذي يكون مخصوصاً ببلد أو مكان دون آخر، أو بين فئة من الناس دون أخرى.
وهذا العرف الخاص متنوع كثير متجدد لا تحصى صوره ولا تقف عند حد، لأن مصالح الناس وسيلهم إليها وإلى تسهيل احتياجهم وعلائقهم
متجددة أبداً .
وذلك كعرف التجار فيما يعد عيباً ينقص الثمن في البضاعة المبيعة أو لا يعد عيباً، وكعرفهم في بعض البلاد أن يكون ثمن بعض البضائع المبيعة بالجملة مقسطاً إلى عدد معلوم من الأقساط وعرفهم أن لا تقبل المبالغ
الكبيرة من أجزاء النقود الصغيرة في الصفقة الواحدة إلا بنسبة معينة. ومثل ذلك عرف المحامين اليوم على أن جانباً معلوماً من أجور الدعاوى التي يتقبلونها كالنصف مثلاً يكون مؤجلاً ومعلقاً على ربح الدعوى، وصيرورة الحكم بها مبرماً، واستخراج وثيقة الحكم ووضعها في دائرة التنفيذ .
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. In a matter of affairs, such as istisna' (manufacturing contract) in many needs and necessities like shoes, clothes, tools, etc. Indeed, people have needed it and followed it from ancient times, and today it is not devoid of dealing with it in any place, and it has become current in all needs, even the full manufacturing of factories, ships, and buildings. Similarly, in general today, deferment of part of women's dowers in Islamic lands. The division of dower into prompt and deferred has become customary, with a proportion that varies in amount between one country and another. This proportion may be fixed such that when the prompt portion is mentioned between the parties, the deferred is known, as in our Levantine lands today, for the custom with us is consistent that the deferred portion of the dower is half of the prompt unless otherwise specified in the contract. 5/68 - b - Specific Custom: As for specific custom, it is that which is specific to a country or place to the exclusion of others, or among a group of people to the exclusion of others. This specific custom is diverse, plentiful, and ever-renewing; its forms cannot be enumerated nor limited, because the interests of people and their means to achieve them and to facilitate their needs and relationships are constantly renewed. Such as the custom of merchants regarding what is considered a defect that reduces the price of sold goods or not a defect, and their custom in some countries that the price of some goods sold wholesale is paid in installments over a known number of installments, and their custom that large amounts of small currency denominations are not accepted in a single transaction except in a certain proportion. Similarly, the custom of lawyers today that a known portion of legal fees they accept, e.g., half, is deferred and contingent on winning the case, the verdict becoming final, extracting the judgment document, and placing it in the enforcement department.
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm introduces the classification of custom (urf) into general (amm) and specific (khas). The author first illustrates general custom with the example of istisna' (manufacturing contract), which, though not explicitly detailed in the primary texts, has been universally practiced and recognized. Another example is the customary division of dower (mahr) into prompt (mu'ajjal) and deferred (mu'ajjal) portions, where in the Levant the deferred is typically half of the prompt unless contractually modified. General custom applies across regions and communities. Specific custom is restricted to a particular locale or group, such as commercial usages regarding defects, payment terms, or lawyers' fee structures. These customs are constantly evolving to meet people's needs. The classification is important in Hanafi ifta' because specific custom is considered in fatwa within its sphere, but general custom carries greater weight and can even override analogical reasoning (qiyas) in mu'amalat. The author emphasizes that custom is not static but renews with changing circumstances.

Key Terms
العرف العام (al-urf al-amm): Custom that is widespread across the Muslim world, not restricted to a locality or group.
العرف الخاص (al-urf al-khas): Custom limited to a specific region, profession, or group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of general custom?
A) The custom of lawyers deferring fees
B) The division of dower into prompt and deferred
C) The custom of merchants regarding coin denominations
D) The custom of a particular country on defects

Key Takeaway
General custom is a binding source in Hanafi ifta for mu'amalat, while specific custom is valid only within its restricted scope.

Custom in Contracts: Waqf, Sale, and Disclaimer
Practical rulings showing custom as a source of contract interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' to identify when customary usage determines the legal effect of contractual expressions, using the examples of waqf, fruit sale, and disclaimer of defects.

Core Idea
Custom overrides original linguistic meaning — a word's legal effect follows the custom of the people.

This lesson examines three practical scenarios from Hanafi fiqh where custom directly shapes the validity and interpretation of contracts. Whether it's the weight of currency in a waqf, the phrase 'daman' in a fruit sale, or a colloquial disclaimer like 'hadir halal', the Hanafi school consistently applies the maxim 'custom is authoritative' to give effect to people's actual practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the contested term
For each example, name the type of contract (waqf, sale) and the specific word or phrase whose meaning is in question (e.g., 'dinar/dirham', 'tadmeen wa daman', 'hadir halal').
Understanding what is being sold or stipulated is the first step.
2
Distinguish linguistic/technical meaning vs. customary meaning
Determine what the word originally means in Arabic or in classical fiqh terminology, then what people in the locality actually mean when they use it today.
This contrast highlights the role of custom.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule: 'al-ādah muḥakkamah'
Explain how the maxim 'custom is authoritative' makes the customary meaning legally binding, overriding the original or technical meaning.
Custom is a subsidiary source of law (masdar far'i) in the Hanafi school.
4
Derive the practical consequence
State the resulting ruling: e.g., the waqf follows the original weight, the fruit sale is valid, the buyer cannot return for defects.
These consequences show custom in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف .
ومثل حكم الوقف في هذا حكم الوصية (ر) رسائل ابن عابدین ۲
وكذلك لو شرط الواقف إعطاء دنانير أو دراهم معدودة شهرياً أو سنوياً، وكان الدينار أو الدرهم في عرف زمانه بوزن معين، ثم تبدل وزنه، فالعبرة للقدر الذي كان في زمانه .
٣ - ينعقد بيع الثمار على أشجارها بلفظ «التضمين، والضمان» بلادنا الشامية، لأنه اللفظ العرفي فيها اليوم بين أهل البساتين والكروم . فيقول البائع : «ضمنتك» ويقول المشتري «ضمنت» مع أن التضمين والضمان في لسان الشرع معناهما الإلزام والالتزام بقيمة المتلفات ونحوها . وكذلك ينعقد بيع أحد الشريكين في الدابة المشتركة حصته الشريكه الآخر بلفظ «المقاصرة» لتعارفهم هذا اللفظ في هذا البيع. فيقول البائع : قاصرتك على حصتي بكذا ويقبل الشريك المشتري (ر: رد المحتار أول البيوع ٩/٤).
٤ - يصح للبائع أن يشترط في عقد البيع البراءة العامة من ضمان العيوب، وعندئذ لا يحق للمشتري الرجوع عليه بشيء مهما ظهر في المبيع من عيوب .
وعليه لو باع الإنسان اليوم شيئاً وعبر عن هذا التبرؤ من ضمان العيوب ببعض التعابير العرفية، كما لو باع شيئاً على أنه «حاضر حلال» أو «كوم تراب» أو «حراق على الزناد» أو نحو ذلك من التعابير التي اعتادها اليوم العوام في هذا المقام فليس للمشتري رد المبيع بعيب ما ظاهر أو خفي، لأن الناس تعارفوا بهذه التعابير اشتراط براءة البائع من ضمان العيوب كافة. (ر : المجلة / ٣٤٣ / وشرحها للأتاسي، وانظر أيضاً رد المحتار 90/8)
٥ - إذا تعارف الناس إيقاع الطلاق بألفاظ أو تعابير جديدة فشا استعمالها بينهم فإنها يقع بها الطلاق ولو كانت في أصل اللغة لا تقتضي الوقوع، كلفظ «علي الطلاق» الذي يستعمله الرجال في هذا الزمان عند إرادة التطليق مع أن الطلاق وصف يقع على المرأة التي هي محله شرعاً لا على الرجل (١).
(١) حتى لو قال الرجل لزوجته: أنا منك طالق لا يقع به طلاق وإن نواه، لأن الرجل لا يكون طالقاً بل مطلقاً. وإنما صح تطليق الزوجة بقوله : «علي الطلاق» لأن هذا اللفظ قد أصبح في العرف والاستعمال نظير قوله لامرأته: أنت طالق أو قوله: طلقت امرأتي (ر ) رد المحتار ٢ / ٤٣٢ و ٤٤٦ وبهجة المشتاق لأحكام الطلاق ص ١٦ - ١٧).
The Ninth Chapter: The Theory of Custom. And the ruling of waqf in this regard is like the ruling of will (see Ibn Abidin's Letters 2). Similarly, if the waqf stipulates giving counted dinars or dirhams monthly or yearly, and the dinar or dirham in the custom of his time had a specific weight, then its weight changed, then consideration is given to the amount that was in his time. 3 - The sale of fruits on trees is concluded with the words 'al-tadmeen' and 'al-daman' in our Syrian lands, because it is the customary term today among orchard and vineyard owners. The seller says 'dammantuka' and the buyer says 'damintu', even though tadmeen and daman in the language of the law mean obligation and engagement to pay the value of destroyed property and the like. Similarly, the sale of one partner's share to the other partner in a jointly owned animal is concluded with the term 'al-muqasara' due to their custom of this term in this sale. The seller says: I muqasartuka on my share for such and such, and the buyer partner accepts (see: Radd al-Muhtar, first of Buyu', 9/4). 4 - It is valid for the seller to stipulate in the contract of sale a general disclaimer from liability for defects, and then the buyer has no right to recourse for anything, no matter what defects appear in the sold item. Accordingly, if someone sells something today and expresses this disclaimer from liability for defects with some customary expressions, such as selling something as 'hadir halal' or 'kum turab' or 'harraq 'ala al-zinad' or similar expressions that the common people today have become accustomed to in this context, then the buyer cannot return the sold item for any apparent or hidden defect, because people have customarily understood these expressions as stipulating the seller's disclaimer from all liability for defects (see: Majallah 343 and its commentary by al-Atasi, and also see Radd al-Muhtar 8/90). 5 - If people become accustomed to effecting divorce with new words or expressions that become widespread among them, then divorce occurs by them even if in the original language they do not imply occurrence, like the term 'alayya al-talaq' which men use in this time when intending divorce, even though talaq is a description that falls on the woman who is its legal locus, not on the man (1). (1) Even if a man says to his wife: I am divorced from you, divorce does not occur even if he intends it, because the man is not a divorcée but a divorcee. Rather, divorcing the wife is valid by his saying 'alayya al-talaq' because this term has become in custom and usage analogous to his saying to his wife 'you are divorced' or his saying 'I have divorced my wife' (see: Radd al-Muhtar 2/432 and 446, and Bahjat al-Mushtaq li Ahkam al-Talaq pp. 16-17).
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings from Hanafi fiqh that illustrate the operation of custom (urf) in determining the legal effects of transactions and family law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating the general rule that custom is considered, using the example of a waqf stipulation: if the currency weight changes after the waqf, the original weight is followed because that was the custom at the time of the waqf. The same applies to wills. The first numbered example (3) deals with sales in Syria: the terms 'tadmeen' and 'daman' are used for the sale of fruit on trees, and 'muqasara' for one partner selling his share to another. Though these words have different technical meanings in Shariah, they are valid because custom has redefined them for these transactions. The second example (4) concerns disclaimers from liability for defects: if a seller says 'hadir halal' (ready and lawful) or similar expressions that in custom mean a general disclaimer, the buyer cannot return the item for defects. This is based on the Majallah and later Hanafi works. The third example (5) addresses divorce: the phrase 'alayya al-talaq' (upon me is divorce) now constitutes a valid divorce in custom, even though grammatically it would not. The footnote clarifies that saying 'I am divorced from you' does not work. All these examples show that when people customarily use words in a particular sense, that meaning becomes legally binding, even if it contradicts original linguistic or technical usage.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): The established and recurrent practice of people in their transactions, speech, or actions that gains legal consideration when not contradicted by a definitive text.
التضمين والضمان (al-tadmeen wa al-daman): In this context, customary terms used in Syria for the sale of fruits on trees, meaning 'I guarantee you the produce'.
علي الطلاق (alayya al-talaq): A customary phrase used by men to divorce their wives, meaning 'upon me is divorce'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer can still return the item for hidden defects.
B) The buyer cannot return the item for any defect because the expression is customarily understood as a disclaimer.
C) The seller is liable for all defects regardless of the expression.
D) The sale is void because the expression is unclear.

Key Takeaway
Custom can redefine the legal meaning of contractual terms, and the Hanafi school gives it binding force as a source of law.

Custom in Divorce: The Phrase 'علي الطلاق'
A case study in custom's power to create new legal effects in family law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the customary phrase 'alayya al-talaq' as a valid pronouncement of divorce, and distinguish it from other expressions that do not effect divorce.

Core Idea
If people habitually use a phrase to divorce, that phrase becomes legally effective even if it is not the classical wording.

This lesson examines a single but powerful example: the Syrian custom of using 'alayya al-talaq' to divorce. Though the phrase literally means 'upon me is divorce', which grammatically does not fall on the wife, the Hanafi school considers it valid because of widespread custom. We will see how custom can even override grammatical and technical constraints.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression and its literal meaning
The phrase is 'alayya al-talaq' (علي الطلاق), which literally means 'upon me is divorce'. Grammatically, talaq is a quality that attaches to the woman, not the man.
Words alone, without custom, would not effect divorce.
2
Contrast with the classical standard
In classical Hanafi fiqh, the proper divorce pronouncement is 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) or 'tallaqtuki' (I have divorced you). The phrase 'alayya al-talaq' does not address the wife directly.
The footnote also mentions that saying 'ana minik taliq' (I am divorced from you) does not work.
3
Apply the custom rule
Because people customarily use 'alayya al-talaq' to mean divorce, it is treated as equivalent to the standard phrases. The maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' makes it effective.
Custom fills the gap where literal wording falls short.
4
Derive the practical consequence
When a man says 'alayya al-talaq' with the intent to divorce, one divorce occurs. The wife is divorced, and the usual rules (iddah, etc.) apply.
This ruling is the operative fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف .
ومثل حكم الوقف في هذا حكم الوصية (ر) رسائل ابن عابدین ۲
وكذلك لو شرط الواقف إعطاء دنانير أو دراهم معدودة شهرياً أو سنوياً، وكان الدينار أو الدرهم في عرف زمانه بوزن معين، ثم تبدل وزنه، فالعبرة للقدر الذي كان في زمانه .
٣ - ينعقد بيع الثمار على أشجارها بلفظ «التضمين، والضمان» بلادنا الشامية، لأنه اللفظ العرفي فيها اليوم بين أهل البساتين والكروم . فيقول البائع : «ضمنتك» ويقول المشتري «ضمنت» مع أن التضمين والضمان في لسان الشرع معناهما الإلزام والالتزام بقيمة المتلفات ونحوها . وكذلك ينعقد بيع أحد الشريكين في الدابة المشتركة حصته الشريكه الآخر بلفظ «المقاصرة» لتعارفهم هذا اللفظ في هذا البيع. فيقول البائع : قاصرتك على حصتي بكذا ويقبل الشريك المشتري (ر: رد المحتار أول البيوع ٩/٤).
٤ - يصح للبائع أن يشترط في عقد البيع البراءة العامة من ضمان العيوب، وعندئذ لا يحق للمشتري الرجوع عليه بشيء مهما ظهر في المبيع من عيوب .
وعليه لو باع الإنسان اليوم شيئاً وعبر عن هذا التبرؤ من ضمان العيوب ببعض التعابير العرفية، كما لو باع شيئاً على أنه «حاضر حلال» أو «كوم تراب» أو «حراق على الزناد» أو نحو ذلك من التعابير التي اعتادها اليوم العوام في هذا المقام فليس للمشتري رد المبيع بعيب ما ظاهر أو خفي، لأن الناس تعارفوا بهذه التعابير اشتراط براءة البائع من ضمان العيوب كافة. (ر : المجلة / ٣٤٣ / وشرحها للأتاسي، وانظر أيضاً رد المحتار 90/8)
٥ - إذا تعارف الناس إيقاع الطلاق بألفاظ أو تعابير جديدة فشا استعمالها بينهم فإنها يقع بها الطلاق ولو كانت في أصل اللغة لا تقتضي الوقوع، كلفظ «علي الطلاق» الذي يستعمله الرجال في هذا الزمان عند إرادة التطليق مع أن الطلاق وصف يقع على المرأة التي هي محله شرعاً لا على الرجل (١).
(١) حتى لو قال الرجل لزوجته: أنا منك طالق لا يقع به طلاق وإن نواه، لأن الرجل لا يكون طالقاً بل مطلقاً. وإنما صح تطليق الزوجة بقوله : «علي الطلاق» لأن هذا اللفظ قد أصبح في العرف والاستعمال نظير قوله لامرأته: أنت طالق أو قوله: طلقت امرأتي (ر ) رد المحتار ٢ / ٤٣٢ و ٤٤٦ وبهجة المشتاق لأحكام الطلاق ص ١٦ - ١٧).
The Ninth Chapter: The Theory of Custom. And the ruling of waqf in this regard is like the ruling of will (see Ibn Abidin's Letters 2). Similarly, if the waqf stipulates giving counted dinars or dirhams monthly or yearly, and the dinar or dirham in the custom of his time had a specific weight, then its weight changed, then consideration is given to the amount that was in his time. 3 - The sale of fruits on trees is concluded with the words 'al-tadmeen' and 'al-daman' in our Syrian lands, because it is the customary term today among orchard and vineyard owners. The seller says 'dammantuka' and the buyer says 'damintu', even though tadmeen and daman in the language of the law mean obligation and engagement to pay the value of destroyed property and the like. Similarly, the sale of one partner's share to the other partner in a jointly owned animal is concluded with the term 'al-muqasara' due to their custom of this term in this sale. The seller says: I muqasartuka on my share for such and such, and the buyer partner accepts (see: Radd al-Muhtar, first of Buyu', 9/4). 4 - It is valid for the seller to stipulate in the contract of sale a general disclaimer from liability for defects, and then the buyer has no right to recourse for anything, no matter what defects appear in the sold item. Accordingly, if someone sells something today and expresses this disclaimer from liability for defects with some customary expressions, such as selling something as 'hadir halal' or 'kum turab' or 'harraq 'ala al-zinad' or similar expressions that the common people today have become accustomed to in this context, then the buyer cannot return the sold item for any apparent or hidden defect, because people have customarily understood these expressions as stipulating the seller's disclaimer from all liability for defects (see: Majallah 343 and its commentary by al-Atasi, and also see Radd al-Muhtar 8/90). 5 - If people become accustomed to effecting divorce with new words or expressions that become widespread among them, then divorce occurs by them even if in the original language they do not imply occurrence, like the term 'alayya al-talaq' which men use in this time when intending divorce, even though talaq is a description that falls on the woman who is its legal locus, not on the man (1). (1) Even if a man says to his wife: I am divorced from you, divorce does not occur even if he intends it, because the man is not a divorcée but a divorcee. Rather, divorcing the wife is valid by his saying 'alayya al-talaq' because this term has become in custom and usage analogous to his saying to his wife 'you are divorced' or his saying 'I have divorced my wife' (see: Radd al-Muhtar 2/432 and 446, and Bahjat al-Mushtaq li Ahkam al-Talaq pp. 16-17).
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings from Hanafi fiqh that illustrate the operation of custom (urf) in determining the legal effects of transactions and family law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by stating the general rule that custom is considered, using the example of a waqf stipulation: if the currency weight changes after the waqf, the original weight is followed because that was the custom at the time of the waqf. The same applies to wills. The first numbered example (3) deals with sales in Syria: the terms 'tadmeen' and 'daman' are used for the sale of fruit on trees, and 'muqasara' for one partner selling his share to another. Though these words have different technical meanings in Shariah, they are valid because custom has redefined them for these transactions. The second example (4) concerns disclaimers from liability for defects: if a seller says 'hadir halal' (ready and lawful) or similar expressions that in custom mean a general disclaimer, the buyer cannot return the item for defects. This is based on the Majallah and later Hanafi works. The third example (5) addresses divorce: the phrase 'alayya al-talaq' (upon me is divorce) now constitutes a valid divorce in custom, even though grammatically it would not. The footnote clarifies that saying 'I am divorced from you' does not work. All these examples show that when people customarily use words in a particular sense, that meaning becomes legally binding, even if it contradicts original linguistic or technical usage.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): The established and recurrent practice of people in their transactions, speech, or actions that gains legal consideration when not contradicted by a definitive text.
علي الطلاق (alayya al-talaq): A customary phrase used by men to divorce their wives, meaning 'upon me is divorce'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Divorce occurs because the intention is clear.
B) Divorce does not occur because the phrase implies the man is the object of divorce, which is linguistically incorrect.
C) Divorce occurs only if the wife agrees.
D) The matter is referred to a judge.

Key Takeaway
A customary divorce phrase becomes legally binding even if it deviates from classical wording, as long as it is widely understood and intended.

The Authority of Verbal Custom in Hanafi Law
Explains that verbal custom creates a new language that governs legal interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that verbal custom creates a new language for interpreting contracts and declarations, and explain its implications for fatwa.

Core Idea
What people commonly mean by their words becomes the law for their transactions.

This passage from al-Zarqa synthesizes the earlier examples on verbal custom. It establishes a fundamental rule in Hanafi legal interpretation: when people consistently use words in a certain way, that usage becomes the authoritative meaning for legal purposes. This is especially important for muftis, who must interpret contracts, divorces, and other verbal acts based on local custom rather than classical Arabic grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage states that verbal custom creates a new language that is authoritative for interpreting people's speech. Recognize that this is not merely an observation but a legal principle that guides interpretation.
Al-Zarqa is concluding from earlier examples, so the claim is both descriptive and prescriptive.
2
Understand the scope
Note that the principle applies to 'كل تصرفاتهم القولية' (all verbal dispositions), including contracts, suspensions, permissions, ratifications, etc. This means any legal act expressed through words is subject to customary interpretation.
This includes both oral and written statements made in everyday transactions.
3
Recognize flexibility by locale
The passage explicitly acknowledges that colloquial language varies from one country to another. Therefore, the mufti must ascertain the specific custom of the people in the region where the transaction occurred.
What is common in one city may not be common in another; each place has its own 'urf khass'.
4
Note the consequence for grammar
The passage says that even if the common people's expressions contradict the grammatical rules of classical Arabic (مذاهب النحويين), they are still legally valid. This shows that formal grammar is not the ultimate authority; custom is.
Ibn Abidin's letters are cited to reinforce this Hanafi position.
5
Apply as a mufti
When issuing a fatwa, always investigate how the community uses key terms in contracts, divorce, gifts, etc. Do not assume classical Arabic definitions. If there is doubt, the mufti should ask about local usage.
This is part of 'rasm al-mufti' methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥/٦٩ - يتضح من الأمثلة السابقة البيان أن العرف اللفظي بوجه عام تنشأ لغة جديدة تكون هي المعتبرة في تنزيل كلام الناس عليها، وتحديد ما يترتب على تصرفاتهم القولية من حقوق وواجبات بحسب المعاني العرفية .
واللغة العامية في كل مكان هي من هذا القبيل. فيحمل كلام الناس فيها على معناه المتعارف بينهم. وقد يختلف فيه بلد عن آخر، فيعتبر في كل مكان عرفه الخاص في التخاطب.
وإن ما تفيده أساليب العوام البيانية من عقد أو تعليق، أو تنجيز، أو إذن، أو إجازة، أو غير ذلك، هو معتبر وإن خالف مذاهب النحويين في قواعد اللغة الفصحى (رسائل ابن عابدین ۱۳۷/۲ و ۱۳۸).
5/69 - It becomes clear from the previous examples that verbal custom in general creates a new language that is considered authoritative in interpreting people's speech and determining the rights and obligations that result from their verbal dispositions according to customary meanings. And the colloquial language in every place is of this kind. So people's speech is interpreted according to its customary meaning. And it may differ from one country to another, so each place considers its own specific custom in communication. And what is conveyed by the expressive styles of the common people, whether contract, suspension, immediate effect, permission, ratification, or otherwise, is considered valid even if it contradicts the grammatical schools in the rules of classical Arabic (Ibn Abidin's Letters 2/137 and 138).
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage draws a general conclusion from the examples given earlier. The author, al-Zarqa, establishes that verbal custom (al-urf al-lafzi) effectively generates a new language that becomes the standard for interpreting people's statements and determining the legal consequences of their verbal acts. For example, if people commonly use a phrase to mean a specific type of contract, that meaning becomes the legal meaning. This applies particularly to colloquial language, which varies by region. Importantly, even if the common people's expressions deviate from the rules of classical Arabic grammar (as per the grammarians of Basra, Kufa, etc.), they are still legally valid. The reference to Ibn Abidin's Letters confirms this Hanafi stance. Thus, muftis must consider the local custom when issuing fatwas on contracts, divorce, and other verbal transactions.

Key Terms
العرف اللفظي (al-urf al-lafzi): Customary usage of words and phrases that gives them specific meanings in a community, overriding their original linguistic meanings.
اللغة العامية (al-lugha al-ammiyya): The colloquial language spoken by the common people in everyday transactions.
مذاهب النحويين (madhahib al-nahwiyyin): The grammatical schools (e.g., Basran and Kufan) that established the rules of classical Arabic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Verbal custom creates a new language that overrides classical grammar for legal interpretation of contracts and other verbal acts.

The Absolute Authority of Practical Custom in Transactions
States the supremacy of practical custom unless text intervenes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that practical custom governs civil transactions unless it conflicts with an explicit legislative text, and identify the exclusion.

Core Idea
Practical custom has absolute authority in civil transactions—unless it meets an explicit text.

This passage introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi legal philosophy: in the realm of everyday actions and contracts, custom (al-urf al-amali) is the decisive source. The Hanafi school gives custom sweeping power to shape rulings, contractual effects, and obligations. However, this power is checked by a clear limit: if a legislative text from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus explicitly contradicts the custom, the text prevails.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the core rule: practical custom has absolute authority in ordinary transactions.
The passage says 'absolute authority and complete sovereignty'.
2
Define its scope
Recognize that this authority applies to 'ordinary actions' and 'civil transactions' (mu'amalat), not to worship, family law, or criminal law unless custom is also relevant there.
Ibadat are generally immune from custom.
3
Apply to a concrete case
Take a common sales practice: if local custom dictates that payment must be made within a week, the contract's silence implies that custom.
The custom becomes an implicit condition.
4
Identify the exception
The rule does not apply if the custom contradicts a legislative text. For example, no custom can permit interest (riba) despite common practice.
Text (Quran, Sunnah, ijma) overrides custom.
5
Prepare for the mufti's task
When a custom arises, the mufti must verify it is widespread and not conflicting with any nass. This principle guides fatwa in modern nawazil.
Later Hanafi works detail how to balance custom and text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : سلطان العرف العملي
٦/٦٩ - إن النظر في نصوص الفقهاء، ينبيء بأن العرف العملي في ميدان الأفعال العادية والمعاملات المدنية له السلطان المطلق والسيادة التامة في فرض الأحكام وتقييد آثار العقود وتحديد الالتزامات على وفق المتعارف وذلك في كل موطن لا يصادم فيه العرف نصاً تشريعياً (٢).
(٢) فإذا عارض العرف نصاً تشريعياً أو قياساً فلفقهاء الشريعة عندئذ موقف من العرف ونظر تفصيلي في اعتباره وعدمه سوف نراه فيما بعد.
Second: The Authority of Practical Custom
6/69 - Indeed, examining the texts of the jurists indicates that practical custom in the field of ordinary actions and civil transactions has absolute authority and complete sovereignty in imposing rulings, restricting the effects of contracts, and determining obligations according to what is customary, in every place where custom does not conflict with a legislative text (2).
(2) If custom conflicts with a legislative text or analogy, then the jurists of Shariah have a stance towards custom and a detailed view regarding its consideration or non-consideration, which we will see later.
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage establishes the general principle that practical custom (al-urf al-amali) holds supreme authority in the realm of everyday actions and civil transactions (mu'amalat). According to the Hanafi jurists, custom dictates the detailed rules of contracts, defines their effects, and sets obligations—all in accordance with prevailing practice. This authority is 'absolute and complete' as long as the custom does not oppose an explicit textual ruling from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus. The footnote (2) hints at a detailed discussion to come: when custom does conflict with a text or qiyas, the jurists have a more nuanced position, balancing custom against other sources. For now, the focus is on the general rule that in the absence of text, custom is the decisive factor.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-urf al-amali): Customary practice in actions and transactions, such as common sales methods or rental terms.
نصاً تشريعياً (nassan tashri'iyyan): A legislative text from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus that explicitly rules on a matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Practical custom governs civil transactions absolutely, unless it collides with an explicit text from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus.

The Authority of Verbal Custom
Analysing how verbal custom creates new legal meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that verbal custom creates a new legal language for interpreting speech in contracts, divorces, and oaths.

Core Idea
Verbal custom overrides literal language to determine legal meanings.

This lesson explores how verbal custom (al-urf al-lafzi) can give new legal meanings to words, even overriding classical Arabic. The core example is the phrase 'Ali at-talaq' which, by custom, effects divorce despite its literal wording. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti when interpreting people's speech in various contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Example
Locate the phrase 'Ali at-talaq' in the passage. Note that men in some eras use it to pronounce divorce. Recognize the conflict: in original language, 'talaq' is a quality of the woman, so the phrase literally means 'upon me is divorce', not 'you are divorced'.
The example shows how custom can override literal meaning.
2
Understand the Principle
From the example, extract the general rule: verbal custom creates a new language (lugha jadida) that is legally binding. This new language governs interpretation of contracts, conditions, permissions, etc.
The passage says 'concrete' examples show this.
3
Apply to Vernacular
Extend the principle: everyday spoken language (al-lugha al-ammiyya) in any region is interpreted according to its customary meaning, even if it conflicts with classical grammar. Each locality has its own custom in communication.
This is a direct application of the qawida.
4
Recognize Legal Scope
Note that the principle applies to all verbal actions: contracts, conditions, permissions, authorizations, etc. The expressive styles of laymen are legally valid even if they violate grammatical rules.
Referenced to Ibn Abidin's Rasa'il.
5
Internalize the Authority
Understand that the Hanafi madhhab gives significant weight to verbal custom. When issuing fatwa, ascertain the prevailing custom in the region regarding specific phrases.
This is a key aspect of mufti conduct.

The Authority of Practical Custom in Hanafi Law
Establishing practical custom as a source of legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of practical custom and apply the maxim 'specification by custom is like specification by text' to contractual and oath scenarios.

Core Idea
Custom has the binding force of textual proof when no explicit text exists.

This lesson examines practical custom as a source of legal rulings in the Hanafi school. The source passages assert that when no primary text from Quran or Sunnah is available, custom becomes a fully authoritative reference. Two key maxims are introduced: 'what is established by custom is like what is established by text' and 'specification by custom is like specification by text.' Practical custom is divided into two types: ordinary acts and civil transactions. The lesson will guide you through the authority, types, and application of these principles.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Authority Claim
Identify the passage's assertion that practical custom has 'absolute authority' (سلطان مطلق) in imposing rulings and interpreting contracts, limited only when it contradicts a legislative text.
This authority is not innate but derives from the lack of a direct textual proof.
2
Extract the Key Maxims
Locate the two maxims: (1) 'al-thabit bi al-urf ka al-thabit bi al-nass' from al-Sarakhsi, and (2) 'al-ta'yin bi al-urf ka al-ta'yin bi al-nass' from the Majalla. Understand that both equate custom with text for establishing or specifying rulings.
These maxims are used in iftaa to fill gaps left by primary sources.
3
Distinguish the Two Types of Practical Custom
Note the division: 'urf al-af'al al-adiyyah (custom of ordinary acts) and 'urf al-mu'amalat al-madaniyyah (custom of civil transactions). The first governs personal acts, the second governs contracts and dealings.
Both types have legal effect, but the second directly impacts commercial rulings.
4
Apply the Maxim to an Example
Consider the incomplete example: if a community customarily eats only mutton, and a person swears not to eat 'meat', the oath is interpreted according to custom. The maxim 'specification by custom is like specification by text' restricts the oath to mutton only.
The example is cut off, but the legal principle is clear: custom defines the scope of ambiguous terms.

Source Text

ثانياً : سلطان العرف العملي

Secondly: The Authority of Practical Custom
6/69 - Examining the texts of jurists indicates that practical custom in the field of ordinary acts and civil transactions has absolute authority and complete sovereignty in imposing rulings, restricting the effects of contracts, and determining obligations according to what is customary, in every situation where custom does not conflict with a legislative text (2).
(2) If custom conflicts with a legislative text or analogy, then the jurists of the Sharia have a stance towards custom and a detailed consideration of its acceptance or not, which we will see later.

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom
Thus custom is considered a reference and source of rulings, and a legal proof for them when there is no other proof from the primary legislative texts.
Imam al-Sarakhsi said in al-Mabsut: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text.'
Al-Biri said in his commentary on al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir of Ibn Nujaym, quoting from al-Mushri': 'What is established by custom is established by a legal proof.'
(Reference: Ibn 'Abidin's Rasa'il 2/115)
And in this sense came Article 45 of the Majalla with the principle: 'Specification by custom is like specification by text.'
We have already mentioned that practical custom comprises two types: custom of ordinary vital acts, and custom of civil transactions (see 3/68).
We will present below a group of rulings that Islamic law has built on the consideration of practical custom in its two types, showing the authority that our jurists have recognized for it in directing rulings and imposing obligations, within the legal limits.

A) Custom of Ordinary Acts:
7/69 - Ordinary acts — even if they are personal vital acts and not of the type of transactions and civil legal relations — when people become accustomed to them and their life habits go along with them, they gain authority and influence in directing the rulings of civil transactions that are connected to those acts towards a direction that agrees with customary objectives.
Among the examples of the authority of the custom of ordinary acts and its influence on the rulings of dispositions and contracts in Islamic jurisprudence are the following:
1) If it was the custom of a people to eat mutton, and one of them swore: that he would not...
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage discusses the authority of practical custom (al-urf al-amali). It begins by stating that practical custom has absolute authority in imposing rulings and interpreting contracts, as long as it does not contradict a legislative text. A footnote clarifies that if custom conflicts with a text or analogy, a detailed discussion follows later. The author then calls the section 'Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom' and emphasizes that when no primary text exists, custom becomes a direct source of law. He cites Imam al-Sarakhsi's famous maxim from al-Mabsut: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by text.' Similarly, al-Biri states that what is established by custom is established by a legal proof. Article 45 of the Majalla also affirms: 'Specification by custom is like specification by text.' Practical custom is divided into two types: ordinary acts and civil transactions. The passage then begins exemplifying the first type with an oath about eating mutton, but the example is cut off. The overall lesson underscores the high legal authority given to custom in Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-urf al-amali): Custom in actions and dealings, not in speech; it is divided into custom of ordinary acts and custom of civil transactions
الثابت بالعرف كالثابت بالنص (al-thabit bi al-urf ka al-thabit bi al-nass): A principle meaning that what is established by custom has the same legal force as what is established by a primary text
التعيين بالعرف كالتعيين بالنص (al-ta'yin bi al-urf ka al-ta'yin bi al-nass): Specifying the meaning or scope of a term by custom is as effective as specifying it by an explicit text

Checkpoint

A) Only when it is explicitly mentioned in the Quran
B) In every situation, even if it contradicts a text
C) When there is no primary text and custom does not conflict with a legislative text
D) Only in civil transactions, not in ordinary acts

Key Takeaway
Practical custom is a fully authoritative source in Hanafi law when no explicit text exists and the custom does not contradict a text or clear analogy.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Using Custom to Weigh Conflicting Claims
Rule that custom is used to weigh conflicting claims in litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom gives initial weight to a party's claim in a dispute, subject to rebuttal by evidence.

Core Idea
Custom provisionally favours the plausible claim; evidence defeats custom.

In many legal disputes, the judge must decide whose account to accept. Hanafi jurists have developed a clear rule: custom (ʿurf) gives a procedural advantage to the party whose claim aligns with common practice. However, this is only a starting point—the opponent can always produce stronger evidence to overturn the presumption. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Question
The question is: in a dispute between two parties, whose statement is initially given weight (القول لمن)? The Hanafi answer points to custom.
This is a procedural rule, not a substantive one.
2
Understand the Role of Custom
Custom does not decide the case; it creates a provisional presumption in favour of the party whose claim is supported by consistent societal practice.
The custom must be consistent and accepted (ʿurf ṣaḥīḥ).
3
Note the Provisional Nature
The presumption is only prima facie. The judge does not issue a final judgment based on custom alone.
Custom is a starting point for tarjīḥ, not conclusive.
4
Recognize the Role of Evidence
The opposing party can rebut the presumption by bringing clear evidence (bayyinah). Once evidence is presented, the court decides based on that, overriding the initial custom-based preference.
Bayyinah includes documents, witnesses, oaths, etc.
5
Summarize the Rule
Rule: In conflicting claims, the judge initially accepts the statement of the party supported by custom, unless the opponent proves otherwise with evidence.
This rule applies across many chapters of fiqh (e.g., sales, marriage, rent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهكذا في كثير من مسائل القول لمن؟ يستند الفقهاء إلى العرف في الترجيح القضائي بين مزاعم المتداعيين فيرجحون مبدئياً قول من يشهد له العرف حتى يثبت خصمه خلافه بالبينة.
And thus in many issues of 'whose statement is given weight?' the jurists rely on custom in judicial weighing between the claims of the two litigants, so they initially give weight to the statement of the one whom custom supports, until his opponent proves otherwise with evidence.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage introduces a procedural principle in Hanafi fiqh: when two parties dispute and each makes a claim, the judge does not simply believe one or the other arbitrarily. Instead, he first looks to custom (al‑ʿurf) to see which party's claim is more plausible according to common practice. That party's statement is provisionally accepted as truthful. However, this is only a starting point; the opponent can rebut it by bringing clear evidence (bayyinah). This rule applies in many areas, such as contracts, property disputes, and family law. The Hanafi school gives custom significant weight in judicial procedure, recognizing that law does not operate in a vacuum but must reflect societal norms.

Key Terms
العرف (al‑ʿurf): Common practice or custom that is consistent and accepted among people in their dealings.
الترجيح القضائي (al‑tarjīḥ al‑qaḍāʾī): Judicial preponderance given to one party's claim over the other in litigation.
البينة (al‑bayyinah): Clear evidence that can be presented in court, such as documents, witnesses, or admissions.
القول لمن (al‑qawlu liman): A procedural question: 'Whose statement is given weight?' i.e., which party's claim is provisionally accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Custom determines the final outcome regardless of evidence.
B) Custom is used to initially prefer the claim of the party it supports, subject to rebuttal by evidence.
C) Custom is irrelevant in judicial weighing of claims.
D) Custom is only considered in commercial matters.

Key Takeaway
Custom provides a provisional presumption in favour of the claim it supports, but evidence can overturn it.

The Second Condition for Valid Custom: Contemporaneity
Explains that only prior custom governs legal acts, not later custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that only prior custom governs legal acts and distinguish when a later custom is irrelevant.

Core Idea
Only the custom existing at the time of an act governs it; later changes have no retroactive effect.

This lesson explains the second condition for the validity of considering custom: the custom must be contemporaneous with the act. This is a foundational rasm rule for a mufti, ensuring stability and predictability in legal interpretation. The rule applies to both verbal and practical custom, and even to the interpretation of legislative texts.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the core principle: custom operates only on acts that occur after it, not on previous acts. The custom must exist at the time of the act.
This is a dabt sub-maxim restricting the broader qawida 'Custom is authoritative'.
2
Differentiate verbal and practical custom
Understand that both types are governed by the same condition. For verbal custom, words are interpreted according to the custom at utterance. For practical custom, habitual practices at the time of the transaction are considered.
Verbal custom includes contracts, waqf, wills; practical custom includes defects, inclusions, etc.
3
Apply to verbal custom
Given a document (e.g., waqf) using a phrase like 'according to the prescribed share', interpret it based on the custom when the document was created. Later changes do not affect the meaning.
Example: male gets double female share if that was the custom at creation.
4
Apply to legislative texts
Quranic terms like 'fi sabil Allah' must be understood according to their customary meaning at revelation, not later interpretations.
This ensures stability in Sharia interpretation.
5
Verify with furuat
Apply the rule to specific cases from the text: sales contract currency, change in defect criteria, etc.
In each case, the prior custom governs, not later changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۷۰) شرائط اعتبار العرف
۳/۷۰ - الشريطة الثانية أن يكون العرف المراد تحكيمه في التصرفات قائماً عند إنشائها : العرف إنما يعمل فيما يوجد بعده لا فيما مضى قبله.
فالعرف الحاكم في أمر من أن يكون موجوداً الأمور بين الناس يجب عند وجود هذا الأمر ليصح حمله عليه. وهذا احتراز عن العرف الحادث، فإنه لا عبرة له بالنسبة إلى الماضي ولا يحكم فيه .
وهذه الشريطة أيضاً شاملة للعرف اللفظي والعملي:
أ - ففي العرف اللفظي رأينا أن كلام المتصرف بالإرادة المنفردة كالوقف والحلف والنذر والوصيَّة والطلاق وكذا كلام العاقد في العقود، إنما يحمل على معانيه العرفية دون معانيه اللغوية في أصل اللغة ولكن هذا العرف اللفظي الذي تحمل عليه معاني الكلام إنما هو العرف القائم حين صدور هذه التصرفات من أصحابها، لأنه هو الذي يعين مراد المتكلم. فإذا تغير العرف بعد ذلك في مفاهيم تلك الألفاظ والتراكيب الواردة في الوقفيات والوصايا وسائر الصكوك فلا عبرة للعرف الحادث في تفسير التصرفات القولية الواقعة في ظل العرف القديم، وإنما الذي يحمل على العرف اللفظي الجديد هو ما يصدر بعده من تصرفات.
فلفظ على الفريضة الشرعية مثلاً في كلام الواقفين والموصين معناه العرفي إلى اليوم أن يكون للذكر من غلة الوقف أو مال الوصية ضعف ما للأنثى. فلو تبدل العرف في هذا التعبير فأصبح معناه التساوي في الحصص بين الذكور والإناث لا يؤثر هذا التبدل في أحكام الوقفيات والوصايا السابقة الجارية على مقتضى العرف اللفظي الأول.
يقول العلامة ابن نجيم في الأشباه والنظائر الفن الأول ۱۳۳/۱):
العرف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر. ولذلك قالوا: لا عبرة بالعرف الطارىء» ومثل ذلك يقال في نصوص الشارع أيضاً.
فالنصوص التشريعية يجب أن تفهم بحسب مدلولاتها اللغوية والعرفية في عصر صدور النص، لأنها هي مراد الشارع ولا عبرة لتبدل مفاهيم الألفاظ في الأعراف الزمنية المتأخرة، وإلا لم يستقر للنص التشريعي معنى. فمثلاً لفظ في سبيل الله من آية مصارف الزكاة له معنى عرفي إذ ذاك، هو مصالح الجهاد الشرعي، أو سبل الخيرات مطلقاً على اختلاف بين ذلك العلماء في ولفظ ابن السبيل فيها أيضاً معناه العرفي من ينقطع من الناس في السفر (۱).
فإذا تبدل عرف الناس في شيء من هذه التعابير، فأصبح مثلاً معنى سبيل الله» طلب العلم خاصة وأصبح معنى ابن السبيل» الطفل اللقيط الذي لا يعرف له أهل، فإن النص التشريعي يظل محمولاً على معناه العرفي الأول عند صدوره ومعمولاً به في حدود ذلك المعنى، لأنه هو مراد الشارع ولا عبرة للمعاني العرفية أو الاصطلاحية الحادثة بعد ورود النص. يقول القرافي : دلالة العرف مقدَّمة على دلالة اللغة لأن العرف ناسخ للغة، والناسخ يقدم على المنسوخ فكما أن عقد البيع يحمل فيه الثمن على النقود المعتادة، ولا عبرة في هذا البيع لتبدل العادات بعده في النقود، كذلك نصوص الشريعة لا يؤثر فيها إلا ما قارنها من العادات اهـ باختصار )
ب - وفي العرف العملي أيضاً إنما يعتبر في كل تصرف من الأعراف المؤثرة فيه ما كان موجوداً عند التصرف دون الحادث بعده. فلو تبدل عرف الناس مثلاً فيما يعد عيباً في المبيع، أو فيما يدخل (۱) ر: تفسير الجلالين سورة التوبة الآية ٦١ ورد المحتار: باب المصرف في الزكاة .٦١/٢ (۲) ر كتاب (مالك) للأستاذ الشيخ محمد أبي زهرة ف/ ٩٣ ص ٢٥٠ نقلاً عن تنقيح الفصول للقرافي ص .١٩٤
Chapter (70): Conditions for Considering Custom
3/70 - The second condition: The custom intended to be applied in transactions must be existing at the time of their creation. Custom only operates on what occurs after it, not on what preceded it.
Thus, the custom governing a matter among people must exist at the occurrence of that matter for it to be correctly attributed to it. This excludes newly arising custom, which is not considered for the past and does not govern it.
This condition also includes verbal and practical custom:
A - In verbal custom, we have seen that the speech of a person acting by unilateral will, such as endowment, oath, vow, will, and divorce, as well as the speech of a contracting party in contracts, is interpreted according to its customary meanings, not its linguistic meanings in the original language. But this verbal custom by which the meanings of speech are interpreted is only the custom existing at the time these acts were issued by their doers, because it determines the speaker's intent. If the custom later changes in the concepts of those words and phrases contained in endowments, wills, and other documents, then the newly arising custom is not considered in interpreting verbal acts that occurred under the old custom. Rather, only the new verbal custom applies to acts issued after it.
For example, the phrase "according to the prescribed share" in the speech of waqf founders and testators, its customary meaning to this day is that the male receives twice the female from the proceeds of the waqf or the wealth of the will. If the custom in this expression were to change so that it meant equality of shares between males and females, this change would not affect the rulings of previous endowments and wills that were executed according to the first verbal custom.
The scholar Ibn Nujaym says in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, Art 1, p. 133: "The custom by which words are interpreted is the prior concomitant, not the later one. That is why they said: 'No consideration is given to arising custom.' The same is said regarding the texts of the Lawgiver as well."
Thus, legislative texts must be understood according to their linguistic and customary significations at the time of the text's issuance, because that is the intent of the Lawgiver, and no consideration is given to changes in the meanings of words due to later temporal customs. Otherwise, the legislative text would have no stable meaning. For example, the word "fi sabil Allah" from the verse on the categories of zakat expenditure had a customary meaning at that time, namely the interests of legitimate jihad, or generally the ways of goodness, according to scholarly differences. And the word "ibn al-sabil" likewise had its customary meaning of a traveler who becomes stranded.
If people's custom changes in any of these expressions, so that, for example, "sabil Allah" comes to mean seeking knowledge specifically, and "ibn al-sabil" comes to mean a foundling child with no known family, then the legislative text remains interpreted according to its first customary meaning at the time of its revelation and is applied within the limits of that meaning, because it is the intent of the Lawgiver, and no consideration is given to customary or technical meanings arising after the text's revelation. Al-Qarafi says: "The indication of custom is given precedence over the indication of language because custom abrogates language, and the abrogating is given precedence over the abrogated. Just as in a sales contract, the price is interpreted as the usual currency, and no consideration is given in this sale to changes in custom regarding currency afterwards, similarly, the texts of the Sharia are only affected by the customs that were concomitant with them." End quote, summarized.
B - In practical custom as well, what is considered in each transaction from among the effective customs is that which existed at the time of the transaction, not that which arises afterwards. So if people's custom changes, for example, regarding what is considered a defect in the sold item, or regarding what is included [in the sale]...
Source pages p. 842

Commentary
This passage presents the second condition for the validity of considering custom ('urf) in legal rulings: the custom must be contemporaneous with the act being judged. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that custom only operates prospectively, not retroactively. This is a rasm rule (methodological principle) for muftis. The condition applies to both verbal custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). For verbal custom, words in contracts, wills, endowments, etc., are interpreted according to the custom prevailing at the time of utterance, not later changed meanings. For example, the phrase 'according to the prescribed share' (على الفريضة الشرعية) in a waqf document gives males twice females; if custom later changes to equality, the old documents remain interpreted by the old custom. The author quotes Ibn Nujaym's maxim: 'The custom by which words are interpreted is the prior concomitant, not the later one.' This is extended even to the Quranic text: words like 'fi sabil Allah' and 'ibn al-sabil' must be understood according to their customary meaning at revelation, not later shifts. Al-Qarafi's statement is cited to emphasize that custom abrogates language and is given precedence, but only the custom contemporaneous with the text. The author then applies the principle to practical custom: a change in what is considered a defect in goods does not affect past sales. The rule guards against the danger of unstable legal meanings and ensures predictability in contracts and texts.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; recurrent practice of people that is accepted and not contrary to Sharia.
العرف اللفظي (al-'urf al-lafzi): Verbal custom; the conventional meaning of words and phrases among people, distinct from linguistic origin.
العرف العملي (al-'urf al-'amali): Practical custom; habitual actions and practices in transactions and dealings.
العرف الحادث (al-'urf al-hadith): Newly arising custom that occurs after the act in question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It governs the contract because custom always takes precedence.
B) It governs only if it is a practical custom.
C) It does not affect the contract; the prior custom governs.
D) It governs if both parties agree to apply it.

Key Takeaway
Only the custom contemporaneous with the act governs its interpretation; later changes are irrelevant.
