Interpreting Sharia Texts According to Custom at Revelation
Explaining the first condition: custom at revelation governs interpretation of sharia texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that sharia texts are interpreted according to the custom at the time of revelation, and identify the correct interpretation when faced with shifting customary meanings.

Core Idea
The meaning of a sharia text is fixed by the custom at revelation; later custom cannot change it.

When a Quranic or Sunnah text uses a term, its legal meaning is determined by how people understood that term at the time the text was revealed. This is because the Lawgiver addressed them with that understanding. Any later shift in custom does not alter the original legal meaning. This lesson explains this foundational principle and its application in Hanafi fiqh, with examples from zakat law and the principle stated by al-Qarafi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text and the term in question
Find the Quranic or hadith phrase that has a customary dimension. For example, 'fi sabil Allah' in the zakat verse (9:60) or 'ibn al-sabil' in the same verse.
The term must be one that had a specific customary meaning at the time of revelation.
2
Determine the customary meaning at the time of revelation
Use historical context, early commentaries, and lexicons to ascertain what people understood by that term at the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him). For 'fi sabil Allah', it meant the interests of legitimate jihad or general good deeds.
This is a fact-finding step relying on reliable sources.
3
Apply that meaning as the authoritative interpretation
The legal ruling derived from the text must be based on that original customary meaning. Any later innovation in meaning does not affect the text.
4
Reject any later customary meaning
If custom later shifts to a different meaning (e.g., 'sabil Allah' becomes 'seeking knowledge'), the text remains fixed to its original meaning. The later custom is irrelevant.
This is a key error to avoid: some may mistakenly apply current custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنصوص التشريعية يجب أن تفهم بحسب مدلولاتها اللغوية والعرفية في عصر صدور النص، لأنها هي مراد الشارع ولا عبرة لتبدل مفاهيم الألفاظ في الأعراف الزمنية المتأخرة، وإلا لم يستقر للنص التشريعي معنى. فمثلاً لفظ في سبيل الله من آية مصارف الزكاة له معنى عرفي إذ ذاك، هو مصالح الجهاد الشرعي، أو سبل الخيرات مطلقاً على اختلاف بين ذلك العلماء في ولفظ ابن السبيل فيها أيضاً معناه العرفي من ينقطع من الناس في السفر (۱). فإذا تبدل عرف الناس في شيء من هذه التعابير، فأصبح مثلاً معنى سبيل الله» طلب العلم خاصة وأصبح معنى ابن السبيل» الطفل اللقيط الذي لا يعرف له أهل، فإن النص التشريعي يظل محمولاً على معناه العرفي الأول عند صدوره ومعمولاً به في حدود ذلك المعنى، لأنه هو مراد الشارع ولا عبرة للمعاني العرفية أو الاصطلاحية الحادثة بعد ورود النص. يقول القرافي : دلالة العرف مقدَّمة على دلالة اللغة لأن العرف ناسخ للغة، والناسخ يقدم على المنسوخ فكما أن عقد البيع يحمل فيه الثمن على النقود المعتادة، ولا عبرة في هذا البيع لتبدل العادات بعده في النقود، كذلك نصوص الشريعة لا يؤثر فيها إلا ما قارنها من العادات اهـ باختصار ) (۲)
Legislative texts must be understood according to their linguistic and customary meanings at the time the text was issued, because that is the intent of the Lawgiver, and no consideration is given to changes in the meanings of words in later temporal customs. Otherwise, no meaning would be established for the legislative text. For example, the phrase 'fi sabil Allah' from the verse on the categories of zakat had a customary meaning at that time, which is the interests of legitimate jihad, or the paths of goodness absolutely, according to disagreement among scholars on that, and the phrase 'ibn al-sabil' therein also had its customary meaning: the person who is stranded among people during travel (1). If the custom of people changes in some of these expressions, such that, for example, the meaning of 'sabil Allah' becomes seeking knowledge specifically, and the meaning of 'ibn al-sabil' becomes the foundling child whose family is unknown, then the legislative text remains carried upon its first customary meaning at the time of its issuance and acted upon within the limits of that meaning, because it is the intent of the Lawgiver, and no consideration is given to customary or terminological meanings that arise after the text's appearance. Al-Qarafi says: The indication of custom is given precedence over the indication of language because custom abrogates language, and the abrogating is given precedence over the abrogated. Just as in a sales contract, the price is understood to be in the customary currency, and no consideration is given in this sale to changes in customs thereafter regarding currency, likewise, the texts of the Sharia are only affected by the customs that coexisted with them. End quote, summarized. (2)
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage presents the first condition in the theory of custom: that sharia texts are interpreted according to the customary meanings at the time of their revelation. The author explains that the lawgiver's intent is embedded in the contemporaneous understanding, so later semantic shifts do not alter the legal meanings. Examples from zakat law illustrate how terms like 'fi sabil Allah' and 'ibn al-sabil' must retain their original customary sense, not later innovations. Al-Qarafi's rule reinforces that custom takes precedence over language because it can abrogate linguistic meanings, similar to how a sale's price is interpreted in the current currency at the time of the contract.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom: what is commonly known and practiced among people in a particular time and place.
دلالة العرف (dalalat al-'urf): The indication or evidence provided by custom in interpreting legal texts and transactions.
النص التشريعي (al-nass al-tashri'i): A legislative text from the Quran or Sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the phrase 'ibn al-sabil' in the zakat verse originally meant:
A) A foundling child
B) A stranded traveler
C) A student seeking knowledge
D) Any poor person

Key Takeaway
Sharia texts must be interpreted according to the customary meaning at the time of revelation; later custom does not alter the text's legal significance.

Practical Custom After a Transaction Does Not Affect It
Explaining that only the custom existing at the time of a transaction is considered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that only the custom existing at the time of a transaction is considered, and distinguish which transactions are affected by a change in custom.

Core Idea
Only the custom at contract time binds; later custom cannot rewrite old deals.

This passage introduces a fundamental rule of practical custom: the custom that existed at the moment of a transaction is the only one that governs it—not any change that occurs later. This principle ensures stability in contracts and prevents retroactive reinterpretation. For a mufti, understanding this rule is crucial when applying custom-based rulings to ongoing or past transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its governing custom
Determine the time of the transaction (tasarruf) and identify the custom that prevailed at that moment.
The 'urun al-amali at the time of the contract is the reference.
2
Compare with later custom
Check if the custom has changed after the transaction. If it has, note that the new custom does not apply to this transaction.
The passage explicitly states 'لا يسري على التصرفات السابقة'.
3
Apply the rule to the specific issue
For the case at hand (e.g., defect, appurtenance, rent payment), state that the original ruling based on the old custom remains unchanged.
The examples in the passage illustrate this application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - وفي العرف العملي أيضاً إنما يعتبر في كل تصرف من الأعراف المؤثرة فيه ما كان موجوداً عند التصرف دون الحادث بعده. فلو تبدل عرف الناس مثلاً فيما يعد عيباً في المبيع، أو فيما يدخل في البيع تبعاً للمبيع، أو في تقسيط أجرة العقارات المأجورة، أو في كون سنة الإيجار شمسية أو قمرية أو في تقسيم المهر في النكاح إلى معجل ومؤجل، إلى غير ذلك من الشؤون، فإنَّ العرف الحادث لا يسري على التصرفات السابقة ولا يبدل شيئاً من أحكامها والتزاماتها، وإنما تخضع التصرفات الجديدة الواقعة في ظله.
B) And in practical custom as well, only those customs that existed at the time of an action are considered among the customs affecting it, not the one that occurs after it. So if people's custom changes, for example, regarding what is considered a defect in a sold item, or what is included in the sale as an appurtenance, or regarding the installment of rent for leased properties, or whether the lease year is solar or lunar, or the division of dowry in marriage into prompt and deferred, and other such matters, then the new custom does not apply to prior transactions and does not change any of their rulings and obligations; rather, new transactions that occur under its influence are subject to it.
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage states the second condition: that only the custom prevailing at the time of a transaction is considered. Customary changes that occur later do not retroactively alter the effects of previous contracts. Examples include defects in goods, appurtenances included in a sale, rent installments, lease year calculation, and division of mahr. The rule ensures stability of transactions.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-'urf al-'amali): Practical custom: the common practice in transactions and actions, as opposed to verbal custom (al-'urf al-qawli).
تصرف (tasarruf): An action or transaction, especially a legal disposition like sale, lease, marriage, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All sales before and after
B) Only sales after the change
C) Only sales before the change
D) None

Key Takeaway
A change in custom after a transaction does not alter the rights and obligations created at the time of that transaction.

The Third Condition: Custom Yields to Explicit Stipulation
Explaining the third condition restricting the custom-as-condition maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that explicit stipulations override custom in Hanafi contracts.

Core Idea
Custom is binding only when there is no explicit contrary stipulation — explicit agreements override implied customs.

In the Hanafi school, custom plays a significant role in shaping contractual obligations, but it is not absolute. An earlier maxim holds that 'what is known by custom is like a stipulated condition' — but only when the parties remain silent. The moment they explicitly agree on a term, that explicit stipulation takes precedence. This lesson unpacks the third condition that restricts the application of custom: the absence of a conflicting explicit statement.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the underlying maxim
Identify the maxim 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan' and understand that it treats custom as an implied condition only when parties are silent.
This maxim is widely applied in fiqh muamalat.
2
Understand the basis in silence
Note that the authority of custom arises from the parties' reliance on it when they fail to stipulate explicitly.
If they do stipulate, the basis for custom disappears.
3
Apply the third condition
The explicit stipulation must be feasible and consistent with the purpose of the contract to override custom.
Impossible stipulations (e.g., working without sleep) void the contract due to gharar.
4
Use the complementary maxim
The principle 'la 'ibrah li al-dalalah fi muqabilat al-tasrih' (no weight to indication against explicit text) reinforces that custom, being a dalalah, yields to tasrih.
This maxim is from the Majallah (article 13).
5
Distinguish valid from invalid stipulations
A stipulation that is possible and consistent with the contract purpose is binding; an impossible one makes the contract void.
Example from the text: working without breaks valid; working without sleep impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٧٠ - الشريطة الثالثة : أن لا يعارض العرف تصريح بخلافه : هذه الشريطة قيد أساسي في القاعدة المتقدمة في بيان عرف المعاملات، وهي أن المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً». فقد تقدم أن علة تنزيل الأمر المعروف منزلة المشروط هي سكوت المتعاقدين عن الأمر المتعارف وعدم اشتراطهم إياه صراحة يعتبر اعتماداً على العرف الجاري (ر : ف ٩/٦٩). واقعاً منهم فإثبات الحكم المتعارف في هذه الحال إنما هو من قبيل الدلالة، فإذا صرح بخلافه بطلت هذه الدلالة، إذ من القواعد الفقهية المقررة أنه: «لا عبرة للدلالة في مقابلة التصريح» (ر: المجلة/ ۱۳). يقول الإمام عز الدين عبد العزيز بن عبد السلام الشافعي في كتابه قواعد الأحكام» (۱۷۸/۲). كل ما يثبت بالعرف إذا صَرَّح المتعاقدان بخلافه بما يوافق مقصود العقد، ويمكن الوفاء به صح. فلو شرط المستأجر على الأجير أن يستوعب النهار بالعمل من غير أكل وشرب ويقطع المنفعة لزمه ذلك. ولو شرط عليه أن يعمل شهراً في الليل والنهار بحيث لا ينام ليلاً ولا نهاراً فالذي أراه بطلان هذه الإجارة لتعذر الوفاء به فكان ذلك غرراً لا تمس الحاجة إليه اه باختصار . ويقول العلامة التركي علي حيدر في شرح المادة / ۳۷/ من المجلة : إنَّ العرف يكون حجة إذا لم يكن مخالفاً لنص أو شرط لأحد
4/70 - The third condition: That custom does not contradict an explicit statement to the contrary. This condition is a basic restriction on the aforementioned rule concerning the custom of transactions, namely: 'What is known by custom is like what is stipulated as a condition.' It was previously stated that the cause for treating a customary matter as equivalent to a stipulated condition is the silence of the contracting parties regarding the customary matter and their not stipulating it explicitly, relying on the prevailing custom (see: f 9/69). So, establishing the customary ruling in this case is in the nature of an indication. Therefore, if they explicitly state something to the contrary, this indication is invalidated, because it is among the established fiqh principles: 'No consideration is given to an indication in the face of an explicit statement' (see: al-Majallah/13). Imam 'Izz al-Din 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn 'Abd al-Salam al-Shafi'i says in his book Qawa'id al-Ahkam (2/178): 'Everything that is established by custom, if the contracting parties explicitly state something to the contrary that is consistent with the purpose of the contract and is feasible, it is valid. For example, if the lessee stipulates upon the lessor that he work the entire day without eating or drinking and cut off the benefit, it is binding on him. And if he stipulates upon him that he work day and night for a month such that he does not sleep night or day, then what I see is the invalidity of this lease because fulfillment is impossible, so it is a gharar (uncertainty) that is not needed. End quote, summarized.' And the Turkish scholar 'Ali Haydar says in his commentary on article 37 of the Majallah: 'Custom is evidence as long as it does not contradict a text or a stipulation of one of the parties.'
Source pages p. 844

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition: custom is not valid if it contradicts an explicit stipulation. The reason custom is treated like a stipulation is because the parties rely on it when silent. But if they explicitly agree otherwise, that explicit agreement takes precedence, based on the principle that explicit statements override implied indications. The author cites Imam 'Izz al-Din ibn 'Abd al-Salam, who gives examples: a valid stipulation that the worker works all day without breaks is binding, but an impossible stipulation like working without any sleep makes the contract void due to gharar. 'Ali Haydar confirms that custom is proof only if not conflicting with a text or a condition.

Key Terms
الشريطة الثالثة (al-sharitah al-thalithah): The third condition: that custom does not contradict an explicit stipulation to the contrary.
المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً (al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan): A legal maxim meaning that what is established by custom is like a stipulated condition.
دلالة (dalalah): An indication or implied meaning, as opposed to an explicit statement.
تصريح (tasrih): An explicit statement or stipulation that clearly expresses the intent of the speaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a contracting party explicitly stipulates something that goes against the prevailing custom but is feasible and consistent with the contract's purpose, what is the ruling?
A) The custom prevails
B) The stipulation is valid and binding
C) The contract is void
D) The stipulation is invalid

Key Takeaway
Explicit stipulations override custom, provided they are feasible and consistent with the contract's purpose.

The Fourth Condition for Custom: Not Nullifying a Definitive Text or Principle
Introduces the fourth condition for valid custom and classifies conflicts with legal evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom is invalid if it nullifies a definitive text or principle, and distinguish between situations where custom is accepted and where it is rejected.

Core Idea
A custom that nullifies a definitive text or principle is invalid; reconciliable custom retains authority.

This lesson introduces the fourth and most critical condition for a valid custom ('urf) in Hanafi jurisprudence: it must not nullify a definitive text (nass thābit) or a definitive principle (aṣl qaṭ‘ī) of Sharia. The passage establishes the general rule, explains how to distinguish between conflicting and non-conflicting customs, and sets the stage for a detailed classification of conflicts. This principle safeguards the primacy of revelation while allowing flexibility for societal changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fourth Condition
Read the passage carefully and locate the statement of the fourth condition for valid custom. State it in your own words.
The condition is explicitly given in the heading: 'That custom does not nullify a definitive text or a definitive principle in Sharia.'
2
Distinguish Definitive vs. Ijtihadi
The passage explains that the outcome depends on whether the custom conflicts with a definitive text/principle or with ijtihadi evidence. Identify the key difference and how it affects the validity of custom.
Definitive = qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalala (Quran, mutawatir sunnah, consensus). Ijtihadi = zanni (single-transmission hadith, qiyas, etc.)
3
Apply the Condition to Examples
The passage gives a detailed example about a borrowed animal and a hired worker. Apply the fourth condition to these examples: why is explicit stipulation decisive? Can custom override it?
In the loan example, the lender's explicit restriction takes precedence over custom. This illustrates that custom cannot nullify an explicit contractual stipulation (which is not a definitive text, but the principle is similar).
4
Classify the Conflict
The passage lists four cases of conflict between custom and legal evidence. Identify the two cases mentioned explicitly and infer the remaining two from the context of the upcoming chapters.
The two explicit cases: (1) custom clashes with a specific text, (2) custom conflicts with a general text (with subcases). The remaining two (likely conflict with ijtihadi opinions and conflict with principles) are implied.

Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
... [the contracting parties]. So if a person hires a worker to work from noon to afternoon only, he cannot force him to work from morning to evening claiming that the custom of the town is like that. (1) Accordingly, the loan (i'ara) is restricted in time, place, and amount by what the lender restricts, even if his restriction is contrary to custom, for custom is not considered with explicit stipulation to the contrary. So if a person borrows an animal, for example, for riding or loading, he is deemed permitted to ride it or load it with the customary amount in similar situations by custom. But if the lender explicitly forbids him from riding it for more than a period or loading it more than a specific amount he set for him, it is not permissible for the borrower to exceed it, even by an amount not exceeding the customary. If he exceeds, he is a transgressor in the ruling of a usurper (see: Majalla articles 816-817).

70/5 - The Fourth Condition: That custom does not nullify a definitive text or a definitive principle in Sharia. If custom is contrary to some legal evidences from the texts of Sharia or its rules and principles, the general principle derived from the sayings of the researching jurists in summary is that: if acting upon custom results in nullifying a definitive text or a definitive principle in Sharia, then custom is not considered, because the text of the Lawgiver takes precedence over custom. But if this nullification does not result from custom, rather custom is such that the legal text can be interpreted in line with it or a reconciliation can be made between them, then custom is considered and has respected authority. The distinction between these two situations is based on a detailed view in which the results differ according to whether the custom is contrary to a text or a definitive principle, or contrary to ijtihad evidence, and also according to whether the custom contradicting the text is contemporaneous with its revelation or arises after it, and according to whether the custom is verbal or practical, general or specific.

This is what we will explain in the following chapters (71-74) which we devote to the investigation of the violation of legal evidence by custom.

(1) Arabic translation of the mentioned explanation by attorney Fahmi al-Husseini.
902

Chapter Seventy-One
Introduction to Chapters 71-74
Cases of Custom's Violation of Legal Evidence

71/1 - Indeed, people's custom and transactions may exceed the basic limits of legislation, so they contradict a text from the texts of Sharia or some established ijtihad opinions. So what is the position of Islamic jurisprudence then regarding the authority of custom, and what is the degree of custom in this transgression? This is a fundamental and serious issue because it pertains to the meaning of legislation and its vitality and sovereignty. Islamic jurisprudence has adopted a wise position on this issue, combining the necessary flexibility for the development of rulings that must adapt according to life circumstances, with the preservation of fixed principles. We will explain this jurisprudential position in detail below.

71/2 - Indeed, the violation of legal evidence by custom occurs in four cases with different results:
1 - Either custom clashes with a specific legislative text from the texts of the Quran or established Sunnah commanding contrary to what custom has established.
2 - Or custom conflicts with a general text, and under it there are two cases,
903
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage is from Chapter Nine of Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aamm, discussing the theory of custom ('urf). It begins by illustrating a practical application: when a contract explicitly restricts the time of work (e.g., from noon to afternoon), custom cannot be used to extend that period. Similarly, in a loan (i'ara), if the lender explicitly restricts the duration or load, custom cannot override that stipulation. This establishes that explicit conditions take precedence over custom.

The author then presents the fourth condition for a valid custom: that it must not nullify a definitive text (nass thabit) or a definitive principle (asl qat'i) in Sharia. This is a critical legal principle. If applying a custom would lead to the negation of a clear Quranic verse, authentic hadith, or a settled rule of fiqh, then that custom is invalid. However, if the custom can be reconciled with the text—for example, by interpreting the text in light of the custom—then custom is accepted and has authority.

The distinction between these two scenarios is nuanced. It depends on:
- Whether the custom contradicts a definitive text/principle or merely an ijtihadi (non-definitive) evidence.
- Whether the custom existed at the time of the text's revelation or arose later.
- Whether the custom is verbal (related to linguistic usage) or practical (related to customary behavior), and whether it is general or specific.

The text then introduces the next four chapters (71-74) which will examine in detail the situations where custom conflicts with legal evidence. The author notes that Islamic jurisprudence strikes a balance between flexibility to accommodate changing customs and preservation of fixed principles. Four main cases are outlined: (1) custom clashing with a specific text (like a clear command in Quran or hadith), (2) custom conflicting with a general text (which has subcases), and then two more cases implied in the following chapters (presumably (3) conflict with ijtihadi opinions, and (4) perhaps custom that is not in direct conflict but affects application).

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): العرف هو ما استقر في نفوس الناس من العادات والمعاملات، سواء كان لفظياً أو عملياً، عاماً أو خاصاً.
النص الثابت (al-nass al-thābit): النص القطعي الثبوت والدلالة من القرآن أو السنة المتواترة.
الأصل القطعي (al-aṣl al-qaṭ‘ī): القاعدة أو المبدأ الشرعي المقطوع به، كحرمة الربا أو وجوب الصلاة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
متى يكون العرف غير معتبر عند الحنفية؟
A) إذا كان مخالفاً لنص ظني.
B) إذا ترتب على العمل به تعطيل لنص شرعي ثابت أو أصل قطعي.
C) إذا كان العرف عاماً.
D) إذا كان العرف لفظياً.

Key Takeaway
Custom is only valid in Hanafi law if it does not nullify a definitive text or principle; otherwise it is rejected.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Four Cases of Conflict Between Custom and Shariah Evidence
Classification of custom-evidence conflicts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the conflict between custom and Shariah evidence into the four cases and identify which type of evidence (specific text, general text, ijtihad) is involved.

Core Idea
Custom cannot override a specific text; its scope is limited to general texts and ijtihad.

Muftis often encounter situations where established custom seems to contradict a ruling from the Quran, Sunnah, or scholarly ijtihad. This lesson provides a systematic classification of such conflicts, distinguishing between specific texts (nass khass), general texts (nass 'amm), and juristic opinions. Understanding these categories is essential for determining when custom can be accommodated and when it must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evidence type
Determine whether the custom opposes a specific text (nass khass), a general text (nass 'amm), or a juristic opinion (ijtihad). This classification determines the legal consequences.
A specific text directly addresses the matter; a general text covers multiple matters including the customary one.
2
For general texts, check temporality
If the custom conflicts with a general text, verify whether the custom existed at the time of revelation (al-‘urf al-muqarin) or arose later (al-‘urf al-hadith). This affects the possibility of specifying the general text by custom.
Hanafis allow specification by custom only if the custom was concurrent or, under certain conditions, if it is later but widespread.
3
Apply Hanafi rules for each case
For specific texts: custom is invalid and cannot override. For general texts: custom may specify if it meets conditions. For juristic opinions: custom can be a factor in preferring one opinion over another.
Hanafi usul: al-‘urf al-khass cannot contradict al-nass al-khass.
4
Conclude on validity
Based on the analysis, decide whether the custom can be accepted (valid) or must be rejected (invalid). Provide the reasoning referencing the evidence type and Hanafi principles.
The mufta bihi position is that custom cannot override a definitive text.

Chapter Seventy-One
Preamble to Sections 71-74
Cases of Custom Violating Legal Evidences
1/71 - Indeed, the custom and dealings of people may transgress the fundamental boundaries of legislation, contradicting a text from the texts of Shariah or some of the established juristic opinions in it.
So what is the position of Islamic jurisprudence at that time regarding the authority of custom, and what is the degree of custom in this transgression?
This is a crucial and dangerous issue, because it relates to the meaning of legislation, its vitality, and its sovereignty.
Islamic jurisprudence has taken a wise stance on this issue, combining the necessary flexibility for the development of rulings that must adapt to the circumstances of life with the preservation of fixed principles.
We will explain this juristic stance in detail below:
2/71 - Indeed, the violation of custom against legal evidences occurs in four cases with different consequences:
1 - Either custom clashes with a specific legislative text, from the texts of the Quran or the established Sunnah, commanding contrary to what the custom is upon.
2 - Or custom conflicts with a general text, and under it there are two cases:
Book Nine: The Theory of Custom
And what is meant by specific text here is whatever is received from the Lawgiver concerning the matter that is the subject of the custom, forbidding it specifically.
As for the general text, what is meant by it here is whatever contains a comprehensive ruling for the matter in which custom operates and also includes others, such that the customary matter is one of the individuals of the text and among its inclusions (1)
3 - Or it violates juristic opinions, i.e., the rulings determined by jurists by way of deduction and juristic understanding through analogy, juristic preference, or public interest in emerging issues for which no explicit text from Shariah has been received.
And upon this, the discussion branches into four chapters:
- The case of custom clashing with a specific legislative text
- The case of custom concurrent with the revelation of a general text that opposes it
- The case of custom arising after a general text that opposes it
- The case of conflict between custom and ijtihad.
(1) Generality and specificity in the terminology of the scholars of usul al-fiqh are originally attributes of words with regard to their meanings. The specific word is that which indicates an individual, like 'man', 'woman', and 'person' (in indefinite form), or indicates limited individuals like the names of numbers such as 'three', 'ten', etc. The general word is that which includes many unlimited individuals in a comprehensive and all-encompassing manner, i.e., it includes all of them, such as Allah's statement in the Quran: 'And to Allah is due the pilgrimage to the House for those who are able to find a way' and the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi (1866) and Abu Dawud (3681) on drinks, from Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'Whatever intoxicates in large amounts, a small amount of it is forbidden.' The word 'people' and the word 'whatever' are both general. Reference: al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali 2/35-36, first Amiri edition, and Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 2/2 onwards). And by the generality of the word in the text, the text and the ruling it contains are also described as general.
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a series of chapters on custom ('urf) and its interaction with legal evidence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that custom can sometimes exceed the boundaries of Shariah by contradicting a specific text from the Quran or Sunnah, or by opposing established juristic opinions. He poses the crucial question of how Islamic jurisprudence balances the authority of custom with the preservation of fixed principles, and he signals the wise approach that combines flexibility with the protection of constants.

The core of the passage is the classification of conflicts between custom and legal evidence into four distinct cases, each with different legal consequences. The author lists them:
1. Custom clashes with a specific legislative text (a clear ruling directed at that particular matter).
2. Custom conflicts with a general text (which covers the matter along with other things). This case is subdivided further but not detailed here.
3. Custom contradicts juristic opinions that were derived through qiyas, istihsan, or istislah for new matters without explicit textual evidence.

These three scenarios give rise to the four chapters that follow: (a) custom against a specific text, (b) custom concurrent with a general text, (c) custom arising after a general text, and (d) custom versus ijtihad.

The footnote explains the usul al-fiqh distinction between 'specific' (khass) and 'general' ('amm) texts, which is essential for understanding the classification. The author clarifies that a specific text is one that deals with a particular matter, while a general text encompasses a wide range of individuals. Examples from the Quran and Sunnah illustrate general texts.

From a Hanafi perspective, this classification is standard; Hanafi usul al-fiqh accepts the authority of custom ('urf) as a source of law under certain conditions, but it cannot override a clear, specific text. When custom conflicts with a general text, the Hanafis apply rules such as takhsis al-'amm bi al-'urf (specification of the general by custom) or consider whether the custom existed at the time of revelation ('urf muqarin) or arose later ('urf hadith). For juristic opinions, custom can be a determining factor in ijtihad as long as it does not contradict a definitive text. This passage lays the groundwork for that discussion.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): the custom and dealings of people that may transgress legislative boundaries
النص الخاص (al-nass al-khass): a text that is specifically about the matter of the custom, forbidding it directly
النص العام (al-nass al-'amm): a text that includes a ruling covering the customary matter and other matters as well, so the custom is part of its general application
الآراء الاجتهادية (al-ara' al-ijtihadiyyah): juristic opinions derived through qiyas, istihsan, or istislah for new matters without explicit text

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, into how many cases does the conflict between custom and legal evidence fall?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The conflict between custom and Shariah evidence is classified into four cases based on whether the evidence is a specific text, a general text (with two subcases), or a juristic opinion.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Custom vs. Specific Text: The Hanafi Rule of Invalidation
Explaining when custom is invalid due to conflict with a specific divine text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that a specific textual ruling overrides custom and identify the exceptional case of necessity.

Core Idea
A specific text always overrides custom – custom has no consideration when the Lawgiver has spoken directly.

The Hanafi school recognizes custom ('urf) as a subsidiary source of law when no text exists. However, when a custom directly contradicts a specific divine text (nass khas) – a clear Quranic verse or prophetic hadith that prohibits or commands a particular matter – the custom becomes invalid. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this rule, its practical application, and the sole exception of dire necessity.

Guided Path
1
Define specific text vs. general text
Identify the difference between a specific text (nass khas) that targets a particular matter and a general text (nass 'amm) that covers a broader category. The rule applies only to specific texts.
The source distinguishes: specific text forbids the custom itself; general text may encompass it but allows for specification (takhsis).
2
State the qawida and its basis
The maxim: 'When custom conflicts with a specific text, the custom has no consideration.' Understand that this is because the Shariah is binding and its texts must be implemented.
The qawida is found in the source and is a sughra (minor) maxim derived from the principle that divine commands override human practices.
3
Examine the example: adoption
Pre-Islamic custom of treating adopted children as biological was specifically prohibited in Surah al-Ahzab. The custom, though widespread, became null and void upon revelation.
This shows that the rule applies regardless of whether the custom preceded or followed the text.
4
Analyze the jurist's two options
The jurist can only follow the custom (permissible only in extreme necessity where the Lawgiver Himself permits temporary suspension) or follow the text (obligatory).
The exception of necessity is governed by the maxim 'al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat' (necessities permit the prohibited).
5
Apply the distinction between good and bad custom
Customs themselves can be good or bad, but the Shariah is the ultimate reference. When a specific text clarifies a matter, the custom's moral quality becomes irrelevant if it contradicts the text.
This prevents subjective judgments from overriding divine commands.

Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom. And what is meant by the specific text here is what comes from the Lawgiver concerning the matter that is the subject of the custom, prohibiting it specifically. As for the general text, what is meant here is that in which there is a ruling inclusive of the matter in which the custom is assumed to operate and also inclusive of other matters, such that the customary matter is one of the individuals of the text and its encompassments. 3 - Or it may oppose the ijtihadi opinions, i.e., the rulings that the jurists have established by derivation and juristic reasoning by way of analogy or istihsan or istislah in incidental issues for which there is no explicit text in the Shariah. And on this the discussion branches into four chapters: - The case of custom colliding with a specific legislative text. - The case of custom concomitant with the revelation of the general text opposing it. - The case of custom arising after the general text opposing it. - The case of conflict between custom and ijtihad. (1) Generality and specificity in the terminology of the scholars of usul al-fiqh are originally attributes of words with respect to their meanings. So the specific word is that which indicates an individual like 'man', 'woman', 'human' (indefinite), or limited individuals like number words such as three, ten, etc. And the general word is that which encompasses many unlimited individuals on the basis of inclusiveness and comprehensiveness, i.e., it includes all of them, like His statement in the Quran: 'And for Allah is the Hajj of the House upon the people, whoever is able to find a way to it' and the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi (1866) and Abu Dawood (3681) on drinks, from Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'Whatever intoxicates in large quantities, a small amount is forbidden.' So the word 'people' and the word 'whatever' are general. See al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali 2/35-36, first Amiri edition, and Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 2/2 and after. And by the generality of the wording in the text, the text and the ruling it contains are also described as general. 904. Chapter (71): Custom colliding with a specific legislative text. And we will see the details of these cases in the following chapters: The case of custom colliding with a specific legislative text. 3/71 - If people customarily engage in an action from ordinary or civil actions, and the matter they are accustomed to is prohibited and forbidden by a specific text, i.e., by a text from the Lawgiver prohibiting this matter specifically, as the pre-Islamic Arabs were accustomed to adoption, for example, and applying the ruling of true sonship to it, and the Quran forbade it specifically, then this custom at that time has no consideration and no value. So it is a rejected custom that must be changed, not confirmed, and it is not permissible to judge by it in any case, whether the custom is specific or general, and whether it arose after the revelation of the text or existed before it. And that is because when custom contradicted the specific will of the Lawgiver regarding a subject, the jurist charged with applying the Shariah is between two matters: either to act according to the custom, which is a complete neglect of the text of the Lawgiver, and that is not permissible except in cases of sudden necessity, because the Lawgiver Himself has legislated temporary neglect in cases of necessity. Or to act according to the text and ignore the custom, and this is what is obligatory to adopt. It is obvious that the Shariah is binding and was only legislated so that its texts are implemented and respected. So it is not permissible to suspend it by acting contrary to it, otherwise there would be no meaning for legislation (see: al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, Issue 14 of the fourth type of the Book of Maqasid, 2/283-284). 4/71 - And we have already stated that among customs are those that are in themselves good and just, and among them are those that are bad and unjust, and that among the purposes of the legislations that are enacted is to affirm the good and forbid the bad, and to direct people in their actions and transactions toward the public good. And the reference in distinguishing the good and just from the bad and unjust is a matter 905.
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' discusses a foundational rule in the theory of custom ('urf) within Hanafi legal theory. The author explains that when a custom contradicts a specific textual ruling (nass khas) from the Quran or Sunnah, the custom is entirely invalid and must be rejected. The specific text is defined as one that directly prohibits a particular matter, such as the Quranic prohibition of adoption (tabanni) which was a pre-Islamic custom. The passage clarifies that this applies regardless of whether the custom is widespread or limited, and whether it existed before or after the revelation of the text. The jurist has only two options: follow the custom (only permissible in cases of necessity, as the Lawgiver Himself permits temporary exemption in dire circumstances) or follow the text, which is the obligatory course. Al-Zarqa emphasizes that the Shariah is binding and its texts must be implemented; to act contrary to them would render legislation meaningless. The author also notes that customs may be good or bad, and the Shariah aims to affirm good customs and forbid bad ones, but the ultimate reference for distinguishing good from bad is the Shariah itself, not custom. This rule is a key principle in Hanafi usul: specific texts override custom absolutely.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice of people in their dealings and actions.
النص الخاص (al-nass al-khas): A divine text (Quran or Hadith) that specifically and directly prohibits or commands a particular matter.
النص العام (al-nass al-'amm): A text that covers a general category which may include the customary practice as one of its instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory of custom, if a widespread custom contradicts a specific Quranic prohibition, what is the ruling?
A) The custom may be followed if it is in the public interest.
B) The specific textual ruling must be implemented and the custom is invalid.
C) A judge may choose based on equity between the two.
D) The custom takes precedence if it was established before the text.

Key Takeaway
A specific text (nass khas) from the Quran or Sunnah overrides any conflicting custom, regardless of its prevalence or age, except in cases of extreme necessity.

Custom Does Not Redefine Sacred Texts
Warning against anachronistic interpretation of sacred texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why later juristic terms (fard, wajib, etc.) must not be read into Quran and Sunnah as their original meanings.

Core Idea
The Quran speaks in its own language; our later categories do not change it.

This passage from al-Zarqa's work warns against a common error: interpreting sacred texts using later juristic classifications as if they were the intended meanings. We explore why this is problematic and how it protects the integrity of the Lawgiver's language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Understand that terminological meanings invented by later jurists should not be used to interpret Quranic commands and prohibitions.
2
Understand the reasoning
The imperative form in the original texts inherently indicates degrees of obligation, preferability, or permission—regardless of what we call them.
3
Recognize the principle
Lughat al-shari' (the language of the Lawgiver) is primary; later technical terms do not create new boundaries.
4
Apply to an example
How would this principle affect understanding of a Quranic command like 'establish prayer'? It remains fard, but the term 'fard' is just a label.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
فالمعاني الاصطلاحية التي تعارفها الفقهاء في تقسيماتهم لضبط المسائل وترتيب الأحكام ،كالفرض والواجب والمسنون، والمندوب، والمكروه تنزيهاً أو تحريماً، وغير ذلك من الاصطلاحات العلمية المستحدثة، لا يصح تفسير نصوص القرآن والسنة بها على أنها هي هي المراد بالأوامر والنواهي. بل إن صيغة الأمر في تلك النصوص تارة تفيد الطلب المحتم للفعل، وتارة أفضليته أو إباحته والنهي يفيد عكس ذلك، ولنسمه بعد ذلك بما شئنا من الاصطلاحات فإنها لا تبدل شيئاً تلك من المدلولات الأصلية التي كانت في لغة الشارع هي المفهومة، ولا توجد فيها الاصطلاحات الحديثة حدوداً جديدة .
وكذلك لفظ «اليمين الوارد في الكتاب والسنة، فإنَّ مفهومه اليمين بالله تعالى لأنها هي المعروفة في لسان الشرع وعليها يحمل قوله تعالى : ولَا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَنِكُمْ وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا عَقَدتُمُ الْأَيْمَنَ) (۱) [سورة المائدة ٨٩/٥]
فلا يصح حمله على اليمين المستحدثة بالطلاق والعتاق، لأنها لم
(۱) اليمين هي لأفعلن كذا أو لا أفعل كذا. وهي مؤنثة اللفظ. وأما القسم (بفتحتين) فهو الحلف على توكيد خبر عن أمر كائن أو سيكون نحو والله ما فعلت كذا، أو سيكون كذا.
الحلف على فعل أو ترك في المستقبل لتوثيق العزيمة بها نحو : والله
وقد يستعمل كل من اليمين والقسم في معنى الآخر تجوزاً وتساهلاً. ويمين اللغو اختلفت الآراء الفقهية في تفسيرها وأحسن تفسير في نظرنا أنها صورة الحلف الذي يجري على لسان بعض الناس كلهجة معتادة لا يقصد بها انعقاد اليمين كقولهم في الجواب بلى والله أو نعم والله . وهذا ما أخذ به جماعة منهم الإمام
الشافعي . وقال الحنفية : هي الحلف على خبر كذب يظنه الحالف صدقاً. فإذا كان عالماً بكذبه فهي اليمين المحرمة التي تسمى الغموس» (بفتح الغين لأنها تغمس صاحبها في
الإثم.

الفصل (۷۳) العرف الحادث بعد نص عام يعارضه
تكن معروفة في الجاهلية فلا يكون اللغو فيها معفى ) (ر: رسالة «العرف والعادة للأستاذ أحمد فهمي أبي سنة ص ٩٥ نقلاً عن «الموافقات»
للشاطبي).
۲/۷۳ - وعلى هذا الأساس يجب أيضاً تطبيق نصوص الفقهاء، وتفسير صكوك الوقفيات والوصايا والبيوع والهبات والزواج وسائر الصكوك العقدية وما يرد فيها من شروط واصطلاحات، فإنها جميعاً يجب أن تفسر بحسب عرف العاقدين المنشئين لها في زمانهم قياساً على نصوص الشارع، ولا يسوغ تنزيلها في الفهم على عرف حادث يخرج به كلامهم عن مرادهم .
فلفظ الأستاذ مثلاً كان معناه العرفي رب الصناعة الذي يستخدم أجيراً
فيها لتعليمه إياها (ر: كتاب «المُعَرَّب» للجوالقي، كلمة «أستاذ»). وبناء على هذا العرف نصَّ الفقهاء على أنه : إذا دفع الشخص ولده الصغير إلى أستاذ ثم اختلفا في استحقاق الأجرة فطلب الأستاذ من الوالد أجراً عن الولد، وطلب الوالد من الأستاذ أجراً عنه، فمن يستحق الأجر منهما على الآخر؟
قالوا: يلجأ إلى تحكيم العرف فإذا كان عرف البلدة أن يأخذ الأستاذ أجراً أو أن يدفع اتبع العرف.

(۱) بل الطلاق والعتاق مما يعتبر فيه الهزل كالجد كما تقدم (ر: ف ٦/٣٣).
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
So the terminological meanings that jurists have conventionally adopted in their classifications for organizing issues and arranging rulings, such as fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, makruh tanzihan or tahriman, and other newly invented technical terms, it is not correct to interpret the texts of the Quran and Sunnah by them as if they are exactly what is meant by commands and prohibitions. Rather, the imperative form in those texts sometimes indicates a compulsory demand for action, sometimes its preferability or permissibility, and the prohibition indicates the opposite. We may subsequently call it whatever we wish of terms, but they do not change anything of those original meanings that were understood in the language of the Lawgiver, and the modern terms do not create new boundaries therein.
Likewise, the word "yamin" (oath) occurring in the Book and Sunnah: its meaning is the oath by Allah the Exalted, because that is what is known in the language of the Law, and upon it is carried His statement: "Allah will not take you to account for thoughtlessness in your oaths, but He will take you to account for what you have contracted in oaths" [Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89]. So it is not correct to apply it to the newly invented oath by divorce or emancipation, because they were not known in the pre-Islamic period, so thoughtlessness therein is not excused. (Ref: Treatise "al-Urf wa al-Adah" by Professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sinnah, p. 95, quoting from "al-Muwafaqat" by al-Shatibi).
2/73 - And on this basis, the texts of the jurists must also be applied, and the interpretation of endowment deeds, wills, sales, gifts, marriage, and all contractual deeds and the conditions and terms contained therein: all of them must be interpreted according to the custom of the contracting parties who created them in their time, by analogy with the texts of the Lawgiver, and it is not permissible to understand them according to a new custom that would remove their words from their intended meaning.
For example, the word "ustadh" (master) originally meant, customarily, the owner of a craft who employs a worker to teach him the craft. Based on this custom, the jurists ruled: If a person entrusts his young son to a master (ustadh) and then they disagree about entitlement to wages, the master demanding wages from the father for the child, and the father demanding wages from the master for the child, who is entitled to wages from the other? They said: Recourse is had to determining the custom. If the custom of the town is that the master takes wages or pays wages, the custom is followed. (Ref: al-Majallah, Article 569; and al-Qawaid al-Fiqhiyyah by al-Hamzawi, in issues of ijarah, p. 76).
In our present era, the name "ustadh" has come to mean teacher of sciences, so it is not correct to apply that juristic text to this new meaning, because that detailed ruling does not apply to it, since the master of the craft teaches the child through employing him in it.
(1) Rather, divorce and emancipation are such that jest is considered serious as previously mentioned (ref: F 6/33).
[Footnote at 862]: (1) Al-yamin is "I will do such-and-such" or "I will not do such-and-such." It is feminine in word form. As for al-qasm (with two fatha), it is swearing to confirm news about something that is or will be, e.g., "By Allah, I did not do such-and-such" or "It will be such-and-such." Swearing to do or leave something in the future to strengthen resolve, e.g., "By Allah, I will do." Each of yamin and qasm may be used in the sense of the other as a license and leniency. As for yamin al-laghw (vain oath), juristic opinions differed on its interpretation. The best interpretation in our view is that it is the form of swearing that occurs on the tongue of some people as a customary expression without intending the binding of an oath, like saying in reply "Certainly, by Allah" or "Yes, by Allah." This is what a group adopted, including Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafis said: It is swearing to a false report that the swearer believes to be true. If he knows it is false, then it is the forbidden oath called "al-ghamus" (with ghayn fatha) because it immerses its doer in sin.
Source pages p. 862

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses the role of 'urf (custom) in legal interpretation, a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by warning against interpreting Quranic and Sunnah texts using later juristic technical terms (like fard, wajib, etc.) as if those terms were the original intended meanings. Instead, these texts carry inherent meanings from the language of the Lawgiver (lughat al-shari'), and subsequent terminology does not alter that. He then applies this to the word 'yamin' (oath) in the Quran: it primarily means an oath by Allah, as was known in early Islamic and pre-Islamic usage. Therefore, new types of oaths (like by divorce or emancipation) cannot be subsumed under Quranic rulings on oaths (e.g., expiation for broken oaths) because they were unknown at the time. The footnote clarifies the definition of 'yamin' and 'qasm', and discusses the dispute over 'yamin al-laghw' (vain oath): the preferred view (taken by al-Shafi'i and others) is that it refers to casual swearing without intent to bind, while the Hanafi view is that it is swearing to a falsehood believed true. The author then shifts to the interpretation of legal documents (contracts, endowments, etc.). These must be interpreted according to the custom of the parties at the time of creation, not according to later customs. He gives the classic Hanafi example: the word 'ustadh' originally meant a master craftsman taking an apprentice. Jurists ruled on wage disputes based on local custom. Today 'ustadh' means teacher, so that specific ruling cannot be applied to the modern context. The footnote adds that divorce and emancipation are exceptions: jest is treated as serious (a known Hanafi principle). Overall, the passage teaches that custom is a valid interpretive tool but must be applied with historical awareness.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice that influences legal interpretation and rulings.
صيغة الأمر (sighah al-amr): The imperative form in Arabic, used in legal texts to convey commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between later juristic terms (e.g., fard, wajib) and the original Quranic commands?
A) The later terms replace the original meanings.
B) The later terms are the same as the original meanings.
C) The later terms are labels that do not change the original meanings.
D) The later terms are more important than the original meanings.

Key Takeaway
Sacred texts must be interpreted by the language of the Lawgiver, not by later juristic categories.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Custom of the Lawgiver: Defining Yamin
Application of custom to define a Quranic term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quranic term 'yamin' means oath by Allah and evaluate the dispute over yamin al-laghw from a Hanafi perspective.

Core Idea
An oath by Allah has a specific legal effect; new oaths like by divorce are not covered by Quranic rules.

The passage uses the example of 'yamin' to illustrate how custom determines meaning. The Quran speaks of oaths as those by Allah; later invented oaths do not fall under the same rulings. The footnote discusses the controversial definition of 'yamin al-laghw', where we see a difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Understand that 'yamin' in Quran and Sunnah means oath by Allah because that was the known custom.
2
Support from the verse
Verse 5:89 implies oaths by Allah; thoughtless oaths (laghw) are excused.
3
Understand the implication
Oaths by divorce or emancipation are not covered because they were not known in pre-Islamic or early Islamic times.
4
Analyze the dispute on yamin al-laghw
Hanafis define it as swearing to a falsehood believed true; Shafi'is as casual swearing without intent. The author prefers the Shafi'i view.
5
Evaluate the practical difference
Under the Hanafi definition, no expiation is required for yamin al-laghw; under the Shafi'i definition, also no expiation but the category is broader or narrower.

(۱) بل الطلاق والعتاق مما يعتبر فيه الهزل كالجد كما تقدم (ر: ف ٦/٣٣).
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
So the terminological meanings that jurists have conventionally adopted in their classifications for organizing issues and arranging rulings, such as fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, makruh tanzihan or tahriman, and other newly invented technical terms, it is not correct to interpret the texts of the Quran and Sunnah by them as if they are exactly what is meant by commands and prohibitions. Rather, the imperative form in those texts sometimes indicates a compulsory demand for action, sometimes its preferability or permissibility, and the prohibition indicates the opposite. We may subsequently call it whatever we wish of terms, but they do not change anything of those original meanings that were understood in the language of the Lawgiver, and the modern terms do not create new boundaries therein.

So it is not correct to apply it to the newly invented oath by divorce or emancipation, because they were not known in the pre-Islamic period, so thoughtlessness therein is not excused. (Ref: Treatise "al-Urf wa al-Adah" by Professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sinnah, p. 95, quoting from "al-Muwafaqat" by al-Shatibi).
2/73 - And on this basis, the texts of the jurists must also be applied, and the interpretation of endowment deeds, wills, sales, gifts, marriage, and all contractual deeds and the conditions and terms contained therein: all of them must be interpreted according to the custom of the contracting parties who created them in their time, by analogy with the texts of the Lawgiver, and it is not permissible to understand them according to a new custom that would remove their words from their intended meaning.
For example, the word "ustadh" (master) originally meant, customarily, the owner of a craft who employs a worker to teach him the craft. Based on this custom, the jurists ruled: If a person entrusts his young son to a master (ustadh) and then they disagree about entitlement to wages, the master demanding wages from the father for the child, and the father demanding wages from the master for the child, who is entitled to wages from the other? They said: Recourse is had to determining the custom. If the custom of the town is that the master takes wages or pays wages, the custom is followed. (Ref: al-Majallah, Article 569; and al-Qawaid al-Fiqhiyyah by al-Hamzawi, in issues of ijarah, p. 76).
In our present era, the name "ustadh" has come to mean teacher of sciences, so it is not correct to apply that juristic text to this new meaning, because that detailed ruling does not apply to it, since the master of the craft teaches the child through employing him in it.
(1) Rather, divorce and emancipation are such that jest is considered serious as previously mentioned (ref: F 6/33).
[Footnote at 862]: (1) Al-yamin is "I will do such-and-such" or "I will not do such-and-such." It is feminine in word form. As for al-qasm (with two fatha), it is swearing to confirm news about something that is or will be, e.g., "By Allah, I did not do such-and-such" or "It will be such-and-such." Swearing to do or leave something in the future to strengthen resolve, e.g., "By Allah, I will do." Each of yamin and qasm may be used in the sense of the other as a license and leniency. As for yamin al-laghw (vain oath), juristic opinions differed on its interpretation. The best interpretation in our view is that it is the form of swearing that occurs on the tongue of some people as a customary expression without intending the binding of an oath, like saying in reply "Certainly, by Allah" or "Yes, by Allah." This is what a group adopted, including Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafis said: It is swearing to a false report that the swearer believes to be true. If he knows it is false, then it is the forbidden oath called "al-ghamus" (with ghayn fatha) because it immerses its doer in sin.
Source pages p. 862

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work discusses the role of 'urf (custom) in legal interpretation, a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by warning against interpreting Quranic and Sunnah texts using later juristic technical terms (like fard, wajib, etc.) as if those terms were the original intended meanings. Instead, these texts carry inherent meanings from the language of the Lawgiver (lughat al-shari'), and subsequent terminology does not alter that. He then applies this to the word 'yamin' (oath) in the Quran: it primarily means an oath by Allah, as was known in early Islamic and pre-Islamic usage. Therefore, new types of oaths (like by divorce or emancipation) cannot be subsumed under Quranic rulings on oaths (e.g., expiation for broken oaths) because they were unknown at the time. The footnote clarifies the definition of 'yamin' and 'qasm', and discusses the dispute over 'yamin al-laghw' (vain oath): the preferred view (taken by al-Shafi'i and others) is that it refers to casual swearing without intent to bind, while the Hanafi view is that it is swearing to a falsehood believed true. The author then shifts to the interpretation of legal documents (contracts, endowments, etc.). These must be interpreted according to the custom of the parties at the time of creation, not according to later customs. He gives the classic Hanafi example: the word 'ustadh' originally meant a master craftsman taking an apprentice. Jurists ruled on wage disputes based on local custom. Today 'ustadh' means teacher, so that specific ruling cannot be applied to the modern context. The footnote adds that divorce and emancipation are exceptions: jest is treated as serious (a known Hanafi principle). Overall, the passage teaches that custom is a valid interpretive tool but must be applied with historical awareness.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, originally by Allah, used to affirm or commit.
يمين اللغو (yamin al-laghw): A vain or thoughtless oath that incurs no expiation.
الغموس (al-ghamus): An oath sworn knowing it is false; a major sin.

D) The Hanbali definition.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic term 'yamin' refers to oaths by Allah; later invented oaths are governed by different rules.

Interpreting Contracts by Custom: The Ustadh Example
Application of custom to contract interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that contracts and legal documents are interpreted according to the custom of the parties at the time of creation.

Core Idea
Words in contracts mean what the parties intended by their own custom, not later usage.

The passage extends the interpretive principle to human documents: endowments, wills, sales, etc. The case of 'ustadh' shows how a word's meaning changes over time and how jurists handle wage disputes by referring to local custom. Understanding this prevents misapplication of classical rulings to modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Restate the general rule
Documents are interpreted by the custom of the contracting parties at their time, by analogy with sacred texts.
2
Examine the example
'Ustadh' originally meant master craftsman; jurists ruled on wage disputes based on that custom.
3
Understand the ruling
If father and master dispute wages, local custom determines who pays whom.
4
Recognize the caveat
Later custom cannot reinterpret an old document; the original meaning is fixed.
5
Apply to a modern case
A contract from 1900 using 'ustadh' as teacher cannot be reinterpreted as master craftsman by today's custom.

Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
So the terminological meanings that jurists have conventionally adopted in their classifications for organizing issues and arranging rulings, such as fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, makruh tanzihan or tahriman, and other newly invented technical terms, it is not correct to interpret the texts of the Quran and Sunnah by them as if they are exactly what is meant by commands and prohibitions. Rather, the imperative form in those texts sometimes indicates a compulsory demand for action, sometimes its preferability or permissibility, and the prohibition indicates the opposite. We may subsequently call it whatever we wish of terms, but they do not change anything of those original meanings that were understood in the language of the Lawgiver, and the modern terms do not create new boundaries therein.
Likewise, the word "yamin" (oath) occurring in the Book and Sunnah: its meaning is the oath by Allah the Exalted, because that is what is known in the language of the Law, and upon it is carried His statement: "Allah will not take you to account for thoughtlessness in your oaths, but He will take you to account for what you have contracted in oaths" [Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89]. So it is not correct to apply it to the newly invented oath by divorce or emancipation, because they were not known in the pre-Islamic period, so thoughtlessness therein is not excused. (Ref: Treatise "al-Urf wa al-Adah" by Professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sinnah, p. 95, quoting from "al-Muwafaqat" by al-Shatibi).
2/73 - And on this basis, the texts of the jurists must also be applied, and the interpretation of endowment deeds, wills, sales, gifts, marriage, and all contractual deeds and the conditions and terms contained therein: all of them must be interpreted according to the custom of the contracting parties who created them in their time, by analogy with the texts of the Lawgiver, and it is not permissible to understand them according to a new custom that would remove their words from their intended meaning.
For example, the word "ustadh" (master) originally meant, customarily, the owner of a craft who employs a worker to teach him the craft. Based on this custom, the jurists ruled: If a person entrusts his young son to a master (ustadh) and then they disagree about entitlement to wages, the master demanding wages from the father for the child, and the father demanding wages from the master for the child, who is entitled to wages from the other? They said: Recourse is had to determining the custom. If the custom of the town is that the master takes wages or pays wages, the custom is followed. (Ref: al-Majallah, Article 569; and al-Qawaid al-Fiqhiyyah by al-Hamzawi, in issues of ijarah, p. 76).
In our present era, the name "ustadh" has come to mean teacher of sciences, so it is not correct to apply that juristic text to this new meaning, because that detailed ruling does not apply to it, since the master of the craft teaches the child through employing him in it.
(1) Rather, divorce and emancipation are such that jest is considered serious as previously mentioned (ref: F 6/33).

Legal Philosophy and Theory

10 min
The Rule on Emergent Custom vs. Prior Text: Two Exceptions
Explaining the Hanafi principle on new custom vs. prior text and its two exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that an emergent custom cannot specify a prior general text, and identify the two exceptions where it is considered.

Core Idea
A new custom cannot overturn a text unless the text was built on that custom or its cause is removed.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi juristic principle: when a new custom (al-'urf al-tari') arises after a general legislative text, does it have any power to restrict or modify that text? The default rule is that it does not. However, in ijtihad and fatwa, two exceptional cases allow the custom to be respected: when the text itself was based on a prior custom, and when the text's underlying cause ('illa) is negated by the new custom. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for muftis navigating changing social norms.

Guided Path
1
State the Default Principle
Ibn Nujaym's maxim: only the custom concurrent with the text's revelation governs interpretation; later customs have no authority. Thus, the general text remains applicable despite new customs.
This is the foundational rule: 'Al-'urf al-muqarin al-sabiq mu'tabar, al-muta'akhir ghayr mu'tabar.'
2
Identify the Two Exceptions
The passage lists two cases where emergent custom is considered: (1) when the text itself is 'based on' a prior custom (Abu Yusuf's view), and (2) when the text's 'illa is negated by the new custom. Both rely on the idea that the ruling follows its cause.
The first exception is unique to Abu Yusuf among the early Hanafis.
3
Understand the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
The majority of Hanafis do not accept the first exception; they hold that even a custom-based text remains fixed. The author (Zarqa) favors Abu Yusuf's view as stronger in reasoning.
This dispute is a valid ikhtilaf within the school, not a departure from Hanafi methodology.
4
Apply to the Example: Corrupt Conditions in Sales
The passage begins an example: stipulating the seller to grind wheat or transport goods was considered a corrupt condition (shart mufsid) because it added a benefit outside the contract. However, if emergent custom makes such services standard, the ruling may change under the exceptions.
This illustrates how custom can remove the cause of prohibition.
5
Relate to Other Qawaid
This qawida interacts with 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative) and 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease). The current qawida sets a limit: custom can only override a text under the two conditions.
These related qawaid help complete the picture for a mufti weighing custom and text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. So one benefits from the work of the child, and thus the custom of people differs in taking and giving wages according to the need of the craft for the hired worker and people's desire to learn it, and its inclusion of secrets that the craftsman does not understand except by explanation and special training. And this does not occur with the professor-teacher of sciences, for teaching is always a service from the teacher to the learner. Its text: Ibn Nujaym said in Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir under the sixth principle, 1/133: 'The custom upon which words are interpreted is only the concurrent prior one, not the later one; therefore they said: no consideration is given to the emergent custom.' And you have known that the non-consideration of the emergent custom in specifying the meanings of texts and restricting them is not limited to verbal custom; rather, the emergent practical custom also does not have this authority against the text, even if it is a general custom, unless the text is based on custom or based on a cause that the custom removes, as we will soon see (4/73). 3/73 - This is the established jurisprudential principle when custom conflicts with general legislative text, summarized as: (a) If the custom existed at the time of the text, then general custom in Hanafi ijtihad has authority to specify the text and restricts its ruling to other than the customary matter; specific custom does not have such authority except according to a weak and preponderated opinion. (b) If the custom is emergent, it is not valid to specify the prior general text; rather, consideration is given to the generality of the text, not the custom, even if the custom is general. 4/73 - The custom that removes the cause of the opposing text is considered even if emergent. However, following the jurisprudential branches and examining their rationales that the jurists use clearly indicates that the emergent custom, even if it contradicts the apparent meaning of the legislative text, is considered and respected in two cases: (1) If the legislative text itself is based on custom, i.e., built upon a practical custom existing at its revelation; then if that custom changes, the ruling of the text changes accordingly, even if the text is specific to the subject. This view is unique to Abu Yusuf among the companions of Abu Hanifa, and it is the strongest view in reasoning and proof, although the majority disagree. This case has been previously explained with examples in the discussion of the clash of custom with specific text, so refer to it (7/71-8). (2) If the legislative text is based on a cause that the emergent custom negates, whether the cause is expressed in the text or derived by ijtihad. In such a case, the emergent custom is considered and respected even if it contradicts the text, because this contradiction becomes apparent and not real as long as the cause of the text is negated by the existence of the custom, since it is established in the principles of usul that the ruling of Sharia revolves around its cause: it exists when the cause exists and is negated when the cause is negated. 5/73 - Jurisprudential examples of this principle: There are many jurisprudential examples of this principle in considering emergent custom; we present some of them below. 6/73 - First example: Like sale. The Hanafi jurists mention in the discussion of the corrupt condition in contracts of exchange that among the conditions that corrupt a contract of exchange is every condition in which a benefit is stipulated that is outside the original ruling of the contract and what is suitable to it, such as the buyer stipulating on the seller to grind the purchased wheat, or to carry the goods at his expense, or to repair the tool.
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the theory of custom from a Hanafi perspective, specifically addressing the interaction between emergent (new) custom and existing legislative texts. The author begins by citing Ibn Nujaym's maxim that only the custom concurrent with the text's revelation governs interpretation, not later customs. Then he lays out the general principle: if a custom was present when the text was revealed, it can specify the text's general meaning; but if the custom arises later, it cannot specify the text. However, two exceptions are given where an emergent custom is considered: first, if the text itself is based on that custom (e.g., the ruling was built on a pre-existing practice), and second, if the text's underlying cause is negated by the new custom. The first exception is attributed uniquely to Abu Yusuf among the early Hanafis, and the author notes that this position is the stronger one in reasoning. The passage then begins providing examples, starting with conditions that invalidate a sale contract, such as requiring the seller to grind wheat or transport goods, which were considered corrupt unless custom allowed them. This illustrates how changes in custom can remove the cause of a prohibition. The commentary stays strictly within the text's content, explaining the legal reasoning without adding outside material.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom, both verbal and practical; here mainly practical custom that is prevalent among people.
النص التشريعي (al-nass al-tashri'i): A legislative text from the Qur'an or Sunnah, or a general ruling derived from them.
العلة (al-'illa): The underlying cause or rationale behind a ruling; if it is negated by new custom, the ruling may change.
العرف الطارىء (al-'urf al-tari'): An emergent custom that arises after the revelation of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi theory presented, in which of the following cases can an emergent custom be considered even if it conflicts with a general legislative text?
A) When the custom is widely practiced by the majority of Muslims.
B) When the text was based on a custom that has changed, according to Abu Yusuf.
C) When the custom is verbal rather than practical.
D) When the text is from the Quran and the custom is universal.

Key Takeaway
An emergent custom can override a prior text only if the text was based on custom or its underlying cause is negated by the custom.

How a New Custom Alters a Text's Ruling by Removing Its 'Illa
Explaining that a new custom opposing a general text does not specify it but removes its 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a new custom does not specify or abrogate a general text but instead removes its 'illa, and apply this principle to contemporary issues of currency and loans.

Core Idea
A new custom cannot override the text itself, but it can negate the underlying cause ('illa), causing the ruling to change.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am tackles a subtle yet critical distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when a new custom appears to conflict with a general textual ruling, the correct response is not to see the custom as specifying (takhsis) or abrogating (naskh) the text, but rather to reconsider whether the 'illa (the ratio legis) of that ruling still exists. If the custom shows that the 'illa is no longer operative, then the ruling falls away naturally. This principle bridges the stability of revealed texts with the dynamism of human custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text and its 'illa
Determine the general ruling from the Quran or Sunnah and the effective cause ('illa) that gives it legal force. For instance, the prohibition of riba in weight-based currencies is based on the 'illa of potential unjust enrichment from unequal exchange.
2
Observe the new custom
Study the contemporary custom that appears to contradict the literal implication of the text. For example, people in a region treat different weights of the same currency as equal in value.
3
Distinguish between opposing the text and opposing the 'illa
The custom does not directly contradict the words of the text, but rather renders its 'illa irrelevant. The text remains valid, but its applicability changes because the cause is no longer present.
This is not naskh (abrogation) but a change in the ruling's applicability.
4
Examine the mechanism in Hanafi fiqh
When the 'illa disappears, the ruling ceases to apply, even if the text remains. This is based on the view of Abu Yusuf that the ruling of a text revolves with custom.
5
Apply to examples from the passage
Apply the principle to the examples: (1) different weights of coins with equal value, where custom ignores small weight differences, and (2) borrowing bread by number among neighbors, where custom allows it despite potential weight differences.

الفصل (73) العرف الحادث بعد نص عام يعارضه

على النص، بل موافقاً لغرضه وروحه ولو كان عرفاً حادثاً (1) (ر: العناية شرح الهداية باب البيع الفاسد (77/6 - 76).

ولم يوجبوا لتصحيح الشرط المتعارف أن يكون العرف فيه عاماً، بل يكفي العرف الخاص، فإنَّ الشروط الممكنة متنوعة أنواعاً لا تحصى، وهي في كل مكان وزمان بحسب حاجات أهله فقد يتعارف في بلد من الشروط العقدية ما لا يتعارف في غيره فيصح من كل قوم أن يشترطوا في بيوعهم وسائر معاوضاتهم ما يتعارفونه من الشروط مالم يوجد نص خاص يمنع شرطاً مخصوصاً بعينه فحينئذ لا يعتبر عرف على خلافه، كما يعلم مما تقدم في بحث تصادم العرف مع النص الخاص.

8/73 - الشاهد الثاني:

في قضية تفاوت وزن الدنانير قرَّر العلامة المحقق ابن عابدين في رسالته «نشر العرف» أنه إذا تعددت أصناف النوع الواحد من النقود، وكان أحدها أثقل والآخر أخف كالدينار المحمودي الجهادي والدينار العدلي، وكأصناف الريال الفرنجي في عصره، فإن هذا الفرق في الوزن - وإن كان ظاهراً في جميع الموازين - يجوز معه دفع الأثقل بدلاً من الأخف، أو العكس في المداينات والقروض والعقود ولا يكون ذلك ربا محرماً ما دامت القيمة واحدة(1)، وقد علل ذلك بأن الناس لا ينظرون إلى هذا التفاوت في الوزن ما لم تختلف به القيمة، وقد ركز هذا العرف في عقولهم من عالم وجاهل فيتعين الإفتاء به تخريجاً على رأي أبي يوسف في النص المعلل بالعرف (2) .

ومعنى هذا أنَّ العرف بين الناس على إهدار فرق الوزن وعلى اتحاد القيمة قد نفى علة النص الذي يعتبر الزيادة رباً محرماً، فصار كالنص المعلل بالعُرْف في رأي أبي يوسف يتبدل حكمه تبعاً لتبدل العرف (ر) رسالة نشر العرف» في مجموعة رسائل ابن عابدین (119/2).
