9/73 - الشاهد الثالث:

يصرح أيضاً فقهاء الحنفية في بابي الربا والقرض من كتاب البيوع بأنه يجوز اقتراض الخبز عدداً بين الجيران، لأنَّ ذلك أصبح متعارفاً بينهم للحاجة إليه وهذا رأي الإمام محمد صاحب أبي حنيفة، وهو الراجح المفتي به في المذهب فيجوز ذلك ولو اختلف رغيف القرض عن رغيف ...

(1) تقدمت الإشارة إلى هذه المسألة في بحث الاستحسان (ف 6/4 في الحاشية). (2) فإن أبا يوسف يقول عندئذ بأن حكم النص يدور مع العرف ويتبدل بتبدله كما تقدم ر ف 8/71 - 4/73).
Chapter (73): The custom that occurs after a general text that opposes it... [The custom is] not contrary to the text, but rather in agreement with its purpose and spirit, even if it is a newly arisen custom (1) (Refer to: al-/Inayah Sharh al-Hidayah, chapter of invalid sale (6/76-77).

And they [the Hanafi jurists] did not require for the validity of a customary condition that the custom be general; rather, a special custom suffices. For possible conditions are of countless types, and they vary according to the needs of the people of each time and place. Thus, in one country, conditions in contracts may become customary that are not customary elsewhere. So it is valid for each group to stipulate in their sales and other exchanges whatever conditions they customarily practice, as long as there is no specific text that prohibits a particular condition. In that case, a custom contrary to it is not considered, as is known from the earlier discussion on the conflict of custom with a specific text.

Refer also to: al-/Inayah and Fath al-Qadir, at the same location, and Radd al-Muhtar (4/121-123).

8/73 - The Second Witness:

Concerning the issue of differences in weight of dinars, the verifying scholar Ibn /Abidin decided in his epistle 'Nashr al-/Urf' that if there are multiple types of the same kind of currency, some heavier and some lighter, such as the Mahmudi Jihadi dinar and the /Adli dinar, and like the types of the French riyal in his time, then this difference in weight, even if apparent on all scales, it is permissible to pay the heavier instead of the lighter, or vice versa, in debts, loans, and contracts, and it is not prohibited usury as long as the value is the same (1). And this was reasoned by the fact that people do not pay attention to this difference in weight unless the value differs. This custom has become ingrained in the minds of both scholar and layman, so it is necessary to give fatwa accordingly, based on the opinion of Abu Yusuf concerning a text that is reasoned by custom (2).

And this means that the custom among people of disregarding the difference in weight and considering the value as one has negated the cause of the text that considers the excess as prohibited usury. Thus, it becomes like a text reasoned by custom in the view of Abu Yusuf: its ruling changes according to the change of custom (Refer to: Risalah Nashr al-/Urf in Majmu/ah Rasa'il Ibn /Abidin (2/119).

And if the newly arisen custom that removes the cause of the ruling has this effect on a general or specific text, as in these two preceding witnesses, then by even greater reason it has the same effect on analogical rulings that are based on qiyas, as will be clear from the following third juridical witness:

9/73 - The Third Witness:

The Hanafi jurists explicitly state in the chapters on usury and loans from the Book of Sales that it is permissible to borrow bread by number among neighbors, because this has become customary among them due to necessity. And this is the opinion of Imam Muhammad, the companion of Abu Hanifah, and it is the preponderant and mufta bihi opinion in the school. So it is permissible even if the loaf borrowed differs from the loaf...

(1) Reference was made to this issue in the discussion of istihsan (Section 4/6 in the footnote). (2) For Abu Yusuf says then that the ruling of the text revolves with custom and changes with its change, as previously referenced in Sections 8/71-73/4).
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on 'Urf (Custom) in Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-/Am addresses a crucial theoretical point: how should we understand the interaction between a general textual ruling (nass /amm) and a new custom that appears to contradict it? The author clarifies that the new custom does not actually specify (takhsis) or abrogate (naskh) the text, because specification must be contemporaneous with the text's revelation. Instead, the custom indicates that the underlying cause ('illa) of the ruling has ceased to exist. The ruling is tied to its 'illa; when the 'illa disappears due to changed circumstances and custom, the ruling changes accordingly. This is why the Hanafi school permits customary conditions in contracts even if they were not originally mentioned in the texts, as long as no specific text prohibits them. The author then provides two examples (shawahid) to illustrate. The second example, from Ibn /Abidin, concerns differences in weight among coins of the same value. Though a strict textual ruling would consider any excess in weight as riba, the custom of ignoring small weight differences negates the 'illa (the rationale that such excess constitutes unjust gain). Thus, paying a heavier coin for a lighter one of equal value is permissible. The third example, from Imam Muhammad, allows borrowing bread by number among neighbors, even if loaves differ in weight, because custom has made this practice acceptable. The author concludes that if a new custom can affect a general or specific text, it applies with even greater force to rulings derived by analogy (qiyas), since analogical rulings are more contingent on changing circumstances.

Key Terms
عرف ('urf): Custom: established and recurrent practice among people, whether general or specific, that is considered a source of law in the absence of explicit textual prohibition.
علة ('illa): The effective cause or ratio legis of a legal ruling; the attribute that is the basis for the ruling in qiyas or textual reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of how a new custom can affect a general text from the Shari'ah?
A) The new custom specifies the general text by limiting its application.
B) The new custom abrogates the general text if it contradicts it.
C) The new custom removes the 'illa (effective cause) of the general text, thus changing its ruling.
D) The new custom has no effect on a general text because it came later.

Key Takeaway
A new custom does not override the text itself but can remove the 'illa (effective cause) of the ruling, causing the ruling to change while the text remains intact.

Interaction of New Custom with General Texts: A Hanafi Principle
Explaining how a new custom can override the 'illa of a general text without abrogating it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida on interaction of new custom with general texts to determine when a custom overrides a textual ruling.

Core Idea
A new custom overrides a general text only if it negates the text's effective cause; otherwise it's abrogation.

New customs often emerge that seem to conflict with established textual rulings. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced approach: rather than discarding the text or blindly following it, we examine whether the custom negates the effective cause ('illa) behind the ruling. If it does, the ruling changes without abrogating the text. This lesson unpacks that principle through key examples from Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual ruling and its 'illa
Determine the general text (e.g., prohibition of riba in weighed items) and the 'illa that triggers the prohibition (e.g., inequality in weight).
The 'illa must be explicit or derived from the text's reasoning.
2
Examine the custom: does it negate the 'illa?
Analyze whether the new custom effectively removes the 'illa in practice. For example, if people disregard weight differences and focus on value, the 'illa of inequality is absent.
Custom must be widespread and consistent among both scholars and laymen.
3
Apply the qawida
If the 'illa is negated, the custom is legally respected and the ruling changes. If not, the text's generality prevails and custom cannot override.
This is not abrogation (naskh) but a change in application due to change in cause.
4
Verify with Hanafi furuat
Test the principle against established examples: coin exchange (Abu Yusuf) and bread borrowing (Imam Muhammad). Ensure the reasoning aligns.
These examples are mufta bihi in the madhhab.
5
Recognize the limits
If the 'illa remains intact despite the custom, the custom cannot override the text. This would be naskh, which custom cannot accomplish.
Refer to the istithna' in the qawida.

أحدها أثقل والآخر أخف كالدينار المحمودي الجهادي والدينار العدلي، وكأصناف الريال الفرنجي في عصره، فإن هذا الفرق في الوزن - وإن كان ظاهراً في جميع الموازين - يجوز معه دفع الأثقل بدلاً من الأخف، أو العكس في المداينات والقروض والعقود ولا يكون ذلك ربا محرماً ما دامت القيمة واحدة، وقد علل ذلك بأن الناس لا ينظرون إلى هذا التفاوت في الوزن ما لم تختلف به القيمة، وقد ركز هذا العرف في عقولهم من عالم وجاهل فيتعين الإفتاء به تخريجاً على رأي أبي يوسف في النص المعلل بالعرف.
ومعنى هذا أنَّ العرف بين الناس على إهدار فرق الوزن وعلى اتحاد القيمة قد نفى علة النص الذي يعتبر الزيادة رباً محرماً، فصار كالنص المعلل بالعُرْف في رأي أبي يوسف يتبدل حكمه تبعاً لتبدل العرف.

٩/٧٣ - الشاهد الثالث :
يصرح أيضاً فقهاء الحنفية في بابي الربا والقرض من كتاب البيوع بأنه يجوز اقتراض الخبز عدداً بين الجيران، لأنَّ ذلك أصبح متعارفاً بينهم للحاجة إليه وهذا رأي الإمام محمد صاحب أبي حنيفة، وهو الراجح المفتي به في المذهب فيجوز ذلك ولو اختلف رغيف القرض عن رغيف الآخر الوفاء وزناً، مع أنَّ الخبز مال ربوي من صنف الموزونات. ونصوص الشريعة توجب التساوي في مبادلة الأموال الربوية بجنسها وزناً في الموزونات وكيلاً في المكيلات، وتعتبر الفضل الزائد في أحدها عن رباً محرماً وتستلزم اعتبار عقده باطلاً أو فاسداً، وذلك في الأشياء الستة المتقدمة في الكلام على النص العرفي، وقاس الفقهاء على تلك الأشياء الستة ما يشبهها في الوصف والعلة كالمطعومات أو كل مَالَيْن يجمع بينهما صفتان مشتركتان هما وحدة الجنس ووحدة المقياس، كالخبز وسائر المعادن الأخرى غير الذهب والفضة. واعتبروا أن شبهة الربا كتحققه في الحرمة والمنع باتفاق الفقهاء.
وهذا التجويز لاقتراض الخبز عدداً بين الجيران دون نظر إلى تفاوت الوزن قد استند فيه الإمام محمد إلى العرف الشائع محتجاً بأنه قد تعارف الجيران إهدار فرق الوزن واعتبار العدد فقط.
وبذلك أصبح هذا التعامل غير صالح لأن يتخذ طريقاً للمراباة الممنوعة شرعاً، فكانت علة النصوص العامة في تحريم الربا (وهي الاسترباح غير متحققة بهذا الطريق، والحكم يدور مع علته. ولا يوجد نص خاص يمنع اقتراض الخبز عدداً بين الجيران على سبيل التخصيص والتعيين ليكون هذا العرف مصادماً للنص الصريح، فأصبح المقياس المنظور علة النصوص العامة في منع المراباة وهذه العلة لا تتحقق في هذا العرف بين الجيران حيث لا يعتبرون فرق الوزن بين الأرغفة.
١٠/٧٣ - هذه طائفة من الشواهد الفقهية المقرّرة تنطق هي وتعليلاتها المنقولة عن الفقهاء بأنّ العرف إذا كانت تزول وتنتفي به علة النص المعارض يحترم شرعاً ولو كان عرفاً حادثاً.
أما إذا كانت علة النص المعارض لا ينفيها العرف الحادث، فلا عبرة لهذا العرف تجاه عموم النص، لأنَّ اعتبار العرف الحادث عندئذ يكون نسخاً للنص والعرف ليس له هذا السلطان.
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
One of them is heavier and the other lighter, like the Mahmudi Jihadi dinar and the Adli dinar, and like the types of the French rival in its era. This difference in weight—even if apparent in all scales—it is permissible to pay the heavier instead of the lighter, or vice versa, in debts, loans, and contracts, and it is not prohibited usury as long as the value is one. This was reasoned that people do not look at this disparity in weight unless the value differs by it, and this custom has been ingrained in the minds of both scholar and layman, so it is incumbent to issue fatwa accordingly, based on the opinion of Abu Yusuf regarding a text that is reasoned by custom.
The meaning is that the custom among people of disregarding the weight difference and treating the value as one has negated the effective cause of the text that considers the excess prohibited usury. Thus it became like a text reasoned by custom in the view of Abu Yusuf: its ruling changes with the change of custom.
If the new custom that removes the cause of the ruling has this effect towards a general or specific text, as in these two preceding examples, then a fortiori it has the same effect towards analogical rulings that are based on qiyas, as becomes clear from the following third fiqhi example:
9/73 - The Third Example:
The Hanafi jurists also state clearly in the chapters of usury and loans from the Book of Sales that it is permissible to borrow bread by count among neighbors because this has become customary among them due to need. This is the opinion of Imam Muhammad, the companion of Abu Hanifa, and it is the preponderant and mufta bihi view in the madhhab. Thus it is permissible even if the loaf lent differs from the loaf repaid in weight, although bread is ribawi wealth of the weighed category. The texts of Sharia require equality in exchange of ribawi wealth of the same kind by weight for weighed items and by measure for measured items, and consider the excess in one as prohibited usury, necessitating the contract to be void or corrupt. This is regarding the six items mentioned earlier in the discussion of textual custom. The jurists analogized to those six items whatever resembles them in description and effective cause, such as foodstuffs or any two properties that share two common attributes: unity of genus and unity of measure, like bread and other metals besides gold and silver. They considered that the suspicion of usury is like its realization in prohibition and prevention by agreement of the jurists.
This permission to borrow bread by count among neighbors without regard to weight difference was based by Imam Muhammad on prevailing custom, arguing that neighbors have customarily disregarded the weight difference and considered only the count.
Thus this transaction became unsuitable as a means for prohibited usury, because the effective cause of the general texts prohibiting usury (which is profiteering) is not realized in this way, and the ruling revolves with its cause. There is no specific text prohibiting borrowing bread by count among neighbors by way of specification and designation that would make this custom conflict with an explicit text. So the observed measure became the effective cause of the general texts in prohibiting usury, and this cause is not realized in this custom among neighbors where they do not consider the weight difference between loaves.
10/73 - These are a collection of established fiqhi examples that, along with their explanations transmitted from the jurists, state that if the effective cause of a conflicting text is removed and negated by custom, that custom is legally respected, even if it is a new custom.
But if the effective cause of the conflicting text is not negated by the new custom, then no regard is given to this custom against the generality of the text, because considering the new custom in such a case would be abrogation of the text, and custom does not have such authority.
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage presents a key Hanafi principle in the theory of custom (العرف). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, demonstrates how a new custom (عرف حادث) can interact with general nusus (النصوص العامة) without abrogating them. The passage type is a qawida (maxim) with its furuat (applications).

First, the author gives two examples (the coin example and the bread example) to show that when a custom negates the effective cause (علة) of a textual ruling, the ruling may change. In the coin example, people treat different weights as equal in value due to custom, allowing exchange without riba even though the text normally prohibits excess. This is based on Abu Yusuf's view that a text's ruling revolves with its custom-based cause.

Second, the bread example: borrowing bread by count among neighbors is permitted despite bread being ribawi, because the custom of disregarding weight negates the cause of riba (inequality in weight). This is the view of Imam Muhammad, and it is the relied-upon position in the madhhab.

Third, the author generalizes: if a new custom removes the cause of a text, it is respected; but if the cause remains, the custom cannot override the text, as that would be abrogation (نسخ). This is a crucial limitation: custom can only affect the ruling if it negates the 'illa, not the text itself.

The argument is based on talil (reasoning) from the nature of cause and effect, and the examples illustrate how custom can cause a change in fatwa without violating the scripture.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): A prevalent practice among people that is consistent and acceptable in the Sharia; in this passage, specifically a new custom ('urf hadith) that may affect legal rulings.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause or ratio legis of a legal ruling, derived from the text or analogy; if a custom negates this cause, the ruling may change.
النص العام (al-nass al-'amm): A general textual ruling from the Qur'an or Sunnah that applies to all cases unless specified otherwise; the passage discusses how a new custom may interact with such texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, when does a new custom ('urf hadith) override the ruling of a general text?
A) When the custom is widespread and accepted by scholars.
B) When the custom negates the effective cause ('illa) of the text.
C) When the custom is not explicitly forbidden by the text.
D) When the custom is older than the text.

Key Takeaway
A new custom overrides a general text only if it negates the text's effective cause ('illa); otherwise, the custom cannot alter the ruling, as that would be abrogation (naskh).

Custom Change and Its Effect on Rulings: The Black Dye Example
Case study on custom change affecting a fiqh ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how changing customs can alter legal rulings and apply the Hanafi principle of considering 'urf in fatwa.

Core Idea
When custom changes, the ruling changes—the black dye example shows that a defect can become an enhancement.

This lesson explores a classic Hanafi case study illustrating how legal rulings adapt to changing customs. The example of dyeing a stolen cloth black demonstrates that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) revised Abu Hanifa's original ruling because the custom regarding black color had changed. This highlights the dynamic nature of Islamic law, particularly in the Hanafi school, where 'urf (custom) is a recognized source for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and its basis
Examine Abu Hanifa's original position that dyeing a stolen cloth black is a defect. Note that this ruling was based on the custom of his time, where black was considered undesirable.
The ruling was not based on a text but on prevailing custom.
2
Identify the change in custom
Understand that the custom changed when the Abbasids adopted black as their official colour, making it desirable. This change is the catalyst for the revised ruling.
Custom change is a factual shift in societal norms.
3
See how the Sahibayn's ruling changed
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani ruled that dyeing black is now an enhancement, not a defect. Consequently, the owner can either take the cloth and pay for the enhancement or leave it and claim compensation for its un-dyed value.
The practical ruling (thamarah al-khilaf) is the choice given to the owner.
4
Understand the principle behind the change
The Hanafi jurists emphasize that this difference is solely due to change in custom and time, not a disagreement on legal methodology. If Abu Hanifa were alive, he would agree with his companions.
This affirms that rulings based on custom are contingent on that custom.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Consider a mufti today facing a similar situation where a custom has changed, such as new valuation methods for commodities. The principle is that fatwas must adapt to the current 'urf, provided it does not contradict a clear text.
This dynamic approach is part of the Hanafi theory of fatwa.

Source Text

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
فلما تبدل عرف الناس في اللون الأسود وأصبح مرغوباً فيه لما اتخذه العباسيون شعاراً قال الصاحبان بأن صبغ الغاصب الثوب بالأسود يعتبر زيادة فيه لا تعييباً له فيطبق عليه حكم زيادة الغاصب في المغصوب، فيخير المالك بين تركه له وتضمينه قيمته غير مصبوغ، أو أن يأخذه ويدفع للغاصب قيمة الزيادة صيانة لحقيهما. وقد نص فقهاء المذهب على أن هذا الاختلاف عن أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في هذه المسألة وكثير من أمثالها اختلاف عرف وزمان لا اختلاف نظر، وبرهان، فلو كان أبو حنيفة حياً لقال بما قال صاحباه عند تبدل العرف (ر: كتاب الغصب في «الكفاية» شرح الهداية، ٢٧١/٨، وفي رد المحتار ١٢٥/٥).
إنما هو خلاف
٢ - وفي المذاهب الأخرى ذكر القرافي المالكي في كتابه «الإحكام» أن الإفتاء بالأحكام التي مستندها العوائد بعد تغير تلك العوائد هو الإجماع، ووصف ذلك بأنه: «جهالة في الدين ممن يقولون: «إننا مقلدون ليس لنا إلا أن نفتي بما في الكتب من الأحكام المنقولة عن المجتهدين» وقد بين أن تغير تلك الأحكام وفقاً للعوائد المتجددة ليس إنشاء اجتهاد ينقض اجتهاد المجتهدين، بل هو تطبيق لقاعدة أجمع عليها المجتهدون في الشريعة وأوجبوا السير في ظلها (١).
(١) ر: «الإحكام للقرافي السؤال ٣٩ ص ٦٨، ونشر العرف لابن عابدين في رسائله ١٢٥/٢، ورسالة العرف والعادة للأستاذ أبي سنة ص ١٠٥.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. When the custom of people regarding the color black changed and it became desirable because the Abbasids adopted it as a symbol, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that the usurper's dyeing the cloth black is considered an increase in it and not a defect, so the ruling of the usurper's increase in the usurped property applies. The owner is given the choice between leaving it and claiming compensation for its value undyed, or taking it and paying the usurper the value of the increase to preserve both their rights. The jurists of the school have stated that this difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions in this issue and many similar ones is a difference of custom and time, not a difference of opinion and proof. If Abu Hanifa were alive, he would have said what his two companions said when the custom changed. (See: the chapter on usurpation in al-Kifaya sharh al-Hidaya, 8/271, and in Radd al-Muhtar, 5/125). It is merely a difference. 2- In other schools, al-Qarafi al-Maliki mentioned in his book al-Ihkam that issuing fatwas on rulings that are based on customs after those customs have changed is a matter of consensus, and he described it as: 'Ignorance of the religion on the part of those who say: We are mere followers; we have nothing to do except issue fatwas based on what is in the books of rulings transmitted from the mujtahids.' He explained that changing those rulings in accordance with renewed customs is not creating a new ijtihad that invalidates the ijtihad of the mujtahids, but rather is an application of a principle upon which the mujtahids of the Sharia have agreed and obligated people to proceed under its umbrella (1). (1) See: al-Ihkam by al-Qarafi, question 39, p. 68; Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin in his treatises, 2/125; Treatise on Custom and Habit by Professor Abu Sunnah, p. 105.
Source pages p. 875

Commentary
This passage from Chapter Nine on the Theory of Custom presents a case study demonstrating how legal rulings can change with changing customs. It begins by stating that when the custom regarding black color changed (from being undesirable to desirable due to Abbasid adoption), the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) ruled that a usurper dyeing a stolen cloth black is considered an enhancement, not a defect. Consequently, the owner has the option to either take the cloth and pay for the enhancement or leave it and claim compensation for its un-dyed value. The text emphasizes that the difference between Abu Hanifa (who considered it a defect in his time) and his companions is solely due to change in custom and time, not a fundamental disagreement. It even suggests that if Abu Hanifa were present, he would concur with his companions. The passage then cites al-Qarafi al-Maliki, who affirms that issuing fatwas in accordance with changing customs is a matter of consensus, not an innovation against earlier scholars. This illustrates the Hanafi recognition of 'urf (custom) as a dynamic factor in legal rulings, provided it does not contradict definitive texts.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice that is established among people and used as a basis for legal rulings.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property without right.
زيادة الغاصب (ziyadat al-ghasib): An increase or enhancement made by the usurper to the usurped property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Sahibayn consider dyeing a stolen cloth black as an enhancement rather than a defect?
A) Because black dye is inherently valuable
B) Because the custom changed and black became desirable due to its use by the Abbasids
C) Because they disagreed with Abu Hanifa's methodology
D) Because the owner preferred black

Key Takeaway
Custom is a dynamic source in Hanafi fiqh; rulings based on custom change when the custom changes, and this is not a contradiction but an application of a fundamental principle.

Customary Indicators: Definition, Types, and Judicial Application
Introducing customary indicators and their distinction from custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a customary indicator (qarinah 'urfiyya), distinguish it from a rational indicator, and apply the Hanafi position that conclusive indicators are sufficient proof in court.

Core Idea
A customary indicator is a sign whose evidentiary force depends on custom — distinct from custom itself.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies a common confusion: customary indicators (qara'in 'urfiyya) are not the same as custom ('urf). While related, they are a distinct category of legal evidence. This lesson defines qara'in, explains their two types (rational and customary), and shows how Hanafi law uses them in judicial proof — even allowing conclusive indicators to stand alone as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Qara'in from 'Urf
Understand why Zarqa insists on separating customary indicators from custom. He argues they are independent subjects with their own rules, though related.
The key difference: custom is the basis, the indicator is the sign.
2
Define Qarinah and Its Components
A qarinah is an apparent sign that accompanies a hidden matter and indicates it. Identify the three components: sign (amaanah), hidden matter (shay' khafiyy), and indication (dalalah).
The word comes from muqaranah = accompaniment.
3
Categorize Indicators: Rational vs Customary
Rational indicators have a fixed logical connection (e.g., smoke–fire). Customary indicators depend on prevalent custom or habit (e.g., buying sheep before Eid indicates sacrifice).
The same act can be a customary indicator in one society and not in another if the custom differs.
4
Assess the Strength of Indication
Indicators vary: some are definitive (qat'iyyah) like smoke for fire, others are probable (dhanniyyah). Only definitive indicators alone can serve as complete proof in court.
Zarqa mentions the Majalla article 1741 for the definitive scenario.
5
Apply Judicial Ruling: Conclusive Indicator as Sufficient Proof
Hanafi position: a conclusive indicator (e.g., a person emerging from a house with bloody knife and a slain person inside) is sufficient evidence for conviction. This demonstrates the high evidentiary value of qara'in.
Note that this is a qarinah 'aqliyyah, but customary ones can also be conclusive if the custom is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس والسبعون
تمهيد للفصول ٧٥ - ۷۸
مقارنة العرف بالقرائن العرفية واختلاف الزمان
١/٧٥ - اعتاد الباحثون في موقع العرف وسلطانه ومدى اعتباره في الفقه الإسلامي أن يدخلوا في بحثه وأمثلته مسائل القرائن العرفية، ومسائل تغير الزمان التي تتبدل فيها الأحكام بحسب الأحوال الخاصة والأخلاق العامة.
ونحن نرى أن هذين الموضوعين، على مالهما في الفقه الإسلامي من خطر وعظيم أثر في إثبات الأحكام وتبدلها لا يصح إدماجهما في مفهوم العرف، لأنهما يؤلفان موضوعين مستقلين ونوعاً من الأدلة الشرعية متميزاً عن العرف بمفهومه الفقهي الخاص وإن كان بينهما وبين العرف تقارب وتناسب.
ونحن اجتنبنا التمثيل بشيء من أمثلتهما في جميع مباحثنا السابقة عن نظرية العرف لهذه الملاحظة.
وسنشرح فيما يلي كلاً منهما فيما يلي من الفصول للمقارنة؛ كي ينجلي الفرق بينهما وبين العرف.

الباب التاسع: نظرية العُرف
القرائن العرفية
٢/٧٥ - القرائن جمع قرينة والمراد بها كل أمارة ظاهرة تقارن شيئاً خفياً فتدل عليه وهي مأخوذة من المقارنة بمعنى المرافقة والمصاحبة. ودلالة القرائن على مدلولاتها تتفاوت في القوة والضعف تفاوتاً كثيراً: فقد تصل في القوة إلى درجة الدلالة القطعية كالرماد أو الدخان، فإنهما قرينة قاطعة على وجود النار. وقد تضعف حتى تنزل دلالتها إلى مجرد الاحتمال.
والقرينة قد تكون عقلية وقد تكون عرفية:
فالقرينة العقلية هي التي تكون النسبة بينها وبين مدلولها ثابتة يستنتجها العقل دائماً، كوجود المسروقات عند المتهم بالسرقة.
والقرينة العرفية هي التي تكون هذه النسبة بينها وبين مدلولها قائمة على عرف أو عادة تتبعها دلالتها وجوداً وعدماً، وتتبدل بتبدلهما، كشراء المسلم شاة قبيل عيد الأضحى، فإنها قرينة على قصد الأضحية، وكشراء الصائغ خاتماً فإنه قرينة على أنه اشتراه للتجارة فلولا عادة التضحية عند الأول، والتجارة بالمصوغات عند الثاني لما كان ذلك قرينة.
٣/٧٥ - درجات اعتماد الفقهاء على القرائن بنوعيها في القضاء: والفقه الإسلامي قد اعتبر القرائن مهما كان نوعها من الأدلة المثبتة التي يعتمد عليها في القضاء بدرجات مختلفة:
أ - فإذا كانت القرينة قطعية كانت وحدها بينة نهائية كافية للقضاء، كما لو رُئي شخص خارجاً من دار وهو مرتبك وفي يده سكين ملوث بالدم ووجد في الدار شخص ذبيح يضطرب، فيُعتبر الخارج هو القاتل (ر: المجلة / ١٧٤١ / وشرحها).
Chapter Seventy-Five: Preface to Chapters 75-78: Comparison of Custom with Customary Indicators and Change of Time. 1/75: Researchers on the status, authority, and extent of consideration of custom in Islamic jurisprudence have been accustomed to include in its discussion and examples the issues of customary indicators and the issues of change of time in which rulings change according to particular circumstances and general morals. We see that these two topics, despite their danger and great impact in establishing and changing rulings, should not be integrated into the concept of custom, because they constitute independent subjects and a type of legal evidence distinct from custom in its specific jurisprudential concept, even though there is proximity and proportionality between them and custom. We have refrained from illustrating with any of their examples in all our previous discussions of the theory of custom for this observation. We will explain each of them in the following chapters for comparison, so that the difference between them and custom becomes clear. Book Nine: Theory of Custom. Customary Indicators. 2/75: Al-qara'in is the plural of qarinah, and by it is meant every apparent sign that accompanies a hidden thing and indicates it, taken from muqaranah meaning accompaniment and companionship. The indication of indicators to their significations varies greatly in strength and weakness: it may reach in strength the degree of definitive indication, like ashes or smoke, for they are a definitive indicator of the existence of fire. And it may weaken until its indication descends to mere probability. The indicator may be rational or customary. The rational indicator is that whose relationship between it and its indication is fixed, deduced by the mind always, like the presence of stolen goods with the accused of theft. The customary indicator is that whose relationship between it and its indication is based on a custom or habit, its indication follows them in existence and non-existence, and changes with their change, like a Muslim buying a sheep shortly before Eid al-Adha, for it is an indicator of the intention of sacrifice, and like a jeweler buying a ring, for it is an indicator that he bought it for trade; were it not for the custom of sacrifice in the first and trade in jewelry in the second, that would not be an indicator. 3/75: Degrees of reliance of jurists on both types of indicators in adjudication: Islamic jurisprudence has considered indicators, whatever their type, as established evidence relied upon in adjudication to varying degrees: a. If the indicator is conclusive, it alone is sufficient final proof for adjudication, as if a person is seen coming out of a house in a state of confusion with a bloodstained knife in his hand, and a slaughtered person is found in the house writhing, then the one coming out is considered the killer (cf. Majalla, article 1741 and its commentary).
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical introduction by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. He begins by noting that researchers on custom ('urf) have traditionally included the topics of customary indicators (qara'in 'urfiyya) and change of time (taghayyur al-zaman) within the study of custom. However, he argues that these are independent subjects and should be treated separately, even though they are related to custom. The author then defines qara'in (sing. qarinah) as an apparent sign that accompanies something hidden and points to it. The strength of indication varies: some are conclusive (like smoke indicating fire) and some merely probable. He distinguishes two types: rational indicators (qara'in 'aqliyya), where the connection is logically necessary (e.g., stolen goods with a thief), and customary indicators (qara'in 'urfiyya), where the connection depends on prevailing custom (e.g., buying a sheep before Eid indicates intent to sacrifice). Finally, he explains that Islamic law accepts both types as evidence in court, with conclusive indicators alone being sufficient for a verdict. He gives the example from the Majalla of a person emerging from a house with a bloody knife and a slain person inside; this circumstantial evidence is enough to convict. This passage clarifies that customary indicators are not the same as custom itself but are a separate type of evidence based on custom.

Key Terms
القرينة (al-qarinah): An apparent sign that accompanies a hidden matter and indicates it
القرينة العقلية (al-qarinah al-'aqliyyah): A sign whose connection to its significance is logically fixed, independent of custom
القرينة العرفية (al-qarinah al-'urfiyyah): A sign whose connection to its significance depends on a prevalent custom or habit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a customary indicator (qarinah 'urfiyyah) according to the passage?
A) Smoke indicating fire
B) Stolen goods found with a thief
C) A Muslim buying a sheep just before Eid al-Adha
D) A person seen leaving a house with a bloody knife

Key Takeaway
Customary indicators are signs whose evidentiary force depends on custom; they are distinct from custom itself and vary in strength, with conclusive ones being sufficient as evidence in Hanafi courts.

Customary Presumptions and Their Types
Classification of legal presumptions with Hanafi example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between legal/statutory and judicial presumptions, and explain the Hanafi application of the legal presumption in talaq al-firar.

Core Idea
Presumptions are either lawgiver-established (legal) or judge-employed (judicial); customary presumptions attach to custom, as in talaq al-firar.

In this passage, the author classifies legal presumptions (qara'in) into two types: those established by the Lawgiver as a basis for rulings, and those used by judges to ascertain facts. The Hanafi example of talaq al-firar (deathbed divorce) illustrates how a customary presumption can override the apparent intent of the husband, preserving the wife's inheritance rights. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for a mufti when applying custom (urf) in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of presumptions
From the passage, list the two types: legal/statutory (shar'iyyah/qanuniyyah) and judicial (qada'iyyah). Note their definers: the Lawgiver for the first, the judge for the second.
Legal presumptions are predominantly rational, judicial can be rational or customary.
2
Determine the basis of indication
For customary presumptions (qara'in 'urfiyyah), the basis is custom ('adah). The author notes they can be appended to the study of custom (urf).
This links to the Hanafi principle that custom is a source of law.
3
Analyze the talaq al-firar example
The passage explains that a deathbed divorce without the wife's consent is a legal presumption that the husband intended to deprive her of inheritance. Hanafi jurists rule that she inherits under certain conditions.
This shows prevention of abuse of rights (ta'assuf fi isti'mal al-haqq).
4
Recognize that legal presumptions can also be customary
The passage notes that legal presumptions may rely on permanent general customs, citing disputes over household goods and ship cargo as examples.
Customary presumptions are a subset of legal presumptions when custom is permanent.

الفصل (٧٥) القرائن العرفية
وتعيين مراد الشارع وكل متكلم، وله سلطان في توجيه المسؤوليات كوكيل عن الشارع في كل ما سكت عنه نص الشارع والعاقد.
على أن القرائن العرفية نظراً إلى أن مستند الدلالة فيها إنما هو العادة، يمكن بهذا الاعتبار أن تلحق ببحث العرف إلحاقاً كما فعلنا هنا، لا أن تحشر مسائلها في صلب مباحثه .
هذا، وقد أشرنا في مناسبة سابقة من بحث الاستصلاح إلى أنَّ
القرائن نوعان:
الأول: قرائن شرعية أو قانونية وهي التي يعتمدها الشارع أساساً إيجاب بعض الأحكام.
في
والثاني : قرائن قضائية، وهي التي يتخذها القاضي دليلاً في تمحيص الوقائع وإثباتها، ويعود إليه تقدير دلالتها على الواقع (ر: ف ٨/٤ الحاشية). (1)
فالقرائن الشرعية أو القانونية هي في الأغلب من نوع القرائن العقلية، لأن الشارع يبني عليها حكماً ثابتاً، فيجب أن يقام على نسبة ثابتة بين الدلالة ومدلولها كما في حكم التقادم (مرور الزمان)، وطلاق الفرار أما القرائن القضائية فقد تكون عقلية أو عرفية، لأن القضاء يستأنس الأدلة ولو وقتية لمعرفة الوقائع التي سيبني عليها الحكم.

لأنه
(۱) طلاق الفرار هو أن يطلق الرجل زوجته طلاقاً بائناً دون رضاها وهو في مرض موته أو في موقف يغلب فيه الهلاك. فقد اعتبر الاجتهاد الحنفي أن هذه الحالة قرينة شرعية على أن الرجل أراد بهذا الطلاق أن يحرم زوجته من ميراثه عند يأسه من الحياة، لأنه إذا كان يقصد الخلاص من سوء العشرة الذي شُرعَ له الطلاق فهذا حاصل دون طلاق في مرض الموت على باب الفراق الأبدي. فلذا أوجبوا ميراث زوجته منه بشرائط معينة. وهذا من أبرز صور نظرية منع التعسف في استعمال الحق في الفقه الإسلامي. على أنه قد تكون القرائن الشرعية أو القانونية أيضاً من النوع العرفي الذي تستند فيه دلالة القرينة إلى العادات العامة الدائمة كما في مسألة اختلاف الزوجين في أمتعة البيت ومسألة السفينة وأشباههما .
۹۳۹
Chapter (75): Customary Presumptions. And determining the intent of the Lawgiver and every speaker, and it has authority in directing responsibilities as a deputy of the Lawgiver in everything that the text of the Lawgiver and the contracting party have remained silent about. However, customary presumptions, considering that the basis of indication in them is nothing but custom, can by this consideration be appended to the discussion of custom as we have done here, not that their issues be crammed into the core of its discussions. This, and we have indicated in a previous occasion in the discussion of istislah that presumptions are of two types: First: legal or statutory presumptions, which the Lawgiver relies upon as a basis for imposing some rulings. Second: judicial presumptions, which the judge adopts as evidence in scrutinizing facts and proving them, and it is up to him to assess their indication of reality (see: f 8/4 footnote). (1) The legal or statutory presumptions are mostly of the rational type, because the Lawgiver bases a fixed ruling upon them, so a fixed proportion must be established between the indication and its indicated, as in the ruling of limitation (passage of time), and divorce of escape. As for judicial presumptions, they may be rational or customary, because the judiciary utilizes evidence even if temporary to ascertain the facts upon which the ruling will be based. (1) Divorce of escape is when a man divorces his wife irrevocably without her consent while he is in his deathbed or in a situation where destruction is likely. The Hanafi ijtihad considered this situation a legal presumption that the man intended by this divorce to deprive his wife of inheritance when he despairs of life, because if he intended deliverance from bad companionship for which divorce was legislated, that can be achieved without divorce in death sickness on the door of eternal separation. Hence they obligated her inheritance from him under specific conditions. This is one of the most prominent forms of the theory of prevention of abuse of rights in Islamic jurisprudence. However, legal or statutory presumptions may also be of the customary type where the indication of the presumption relies on permanent general customs as in the case of dispute between spouses over household goods, the case of the ship, and similar cases. 939
Source pages p. 880

Commentary
This passage discusses customary presumptions (qara'in 'urfiyyah) and their role in legal interpretation. The author begins by stating that such presumptions help determine the intent of the Lawgiver and any speaker, and they have authority in guiding responsibilities where the text is silent. He clarifies that since these presumptions rely on custom, they can be studied under the topic of custom rather than being forced into its core discussions. The passage then divides presumptions into two types: legal/statutory (shar'iyyah/qanuniyyah), which the Lawgiver uses as a basis for rulings, and judicial (qada'iyyah), which judges use to examine and prove facts. Legal presumptions are predominantly rational, requiring a fixed link between the indication and what is indicated, as seen in rules like limitation (murur al-zaman) and divorce of escape (talaq al-firar). Judicial presumptions can be rational or customary, as courts may use temporary evidence to ascertain facts. The footnote elaborates on talaq al-firar: a man divorcing his wife irrevocably while on his deathbed without her consent. Hanafi jurists treat this as a legal presumption that he intended to deprive her of inheritance, thus they rule that she inherits from him under certain conditions, illustrating the principle of preventing abuse of rights. Finally, the author notes that legal presumptions can also be customary, based on permanent general customs, citing examples like disputes over household goods or cargo on a ship.

Key Terms
القرائن العرفية (al-qara'in al-'urfiyyah): Presumptions whose basis of indication is custom; used to determine intent in legal contexts.
قرائن شرعية أو قانونية (qara'in shar'iyyah aw qanuniyyah): Presumptions established by the Lawgiver as a basis for imposing certain rulings; they are predominantly rational.
قرائن قضائية (qara'in qada'iyyah): Presumptions used by the judge as evidence to verify and prove facts; can be rational or customary.
طلاق الفرار (talaq al-firar): A divorce pronounced by a man in his deathbed without his wife's consent, presumed to be for evading inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between legal/statutory presumptions and judicial presumptions?
A) Legal presumptions are always rational, while judicial presumptions are always customary.
B) Legal presumptions are relied upon by the Lawgiver for rulings, while judicial presumptions are used by the judge for fact-finding.
C) Legal presumptions are based on custom, while judicial presumptions are based on reason.
D) Legal presumptions are fixed, while judicial presumptions can be overturned by qiyas.

Key Takeaway
Presumptions are either lawgiver-established (legal) or judge-employed (judicial); customary presumptions attach to custom, as illustrated by talaq al-firar preventing abuse of rights.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Maxim: Change of Rulings with Change of Times
Introduces the maxim and its justification through later jurists' practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان', explain its scope as applying only to ijtihadi rulings, and apply it to a new case involving changing customs.

Core Idea
Ijtihadi rulings are dynamic; they change with time to maintain justice, while fixed rulings remain immutable.

Islamic law is not static. The Hanafi school recognizes that ijtihadi rulings — those derived through analogy (qiyas) or consideration of public interest (maslaha) — must evolve with changing societal conditions, means, and morals. This lesson unpacks the famous maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (The change of rulings with changing times is not to be denied). You will learn why later Hanafi jurists sometimes issued fatwas different from the early imams, and how this maxim is applied in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its basis
Locate the maxim in the text: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان'. Note that it is derived from the practice of later jurists who gave fatwas contrary to earlier imams due to changing conditions.
The maxim is recorded in Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah (Article 39).
2
Understand the rationale
Analyze why rulings change: ijtihadi rulings aim to establish justice, bring benefits, and repel harms. They are closely tied to temporal circumstances, means, and public morals. When these change, the ruling may no longer achieve its purpose.
The text gives the example of a ruling that was effective in one era but later becomes counterproductive.
3
Recognize the authority of later jurists
Later jurists from all schools sometimes ruled contrary to their early imams. They justified this by citing changed times and moral decay. The text quotes Ibn Abidin: if the early imams lived later, they would have agreed with the later jurists.
This does not mean contradicting the school; it means adapting the school's ijtihad to new realities.
4
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Consider a modern transaction, like online sales, not explicitly addressed by early Hanafis. Using this maxim, a mufti can derive a ruling based on prevailing customs (urf) as long as it does not violate definitive texts.
The maxim is used alongside other qawaid like 'العادة محكمة' (custom is authoritative).

Source Text

الفصل السادس والسبعون
العرف وتغير الزمان : نظرة عامة
١/٧٦ - من المقرّر في فقه الشريعة أن لتغير الأوضاع والأحوال الزمنية تأثيراً كبيراً في كثير من الأحكام الشرعية الاجتهادية. فإن هذه الأحكام تنظيم أوجبه الشرع يهدف إلى إقامة العدل وجلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد. فهي ذات ارتباط وثيق بالأوضاع والوسائل الزمنية وبالأخلاق العامة. فكم من حكم كان تدبيراً وعلاجاً ناجعاً لبيئة في زمن معين، فأصبح بعد جيل أو أجيال لا يوصل إلى المقصود منه، أو أصبح يفضي إلى عكسه، بتغير الأوضاع والوسائل والأخلاق.
وعن هذا أفتى الفقهاء المتأخرون من شتى المذاهب الفقهية في كثير من المسائل بعكس ما أفتى به أئمة مذاهبهم وفقهاؤها الأولون، وصرّح هؤلاء المتأخرون بأن سبب اختلاف فتواهم عمن سبقهم هو اختلاف الزمان وفساد الأخلاق. فليسوا في الحقيقة مخالفين للسابقين من فقهاء مذاهبهم، بل لو وجد الأئمة الأولون في عصر المتأخرين، ورأوا اختلاف الزمان والأخلاق لعدلوا إلى ما قال المتأخرون.
ر: نشر العرف لابن عابدين في مجموعة رسائله ١٢٥/٢). وعلى هذا الأساس أسست القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان» (م/٣٩).
٢/٧٦ - ما هي الأحكام التي يجب تغييرها بتغير الزمان:
وقد اتفقت كلمة فقهاء المذاهب على أن الأحكام التي تتبدل بتبدل الزمان وأخلاق الناس هي الأحكام الاجتهادية من قياسية ومصلحية، أي التي قررها الاجتهاد بناءً على القياس أو على دواعي المصلحة، وهي المقصودة بالقاعدة الآنفة الذكر.
أما الأحكام الأساسية التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها وتوطيدها بنصوصها الأصلية الآمرة ،الناهية كحرمة المحرمات المطلقة، وكوجوب التراضي في العقود، والتزام الإنسان بعقده، وضمان الضرر الذي يُلحقه بغيره وسريان إقراره على نفسه دون غيره، ووجوب منع الأذى وقمع الإجرام، وسد الذرائع إلى الفساد وحماية الحقوق المكتسبة، ومسؤولية كل مكلف عن عمله وتقصيره وعدم مؤاخذة بريء بذنب غيره إلى غير الأحكام والمبادىء الشرعية الثابتة التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها من ومقاومة خلافها، فهذه لا تتبدل بتبدل الأزمان بل هي الأصول التي جاءت بها الشريعة لإصلاح الأزمان والأجيال؛ ولكن وسائل تحقيقها وأساليب تطبيقها قد تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة المحدثة .
ذلك
فوسيلة حماية الحقوق مثلاً وهو القضاء كانت المحاكم فيه تقوم على أسلوب القاضي الفرد، وقضاؤه على درجة واحدة قطعية، فيمكن أن تتبدل إلى أسلوب محكمة ،الجماعة، وتعدد درجات المحاكم، بحسب المصلحة الزمنية التي أصبحت تقتضي زيادة الاحتياط لفساد الذمم.
فالحقيقة أن الأحكام الشرعية التي تتبدل بتبدل الزمان مهما تغيرت باختلاف الزمن فإن المبدأ الشرعي فيها واحد وهو إحقاق الحق، وجلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد وليس تبدل الأحكام إلا تبدل الوسائل والأساليب الموصلة إلى غاية الشارع، فإن تلك الوسائل والأساليب في الغالب لم. تحدّدها الشريعة الإسلامية، بل تركتها مطلقة لكي يختار منها في كل زمان ما هو أصلح في التنظيم نتاجاً، وأنجح في التقويم علاجاً.
۳/۷۹ - عوامل تغير الزمان نوعان: فساد وتطور قد يكون تغير الزمان الموجب لتبديل الأحكام الفقهية الاجتهادية ناشئاً
Chapter Seventy-Six: Custom and Change of Time: An Overview 1/76 - It is established in the fiqh of Sharia that change in conditions and temporal circumstances has a great influence on many ijtihadi legal rulings. For these rulings are an organization that the Sharia has made obligatory to establish justice, bring benefits, and repel harms. They are closely connected to temporal conditions, means, and public morals. How many a ruling was an effective measure and cure for a particular environment at a certain time, but after a generation or generations, it no longer achieves its purpose, or leads to its opposite, due to changes in conditions, means, and morals. On this basis, later jurists from various schools issued fatwas in many issues contrary to what the founders of their schools and earlier jurists had ruled. These later ones stated that the reason for their different fatwa from those before them is the difference of time and corruption of morals. They are not truly opposing the earlier jurists of their schools; rather, if the earlier imams were in the era of the later ones and saw the difference in time and morals, they would have adopted what the later ones said. (Ref: Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin in his collection of treatises, 2/125). And on this basis, the legal maxim was established: 'The change of rulings with changing times is not to be denied' (M/39). 2/76 - Which rulings must change with change of time: The jurists of the schools have agreed that the rulings that change with the change of time and people's morals are the ijtihadi rulings, whether analogical or based on public interest, i.e., those established by ijtihad based on analogy or considerations of public interest. These are intended by the aforementioned maxim. As for the basic rulings that the Sharia came to establish and consolidate through its original commanding and prohibiting texts, such as the prohibition of absolutely forbidden things, the obligation of mutual consent in contracts, a person's commitment to his contract, liability for harm he causes to others, the validity of his acknowledgement against himself but not others, the obligation to prevent harm and suppress crime, blocking the means to corruption, protecting acquired rights, the responsibility of every accountable person for his actions and negligence, and not holding an innocent person liable for another's sin, and other established Sharia rulings and principles that the Sharia came to establish and resist opposition to—these do not change with changing times; rather they are the foundations that the Sharia brought to reform times and generations. However, the means of achieving them and methods of applying them may change with different times. For example, the means of protecting rights, namely the judiciary: courts were based on a single-judge system and a single degree of final judgment; they may change to a multi-judge panel system and multiple court degrees, according to the temporal interest that now requires increased caution due to corruption of consciences. The truth is that the Sharia rulings that change with the change of time, however much they vary, share one Sharia principle: establishing right, bringing benefits, and repelling harms. The change of rulings is only a change of means and methods that lead to the Lawgiver's objective. For those means and methods were generally not specified by Islamic Sharia; rather, they were left open so that each time may choose what is most suitable in organization and most successful in remedial correction. 3/76 - Factors of change of time are two types: corruption and development. The change of time that necessitates changing ijtihadi fiqh rulings may arise...
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'General Introduction to Fiqh' discusses the relationship between custom ('urf), changing times, and legal rulings. It is a philosophical exposition on the dynamism of Islamic law. The author establishes that ijtihadi rulings—those derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas) or considerations of public interest (maslaha)—are subject to change as societal conditions, means, and morals evolve. He supports this by citing the later jurists who gave fatwas different from their earlier imams, attributing the difference to changed times and moral decay. He quotes Ibn Abidin and references the legal maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (The change of rulings with changing times is not to be denied). Importantly, Al-Zarqa distinguishes between two categories: 1) foundational rulings (الاحكام الأساسية) that are fixed and unchangeable, such as the prohibition of absolute haram, the requirement of mutual consent in contracts, liability for harm, etc. These are based on clear texts and are meant to reform times and generations; 2) ijtihadi rulings that are subject to change. He also clarifies that the underlying principles (like establishing justice, bringing benefits, repelling harms) remain constant; what changes are the means and methods of implementation. As an example, he mentions the judiciary: the method of single judge vs. multi-judge panels and appellate levels can change according to time and need. This passage is fundamental for understanding the Hanafi theory of 'urf and the flexibility of ijtihad in response to changing circumstances.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom; the established practices and norms of people that influence legal rulings when not contradicting a definitive text
أحكام اجتهادية (ahkam ijtihadiyya): Rulings derived through ijtihad based on qiyas (analogy) or maslaha (public interest), as opposed to rulings from definitive texts
قاعدة لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان (qā'idat 'lā yunkaru taghayyuru 'l-aḥkām bi taghayyuri 'l-azmān): Legal maxim that ijtihadi rulings may change with changing times; it is not to be denied

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence supports the maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان'?
A) The early Hanafi imams explicitly stated this maxim.
B) Later jurists from various schools issued fatwas contrary to early imams and attributed it to changed times.
C) The Quran commands Muslims to adapt rulings to changing circumstances.
D) The maxim was derived from a consensus of the Sahaba.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' legitimizes the evolution of ijtihadi rulings in response to changing societal conditions, but it does not apply to rulings established by definitive texts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Fixed vs Changeable Rulings: The Distinction
Further explains which rulings change and which do not, with examples of fixed principles and changeable means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fixed rulings (based on definitive texts) and changeable ijtihadi rulings, and explain that only the means of implementing fixed principles may change.

Core Idea
Foundational rulings from definitive texts are immutable; only ijtihadi rulings derived from qiyas or maslaha change with time.

Not all Islamic rulings are subject to change. In this second part of the passage, Dr. al-Zarqa clarifies what is fixed and what is flexible. He lists foundational principles like the prohibition of harm, consent in contracts, and liability for damage as unchangeable. However, the means to uphold these principles—like court structures—can evolve. This lesson sharpens your ability to categorize rulings and understand the limits of legal adaptation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of rulings
The passage explicitly divides rulings into: (1) Fixed (أحكام أساسية) — based on definitive texts, e.g., prohibition of absolute haram, obligation of mutual consent; (2) Changeable (أحكام اجتهادية) — based on ijtihad, qiyas, or maslaha.
Examples of fixed rulings are given: حرمة المحرمات المطلقة, وجوب التراضي, ضمان الضرر, etc.
2
Analyze the fixed rulings list
Read the list carefully. These are principles that the Sharia came to establish and protect. They are not subject to change because they are based on unequivocal texts.
Note that even these fixed principles have means (وسائل) that can change.
3
Understand the role of means (وسائل)
The text states that the means of achieving fixed principles can change. Example: protecting rights through judiciary — the method can shift from single judge to multi-judge panels.
This shows flexibility without altering the principle.
4
Apply the distinction to a new case
Take a modern example: the fixed ruling is that contracts require mutual consent. The means of proving consent (e.g., written agreement, electronic signature) can change with technology.
The ruling on consent itself does not change; only the evidentiary method does.

Source Text

الفصل السادس والسبعون
العرف وتغير الزمان : نظرة عامة

وعن هذا أفتى الفقهاء المتأخرون من شتى المذاهب الفقهية في كثير من المسائل بعكس ما أفتى به أئمة مذاهبهم وفقهاؤها الأولون، وصرّح هؤلاء المتأخرون بأن سبب اختلاف فتواهم عمن سبقهم هو اختلاف الزمان وفساد الأخلاق. فليسوا في الحقيقة مخالفين للسابقين من فقهاء مذاهبهم، بل لو وجد الأئمة الأولون في عصر المتأخرين، ورأوا اختلاف الزمان والأخلاق لعدلوا إلى ما قال المتأخرون.
ر: نشر العرف لابن عابدين في مجموعة رسائله ١٢٥/٢). وعلى هذا الأساس أسست القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان» (م/٣٩).
٢/٧٦ - ما هي الأحكام التي يجب تغييرها بتغير الزمان:
وقد اتفقت كلمة فقهاء المذاهب على أن الأحكام التي تتبدل بتبدل الزمان وأخلاق الناس هي الأحكام الاجتهادية من قياسية ومصلحية، أي التي قررها الاجتهاد بناءً على القياس أو على دواعي المصلحة، وهي المقصودة بالقاعدة الآنفة الذكر.
أما الأحكام الأساسية التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها وتوطيدها بنصوصها الأصلية الآمرة ،الناهية كحرمة المحرمات المطلقة، وكوجوب التراضي في العقود، والتزام الإنسان بعقده، وضمان الضرر الذي يُلحقه بغيره وسريان إقراره على نفسه دون غيره، ووجوب منع الأذى وقمع الإجرام، وسد الذرائع إلى الفساد وحماية الحقوق المكتسبة، ومسؤولية كل مكلف عن عمله وتقصيره وعدم مؤاخذة بريء بذنب غيره إلى غير الأحكام والمبادىء الشرعية الثابتة التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها من ومقاومة خلافها، فهذه لا تتبدل بتبدل الأزمان بل هي الأصول التي جاءت بها الشريعة لإصلاح الأزمان والأجيال؛ ولكن وسائل تحقيقها وأساليب تطبيقها قد تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة المحدثة .
ذلك
فوسيلة حماية الحقوق مثلاً وهو القضاء كانت المحاكم فيه تقوم على أسلوب القاضي الفرد، وقضاؤه على درجة واحدة قطعية، فيمكن أن تتبدل إلى أسلوب محكمة ،الجماعة، وتعدد درجات المحاكم، بحسب المصلحة الزمنية التي أصبحت تقتضي زيادة الاحتياط لفساد الذمم.
فالحقيقة أن الأحكام الشرعية التي تتبدل بتبدل الزمان مهما تغيرت باختلاف الزمن فإن المبدأ الشرعي فيها واحد وهو إحقاق الحق، وجلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد وليس تبدل الأحكام إلا تبدل الوسائل والأساليب الموصلة إلى غاية الشارع، فإن تلك الوسائل والأساليب في الغالب لم. تحدّدها الشريعة الإسلامية، بل تركتها مطلقة لكي يختار منها في كل زمان ما هو أصلح في التنظيم نتاجاً، وأنجح في التقويم علاجاً.
۳/۷۹ - عوامل تغير الزمان نوعان: فساد وتطور قد يكون تغير الزمان الموجب لتبديل الأحكام الفقهية الاجتهادية ناشئاً
Chapter Seventy-Six: Custom and Change of Time: An Overview 1/76 - It is established in the fiqh of Sharia that change in conditions and temporal circumstances has a great influence on many ijtihadi legal rulings. For these rulings are an organization that the Sharia has made obligatory to establish justice, bring benefits, and repel harms. They are closely connected to temporal conditions, means, and public morals. How many a ruling was an effective measure and cure for a particular environment at a certain time, but after a generation or generations, it no longer achieves its purpose, or leads to its opposite, due to changes in conditions, means, and morals. On this basis, later jurists from various schools issued fatwas in many issues contrary to what the founders of their schools and earlier jurists had ruled. These later ones stated that the reason for their different fatwa from those before them is the difference of time and corruption of morals. They are not truly opposing the earlier jurists of their schools; rather, if the earlier imams were in the era of the later ones and saw the difference in time and morals, they would have adopted what the later ones said. (Ref: Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin in his collection of treatises, 2/125). And on this basis, the legal maxim was established: 'The change of rulings with changing times is not to be denied' (M/39). 2/76 - Which rulings must change with change of time: The jurists of the schools have agreed that the rulings that change with the change of time and people's morals are the ijtihadi rulings, whether analogical or based on public interest, i.e., those established by ijtihad based on analogy or considerations of public interest. These are intended by the aforementioned maxim. As for the basic rulings that the Sharia came to establish and consolidate through its original commanding and prohibiting texts, such as the prohibition of absolutely forbidden things, the obligation of mutual consent in contracts, a person's commitment to his contract, liability for harm he causes to others, the validity of his acknowledgement against himself but not others, the obligation to prevent harm and suppress crime, blocking the means to corruption, protecting acquired rights, the responsibility of every accountable person for his actions and negligence, and not holding an innocent person liable for another's sin, and other established Sharia rulings and principles that the Sharia came to establish and resist opposition to—these do not change with changing times; rather they are the foundations that the Sharia brought to reform times and generations. However, the means of achieving them and methods of applying them may change with different times. For example, the means of protecting rights, namely the judiciary: courts were based on a single-judge system and a single degree of final judgment; they may change to a multi-judge panel system and multiple court degrees, according to the temporal interest that now requires increased caution due to corruption of consciences. The truth is that the Sharia rulings that change with the change of time, however much they vary, share one Sharia principle: establishing right, bringing benefits, and repelling harms. The change of rulings is only a change of means and methods that lead to the Lawgiver's objective. For those means and methods were generally not specified by Islamic Sharia; rather, they were left open so that each time may choose what is most suitable in organization and most successful in remedial correction. 3/76 - Factors of change of time are two types: corruption and development. The change of time that necessitates changing ijtihadi fiqh rulings may arise...
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'General Introduction to Fiqh' discusses the relationship between custom ('urf), changing times, and legal rulings. It is a philosophical exposition on the dynamism of Islamic law. The author establishes that ijtihadi rulings—those derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas) or considerations of public interest (maslaha)—are subject to change as societal conditions, means, and morals evolve. He supports this by citing the later jurists who gave fatwas different from their earlier imams, attributing the difference to changed times and moral decay. He quotes Ibn Abidin and references the legal maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (The change of rulings with changing times is not to be denied). Importantly, Al-Zarqa distinguishes between two categories: 1) foundational rulings (الاحكام الأساسية) that are fixed and unchangeable, such as the prohibition of absolute haram, the requirement of mutual consent in contracts, liability for harm, etc. These are based on clear texts and are meant to reform times and generations; 2) ijtihadi rulings that are subject to change. He also clarifies that the underlying principles (like establishing justice, bringing benefits, repelling harms) remain constant; what changes are the means and methods of implementation. As an example, he mentions the judiciary: the method of single judge vs. multi-judge panels and appellate levels can change according to time and need. This passage is fundamental for understanding the Hanafi theory of 'urf and the flexibility of ijtihad in response to changing circumstances.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom; the established practices and norms of people that influence legal rulings when not contradicting a definitive text
أحكام أساسية (ahkam asasiyya): Foundational rulings established by definitive texts; they are not subject to change
أحكام اجتهادية (ahkam ijtihadiyya): Rulings derived through ijtihad based on qiyas (analogy) or maslaha (public interest), as opposed to rulings from definitive texts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a fixed ruling that does not change with time?
A) The means of protecting rights, e.g., court structure
B) The prohibition of absolutely forbidden things (حرمة المحرمات المطلقة)
C) The method of executing a contract (e.g., written vs oral)
D) The system of judiciary (single vs multi-judge panels)

Key Takeaway
Fixed rulings based on definitive texts are immutable; only ijtihadi rulings and the means of implementing fixed principles may change with time.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Fixed vs. Changeable Rulings: The Theory of Custom and Time
Conceptual framework for distinguishing fixed and changeable Shari'a rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fundamental (thabita) and ijtihadi rulings, and apply the Hanafi principle that only the means of application change, not the underlying Shari'a objectives.

Core Idea
Fundamental rulings are immutable; ijtihadi rulings change with custom and time to serve unchanging Shari'a purposes.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies a critical principle in Hanafi legal philosophy: not all rulings change with time. Only those derived by ijtihad (qiyas or maslaha) are open to modification. The foundational rulings based on definitive texts remain constant. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti navigating contemporary issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of rulings
Locate where the author distinguishes between 'al-ahkam al-ijtihadiyya' (ijtihadi rulings) and 'al-ahkam al-asasiyya' (fundamental rulings). List the characteristics of each.
The fundamental rulings are derived from definitive texts (nusus asliyya), while ijtihadi rulings are based on qiyas or maslaha.
2
Understand why fundamental rulings are fixed
Examine the listed examples: prohibition of absolute haram, mutual consent, liability for harm, etc. Recognize that these are based on definitive Qur'an and Sunnah and form the unchangeable core of Shari'a.
The ends of Shari'a (maqasid) are constant; only the means to achieve them can be adjusted.
3
Recognize that ijtihadi rulings are means that can change
Note the author's assertion that change occurs in the 'means and methods' (wasā'il wa asālīb) of application, not in the underlying principle. Example: the shift from single-judge courts to multi-judge panels due to corruption of consciences.
The principle remains 'establishing truth and preventing harm'; the method adapts to times.
4
Apply the two factors that necessitate change
Identify the two factors: (a) fasad al-zaman (corruption of the age) and (b) tatawwur (development). Understand that both require re-evaluating previous ijtihadi rulings if they become futile or harmful.
The Shari'a is free from futility and harm; therefore rulings must align with current maslaha.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
الزمان وأخلاق الناس هي الأحكام الاجتهادية من قياسية ومصلحية، أي التي قررها الاجتهاد بناءً على القياس أو على دواعي المصلحة، وهي المقصودة بالقاعدة الآنفة الذكر.
أما الأحكام الأساسية التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها وتوطيدها بنصوصها الأصلية الآمرة ،الناهية كحرمة المحرمات المطلقة، وكوجوب التراضي في العقود، والتزام الإنسان بعقده، وضمان الضرر الذي يُلحقه بغيره وسريان إقراره على نفسه دون غيره، ووجوب منع الأذى وقمع الإجرام، وسد الذرائع إلى الفساد وحماية الحقوق المكتسبة، ومسؤولية كل مكلف عن عمله وتقصيره وعدم مؤاخذة بريء بذنب غيره إلى غير الأحكام والمبادىء الشرعية الثابتة التي جاءت الشريعة لتأسيسها ومقاومة خلافها، فهذه لا تتبدل بتبدل الأزمان بل هي الأصول التي جاءت بها الشريعة لإصلاح الأزمان والأجيال؛ ولكن وسائل تحقيقها وأساليب تطبيقها قد تتبدل باختلاف الأزمنة المحدثة .

الأحكام لتغير الأزمان بنوعيه.
The ninth chapter: Theory of custom. Time and morals of people are the ijtihadi rulings, analogical and maslaha-based, i.e., those decided by ijtihad based on analogy or on considerations of maslaha, and they are intended by the aforementioned principle. As for the fundamental rulings that the Shari'a came to establish and strengthen with its original commanding and prohibiting texts, such as the prohibition of absolute unlawful things, the obligation of mutual consent in contracts, a person's commitment to his contract, guarantee of harm he inflicts on others, his confession binding upon himself and not upon others, the obligation to prevent harm and suppress crime, blocking the means to corruption, protecting acquired rights, the responsibility of every legally responsible person for his action and negligence, and not holding an innocent person liable for another's sin, and other established Shari'a rulings and principles that the Shari'a came to establish and resist opposition to them—these do not change with changing times, but rather they are the foundations that the Shari'a came with to reform times and generations; but the means of achieving them and the methods of their application may change with different new times. For example, the means of protecting rights, namely the judiciary, courts were based on the method of a single judge, and his judgment was final on one level; so it may change to the method of a panel court and multiple levels of courts, according to the temporal maslaha which now requires increased precaution due to corruption of consciences. Indeed, the Shari'a rulings that change with changing time—however much they change with time—the Shari'a principle in them is one: establishing truth, bringing benefits, and repelling harms. And the change of rulings is only a change of the means and methods leading to the purpose of the Lawgiver, for those means and methods in most cases were not specified by Islamic Shari'a, but rather left open so that in every time one may choose what is most beneficial in terms of organization and most successful in terms of rectification. 3/79 - Factors of change of time are two types: corruption and development. The change of time that necessitates the change of ijtihadi fiqh rulings may arise from corruption of morals, loss of piety, and weakness of restraint—what they call: corruption of time. And it may arise from the occurrence of new organizational circumstances and temporal means, such as legal orders, maslaha-based, administrative arrangements, economic methods, and the like. This second type, like the first, necessitates changing the ijtihadi fiqh rulings established before it if they become incompatible with it, because they become then futile or harmful, and the Shari'a is free from that. We have previously quoted from 'al-Muwafaqat' of al-Shatibi that there is no futility in the Shari'a (see: f. 2/64) and we will show below examples of change of rulings due to change of times of both types.
Source pages p. 882

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy exposition of the theory of custom ('urf) and change of time (taghayyur al-zaman) in relation to ijtihadi rulings. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, distinguishes between two categories of Shari'a rulings: (1) fundamental, immutable rulings that are based on definitive texts and principles; these are the core objectives of the Shari'a, such as the prohibition of absolute haram, the necessity of mutual consent, the binding nature of contracts, liability for harm, and personal accountability. These do not change with time. (2) Ijtihadi rulings—those derived through qiyas (analogy) or based on maslaha (public interest). These rulings are subject to change as circumstances, customs, and needs evolve. The author emphasizes that the change is not in the underlying Shari'a principle (which remains one: achieving justice, benefit, and preventing harm), but in the means and methods of application. For example, the structure of courts can change from a single judge system to multiple judges and appellate courts if societal corruption demands greater caution. The author then identifies two factors that necessitate change: a) fasad al-zaman (corruption of the age) due to moral decay, and b) tatawwur (development) due to new administrative, economic, or legal circumstances. Both factors require that previous ijtihadi rulings be adjusted if they no longer serve the Shari'a's purpose, because the Shari'a is free from futility and harm. The author cites al-Shatibi's al-Muwafaqat to support that there is no futility in Shari'a.

Key Terms
الأحكام الاجتهادية (al-ahkam al-ijtihadiyya): Rulings derived by ijtihad based on qiyas or considerations of maslaha, subject to change with time and custom.
الأحكام الأساسية (al-ahkam al-asasiyya): Fundamental rulings based on definitive texts (nusus asliyya amira nahiya) that are immutable and form the core of Shari'a.
فساد الزمان (fasad al-zaman): Corruption of the age due to moral decay, loss of piety, and weak restraint, necessitating change in ijtihadi rulings to prevent harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa's passage, which of the following is an example of a fundamental (asasi) ruling that does not change?
A) The structure of courts (single judge vs. panel).
B) The prohibition of riba (usury).
C) The method of conducting a marriage contract.
D) The number of witnesses required in a contract.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihadi rulings are changeable means; fundamental rulings are fixed ends.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Qa'ida: Ijtihad Rulings Change Due to Corruption of Times
Principle with three illustrative furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'ida 'tataghayyar al-ahkam al-ijtihadiyya bi-fasad al-zaman' to identify when later Hanafi rulings supersede earlier ones based on changed social conditions.

Core Idea
When public morals decay, ijtihad rulings must adapt to preserve justice.

This lesson examines a foundational principle of Hanafi legal methodology: the adaptability of ijtihad rulings to changing social conditions. Through three concrete examples—debtor's gifts, usurper liability, and marital travel—you will see how later Hanafi jurists modified earlier rulings when widespread dishonesty and injustice undermined the original assumptions. Understanding this mechanism is essential for anyone issuing fatwas in a contemporary context.

Guided Path
1
State the qa'ida
Identify the general principle: 'Ijtihad rulings change due to corruption of the times.' Note that this applies only to rulings based on ijtihad, not definitive texts.
The qa'ida is a middle-rank (wusta) universal principle.
2
Analyze the first fur' (debtor's donations)
Read the original ruling: a debtor's gifts and endowments are valid irrespective of debts. Then see the change: later jurists restricted them to surplus after debts. Connect the change to fasad al-zaman (debtors hiding assets).
This belongs to Hibah, Waqf, and Iflas.
3
Analyze the second fur' (usurper's liability for usufruct)
Original: no liability for usufruct. Change: liability for equivalent hire for waqf, orphan, and income-generating property, later generalized. The rationale is the same: increased greed and weakened religious deterrent.
This belongs to Ghasp and Ijarah.
4
Analyze the third fur' (wife's obligation to follow husband)
Original: wife must follow after receiving prompt dower. Change: not compelled if the destination is not her original home. The cause is men's corruption and frequent mistreatment.
This belongs to Nikah, Nafaqah, Talaq.
5
Identify the istithna' (exception)
Rulings based on definitive texts (nass) are not subject to change. Confirm that the furuat are ijtihadi, not nass-based.
The principle 'لا اجتهاد في مورد النص' governs.
