الفصل السابع والسبعون
تغير الأحكام الاجتهادية بفساد الزمان
١/٧٧ - فمن المسائل التي غير الفقهاء المتأخرون أحكامها التي قررها اجتهاد الأئمة الأولين وعللوا ذلك بفساد الزمان، أي بفساد الأخلاق العامة القضايا التالية :
٢/٧٧ - أ - من المقرَّر في أصل المذهب الحنفي أن المدين تنفذ تصرفاته في أمواله بالهبة والوقف وسائر وجوه التبرع، ولو كانت دیونه مستغرقة أمواله ،كلها، باعتبار أن الديون تتعلق بذمته، فتبقى أعيان أمواله حرة، فينفذ فيها تصرفه وهذا هو مقتضى القواعد القياسية .
ثم لما فَسدت ذِمَمُ الناس، وكثر الطمع، وقل الورع، وأصبح المدينون يعمدون إلى تهريب أموالهم من وجه الدائنين عن طريق وقفها أو هبتها لمن يثقون به من قريب أو صديق أفتى المتأخرون من فقهاء المذهبين الحنبلي والحنفي بعدم نَفَاذ هذه التصرفات من المدين إلا فيما يزيد عن وفاء الدين من أمواله كما تقدّم تفصيله في محله من نظرية الأهلية (0/70).
٣/٧٧ - ب - في أصل المذهب الحنفي أنَّ الغاصب لا يضمن قيمة منافع المغصوب عن مدة الغصب، بل يضمن العين فقط إذا هلكت أو تعيبت، لأن المنافع عندهم ليست متقومة في ذاتها، وإنما تتقوم بعقد الإجارة، ولا عقد في الغصب (1)
ولكن المتأخرين من فقهاء المذهب الحنفي نظروا تجرؤ الناس على الغصب، وضعف الوازع الديني في نفوسهم، فأفتوا بتضمين الغاصب أجرة المثل عن منافع المغصوب إذا كان المغصوب مال وقف أو مال يتيم أو معداً للاستغلال على خلاف الأصل القياسي في المذهب، زجراً للناس عن العدوان لفساد الزمان وهذه الأموال الثلاثة مطمع لضعف الباعث الشخصي على حمايتها .
وعلى هذا استقرَّ العمل وجاءت المجلة (ر: م/ ٥٩٦/ وشرحها) واستناداً إلى العلة نفسها نستطيع أن نقول : إنَّ قواعد الاجتهاد الحنفي تتقبل مبدأ تضمين بدل منافع المغصوب مطلقاً في جميع الأموال، لا في هذه الأنواع الثلاثة فقط، لازدياد فساد الذمم وكثرة الطمع في أموال الغير، والتجاوز على الحقوق.
وهذا التعميم في تضمين المنافع توجبه النصوص القانونية لدينا اليوم، وفيه المصلحة .
٤/٧٧ - ج - في أصل المذهب الحنفي أنَّ الزوجة إذا قَبَضَت معجل مهرها تلزم بمتابعة زوجها حيث شاء. ولكن المتأخرين لحظوا انقلاب الأخلاق وغلبة الجور، وأن كثيراً من الرجال يسافرون بزوجاتهم إلى بلاد نائية ليس لهن فيها أهل ولا نصير، فيسيئون معاملتهن ويجورون عليهن فأفتى المتأخرون بأن المرأة، ولو قبضت معجل مهرها لا تجبر على متابعة زوجها إلى مكان إلا إذا كان وطناً لها، وقد جرى فيه عقد الزواج بينهما، وذلك لفساد الزمان وأخلاق الرجال (1)
Chapter Seventy-Seven: Change of Ijtihad Rulings Due to Corruption of Times. 1/77 - Among the issues whose rulings later jurists changed from the ijtihad of the earlier imams, attributing that to corruption of the times—i.e., corruption of public morals—are the following: 2/77 - a) It is established in the original Hanafi school that a debtor's dispositions of his wealth by gift, endowment, and all forms of donation are effective even if his debts consume all his wealth, because debts attach to his liability (dhimmah), so the physical assets remain free and his disposition is effective. This is what analogical rules require. Then when people's honesty became corrupt, greed increased, piety decreased, and debtors began to smuggle their assets away from creditors by endowing or gifting them to trusted relatives or friends, later jurists of the Hanbali and Hanafi schools ruled that such dispositions by a debtor are not effective except for what exceeds the amount needed to repay the debt, as detailed in its place in the theory of legal capacity (0/70). 3/77 - b) In the original Hanafi school, the usurper (ghasib) is not liable for the value of the usufruct of the usurped property during the period of usurpation; he is only liable for the physical object if it is destroyed or damaged, because usufructs are not considered valuable (muttaqawwim) in themselves; they only become valuable through a hire contract, and there is no contract in usurpation. (1) But later Hanafi jurists observed people's audacity in usurpation and the weakness of religious deterrent in their souls, so they ruled that the usurper must pay the equivalent hire for the usufruct of the usurped property if the usurped property is waqf property, an orphan's property, or property prepared for exploitation—contrary to the analogical principle in the school—as a deterrent against aggression due to corruption of the times, and because these three types of property are tempting due to weak personal motivation to protect them. Practice settled on this, and the Majalla (see Article 596 and its commentary) came accordingly. Based on the same rationale, we can say that the rules of Hanafi ijtihad accommodate the principle of liability for the value of usufruct of usurped property absolutely for all properties, not just these three types, due to increased corruption of people's integrity, greed for others' property, and transgression of rights. This generalization in liability for usufruct is mandated by our legal texts today, and it contains benefit. 4/77 - c) In the original Hanafi school, if a wife has received her prompt dower (mu'ajjal), she is obliged to follow her husband wherever he wishes. But later jurists observed the reversal of morals, prevalence of injustice, and that many men travel with their wives to distant lands where they have no family or supporter, and they mistreat and oppress them. So later jurists ruled that a woman, even if she has received her prompt dower, is not compelled to follow her husband to a place unless it is a homeland for her where the marriage contract was concluded, due to corruption of the times and men's morals. (1)
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage illustrates the Hanafi legal principle that ijtihad rulings can change due to corruption of the times (fasād al-zamān). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents three specific examples where later Hanafi jurists (and sometimes Hanbali) departed from the original qiyās-based rulings of the early imams. The first example concerns a debtor's gifts and endowments: originally, such dispositions were valid regardless of debts, but due to widespread dishonesty, later jurists restricted them to surplus wealth. The second example involves a usurper's liability for usufruct: originally, only the property itself was compensable, but later jurists imposed hire liability for waqf, orphan, and income-generating properties, and this was later extended to all property. The third example concerns a wife's obligation to follow her husband: originally, receipt of prompt dower made obedience mandatory, but later jurists exempted her if the destination was not her original home, due to men's corruption. The author notes that these changes are grounded in the same rationale (ʿillah) of fasād al-zamān and are in line with modern legal texts. The passage also shows the flexibility of Hanafi methodology in responding to social change.

Key Terms
فساد الزمان (fasād al-zamān): Corruption of public morals and decline in religious and ethical standards of society, leading to widespread dishonesty and injustice.
تضمين المنافع (taḍmīn al-manāfiʿ): Imposing financial liability for the value of usufruct, specifically requiring the usurper to pay the equivalent hire for benefits taken.
غاصب (ghāṣib): One who wrongfully takes and possesses another's property without right.
معجل (muʿajjal): The prompt or immediate portion of the dower (mahr) payable upon marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the original Hanafi school, why was the usurper not liable for usufruct?
A) Because usufruct is not considered valuable property in itself without a contract.
B) Because the usurper never intended to pay for benefits.
C) Because the property is returned undamaged.
D) Because the owner should have rented it out.

Key Takeaway
Later Hanafi rulings may supersede earlier qiyas-based rulings when social corruption undermines the original rationale, but definitive texts remain unchanged.

Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Corruption of the Age
Legal philosophy: how rulings change due to corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ijtihadi rulings may change due to widespread corruption, using the examples of a judge's personal knowledge and hiring for religious duties.

Core Idea
Corruption of the age can justify changing ijtihadi rulings to prevent greater harm.

This lesson explores how later Hanafi jurists modified two established rulings when they observed widespread corruption among judges and declining motivation for religious duties. The change was not an abandonment of principles but a practical application of maqasid al-shari'ah to prevent greater evils. By understanding these examples, you will see how fiqh adapts to social realities while remaining rooted in original sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two original rulings
State what the original Hanafi position was for: (1) a judge using his personal knowledge, and (2) hiring someone for a religiously obligatory duty.
Both were originally permitted or not allowed as per the original school.
2
Identify the change and its cause
Note how later scholars (المتأخرون) changed each ruling due to 'corruption of the age' (فساد الزمان). For the judge: widespread bribery and dishonesty. For religious duties: decline in motivation and loss of state stipends.
The cause is a change in societal conditions, not textual abrogation.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
Explain why each change was made: the original ruling would lead to greater harm (mafsada) under the corrupted circumstances. The new ruling blocks a means to falsehood (sadd al-dhara'i') or ensures the performance of an obligation.
This is talil (causal reasoning) based on maqasid.
4
Extract the governing qawida
Identify the maxim: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الزمان' (Rulings change with changing times). Relate it to this lesson.
This is a recognized usul principle in Hanafi school.
5
Apply to fatwa hierarchy
Determine which position is mufta bihi today: the later opinion (prohibition of personal knowledge, allowance of payment for religious duties) is operative.
Later scholars' fatwa prevails due to changed circumstances.

Chapter (77) Change of ijtihadi rulings due to corruption of the age. The people. And upon this the fatwa and judiciary in the school became settled (1). In the original Hanafi school and others, the judge judges by his personal knowledge in incidents, meaning that his knowledge of the disputed facts is sufficient as basis for his judgment and relieves the claimant from proving his claim by evidence, so the judge's knowledge of the reality is the evidence. There are judgments reported from Umar and others regarding this. But later it was observed that judges were overcome by corruption and evil and taking bribes, and the most trustworthy, chaste, and capable were no longer chosen for the judiciary, but rather those most ingratiating to the rulers, seeking to please them, and persistent in seeking employment. Therefore, the later scholars gave fatwa that it is not valid for a judge to judge by his personal knowledge in incidents, but rather his judgment must be based on evidence established in the court session. Even if the judge himself witnesses a contract, a loan, or an incident between two people outside the court session, then one of them claims it and the other denies it, the judge may not rule for the claimant without evidence; for if that were permissible after the consciences of many judges became corrupt, they would falsely claim knowledge of incidents, favoring the stronger or the one who gives a bribe. So this prohibition, even if it causes loss of some rights due to lack of proof, prevents much falsehood. (1) It is noted here that Article 71 of the Ottoman Family Rights Law, which was in force with us, obliged the wife to follow her husband wherever he wished, reverting to the original school because the husband is more aware of his place of livelihood. The reason for this reversion is that the aforementioned law, in its Article 130, opened for the wife a way of relief by requesting judicial separation if she was not pleased to stay with her husband. Then Article 70 of the Personal Status Law issued in 1953 CE with us decided to force the wife to travel with her husband unless otherwise stipulated in the contract, or the judge finds an impediment to travel. 947 [PAGE_887] The ninth section: Theory of custom. And thus the practice of the later scholars settled on the non-enforcement of a judge's judgment by his knowledge. (1): However, the judge may rely on his knowledge in matters of hisbah (public order) and precautionary administrative measures, such as if he knows that a woman has become irrevocably divorced while cohabitation continues between her and her husband, or if he knows of usurpation of property, then he may separate the man from his divorced wife and place the usurped property with a trustee until proof is established. 6/77 (2) Among the established principles in the original school: a duty required by Shari'ah on a person cannot be hired out, and he is not allowed to take payment for it. Accordingly, if the usurper refuses to return the usurped property to its place except for payment, and the victim pays it, the usurper does not deserve it; rather it is taken back from him. Similarly, if the wife refuses to perform the household duties that are religiously obligatory on her, and the husband hires her for it, she does not deserve the wage. Among the branches of this fiqh principle: performing worship and religious duties that are obligatory, such as leading prayer, delivering the Friday sermon, teaching the Quran and knowledge, it is not permissible to take payment for them in the original school; rather the capable person should do them for free because it is a religious duty. See al-Bada'i' conditions of the subject matter in leasing 4/191-192. However, the later scholars of the school observed the decline of motivation for these duties and the cessation of stipends from the state treasury to scholars, which compelled them to seek earnings, so much so that performing these duties became unguaranteed except with payment.
Source pages p. 886

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on how ijtihadi rulings change due to corruption of the age (فساد الزمان). The author presents two key examples from Hanafi fiqh where original rulings were modified by later scholars (المتأخرون) due to widespread corruption and changing circumstances.

First: The ruling on a judge using his personal knowledge (علمه الشخصي). In the original Hanafi school, a judge could base his judgment on his own knowledge of the facts, even without formal evidence. This was based on practice of the Companions, including Umar. However, when corruption among judges became prevalent, later scholars ruled that a judge cannot use personal knowledge because of the risk of false claims and bias. They required strict evidence in court. An exception remains for hisbah (public order) matters where the judge may act on his knowledge.

Second: The principle that religiously obligatory acts (واجب ديني) cannot be hired out for payment. This includes returning usurped property, marital duties, and religious duties like leading prayer, delivering Friday sermon, teaching Quran. In the original school, no wage is allowed. However, due to declining motivation and loss of state stipends, later scholars allowed taking payment to ensure these duties are performed.

Both examples illustrate how fiqh adapts to social realities while maintaining principles. The author notes that this change is not arbitrary but based on the maqasid (objectives) of preventing greater harm.

Key Terms
قضاء القاضي بعلمه (qada' al-qadi bi-'ilmihi): A judge's reliance on his own personal knowledge of facts outside the courtroom as sufficient evidence for judgment, without requiring formal evidence from litigants.
فساد الزمان (fasad al-zaman): The corruption of the age or times, meaning widespread moral decay and dishonesty among people, especially officials like judges, affecting the validity of their actions.
الواجب الديني (al-wajib al-dini): A religious duty that is obligatory on a specific person or generally, such as returning usurped property, marital duties, and acts of worship like leading prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars, why is it prohibited for a judge to use his personal knowledge?
A) Because the original school did not allow it.
B) Because corruption among judges made it likely to be abused.
C) Because it contradicts the Quran.
D) Because it violates ijma.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurisprudence allows ijtihadi rulings to change when the age becomes corrupt, provided it serves maqasid and prevents greater harm.

Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Corruption of Times
Explaining how late Hanafi jurists modified rulings due to moral decline

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of fasād al-zamān to identify when late Hanafi jurists modified earlier rulings and explain the rationale based on the preservation of maqasid.

Core Idea
When times corrupt, rulings change not by abandoning principles but by applying them to new realities.

One of the most misunderstood aspects of Hanafi fiqh is its flexibility. In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa demonstrates how later Hanafi jurists (al-muta’akhkhirūn) modified specific rulings—on witness uprightness, moon sighting, and lost property—not because they rejected the original texts, but because moral decline necessitated a fresh application of the Sharia's higher objectives. Let's walk through how this principle works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Each example begins with a gap between the strict classical condition and practical reality due to moral decline. For instance, absolute uprightness (al-‘adālah al-muṭlaqah) became rare.
2
Recognize the Response
The late jurists did not deny the classical condition; they offered a practical accommodation to prevent loss of rights or knowledge, such as accepting relative uprightness (al-‘adālah al-nisbiyyah).
3
Extract the Principle
The underlying principle is that ijtihadi rulings can change with changing custom if the change serves the Lawgiver's purpose of bringing benefit and repelling harm.
4
Apply the General Rule
This is not a rejection of the Sunnah but a recognition that some Prophetic rulings were contingent on the moral state of the people, as seen in Uthman's change regarding lost camels.

(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب العلم باب الغضب في الموعظة والتعليم إذا رأى ما يكره (۹۱) عن زيد بن خالد الجهني أن النبي سأله رجل عن ضالة الإبل، فغضب حتى احمرت وجنتاه فقال : ومالك ولها؟ معها سقاؤها ،وحِذاؤها، تَرِدُ الماء، وترعى الشجر، فذرها حتى يلقاها ربها ورواه في اللقطة (٢٤٣٦) ، ومسلم رقم (۱۷۲۲) في اللقطة. قال ابن الأثير في جامع الأصول» ۷۰۲/۱۰ : إنما شدد في ضالة الإبل بقوله : معها حذاؤها، وهو ما تطأ به الأرض من خفها لأنه أراد أنها تقوى به على قطع الأرض. وقوله : (سقاؤها أراد أنها تقوى على ورود المياه ورعي الشجر والامتناع من السباع المفترسة وكذا ما كان في معنى الإبل من البقر والخيل والحمير. انتهى . . 90.
Chapter (77): Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Corruption of Times. Therefore, the late jurists gave a fatwa permitting taking wages for [teaching Quran] out of concern for teaching the Quran, spreading knowledge, and establishing religious rituals among people. 7/77 - W: The witnesses upon whose testimony judgment is passed in incidents must be upright (udul), i.e., trustworthy, those who preserve religious obligations, known for truthfulness and honesty. Indeed, the uprightness of witnesses is a condition stipulated by the Quran for accepting their testimony, and the Sunnah affirmed it and the jurists of Islam agreed upon it. However, the late jurists among us observed the scarcity of complete uprightness as interpreted by the texts, due to the corruption of time, weakness of consciences, and the decline of deterrent religious sentiment. If judges always required the full measure of legal uprightness in witnesses, rights would be lost due to the impossibility of proof. So they gave a fatwa accepting the testimony of the best then the best (al-amthal fa-al-amthal) among the people where complete uprightness is rare. The meaning of al-amthal fa-al-amthal: the best in situation among those present, even if in himself he is not completely upright by its legal limit, meaning they waived the requirement of absolute uprightness to relative uprightness. (See: Mu'in al-Hukkam, Section Two, Chapter 22, p. 145). 8/77 - Z: Likewise, the late jurists gave a fatwa regarding establishing the new moons for fasting Ramadan and for the two Eids by accepting the sighting of two persons, even if there is no obstacle to sighting from clouds, fog, or dust, whereas in the original Hanafi school the crescent moon is not established when the sky is clear except by the sighting of a large crowd, because most people seek to see it. The claim of two individuals of seeing it is a cause of error or doubt. The late jurists justified accepting the sighting of two by the decline of people's motivation to seek the crescent moon, so the sighting of two of them is no longer a cause of error if there is no doubt or accusation in their testimony that leads to suspicion. (See: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym, and Treatise on Custom and Habit by Professor Abu Sannah, pp. 111-112). 949 [Page 889] Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. 9/77 - These are a few examples of many issues in which juristic opinions, fatwas, and judicial practice changed, not due to a difference in juristic views and principles upon which the initial rulings were built, but due to change of time and corruption of public morals, such as the decline of enthusiasm for duties, corruption of consciences in transactions, prevalence of injustice, weakness of religious deterrent against consuming rights falsely, etc. Even the rulings contained in the Prophetic Sunnah themselves, if some of them are built upon consideration of people's conditions and morals in the era of prophethood, then their conditions changed and their morals corrupted, it is necessary to change the prophetic ruling accordingly to what is in accordance with the Lawgiver's purpose of bringing benefits, repelling harms, and safeguarding rights. Upon this principle the noble Companions proceeded after the era of prophethood. It is reported in Sahih al-Bukhari and others (1) that the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, was asked about a lost camel: should the one who sees it pick it up to announce it and return it to its owner when he appears, like a lost sheep and similar small things that are feared for? The Prophet forbade picking it up because it is not feared for what other things are feared for of loss, and he ordered to leave it: it comes to water and grazes on grass until its owner meets it. This ruling remained preserved until the end of Umar's era. Then when it was the era of Uthman ibn Affan, he ordered the picking up of lost camels and their sale. (1) Narrated by Bukhari in the Book of Knowledge, Chapter: Anger in Preaching and Teaching when One Sees What He Dislikes (91) from Zayd ibn Khalid al-Juhani: A man asked the Prophet about a lost camel, and he became angry until his cheeks reddened and said: 'What have you to do with it? It has its water-skin and its shoes; it comes to water and eats trees; leave it until its owner meets it.' Also narrated by him in the Book of Lost Property (2436), and Muslim no. (1722) in the Book of Lost Property. Ibn al-Athir said in Jami' al-Usul (10/702): He was strict regarding the lost camel by saying 'it has its shoes,' meaning what it walks on with its hooves, because he meant that it is strong enough to traverse the land. And his saying 'its water-skin' meant that it is strong enough to come to water sources, graze on trees, and defend against predatory beasts, and likewise what is in the meaning of camel of cattle, horses, and donkeys. End quote. 90.
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses the principle that ijtihadi rulings may change due to the corruption of times (fasād al-zamān). The author is a contemporary Hanafi legal scholar, and the book is a modern introduction to fiqh methodology. The passage presents three concrete examples from Hanafi practice where later jurists (al-muta’akhkhirūn) modified earlier rulings in response to moral decline. First, they permitted taking wages for teaching Quran—originally prohibited because teaching Quran should be for reward from Allah—in order to preserve the institution of Quranic education. Second, they relaxed the strict requirement of complete uprightness (canonical justice) for witnesses, accepting instead relative uprightness (al-adālah al-nisbiyyah) by choosing the best available (al-amthal fa-al-amthal). This was to prevent loss of rights due to the scarcity of fully upright people. Third, they allowed the sighting of the crescent moon to be established by the testimony of only two persons even on a clear night, whereas the earlier Hanafi position required a large multitude because most people would be looking; the later view accepted that people's diligence had waned, so two trustworthy witnesses suffice. The passage then generalizes: these changes are not due to differences in legal principles but solely to the change of times and corruption of public morals. It even argues that certain rulings from the Prophetic Sunnah itself were based on the conditions of the Prophet's era, and if those conditions change, the ruling must change to uphold the Sharia's objectives of bringing benefit and repelling harm. The Companions, notably Uthman, applied this by ordering the picking up and sale of lost camels—a reversal of the Prophet's explicit prohibition—because in Uthman's time the trustworthiness of people had declined and leaving camels led to their loss. The lesson is a clear exposition of the Hanafi legal theory's dynamism and its reliance on customary change as a valid cause for modifying non-textual rulings.

Key Terms
فساد الزمان (fasād al-zamān): The corruption of the era, referring to the moral and religious decline in society that necessitates modification of some ijtihadi rulings.
العدالة المطلقة (al-‘adālah al-muṭlaqah): Absolute uprightness: the strict requirement of complete religious integrity in a witness as defined by classical texts.
العدالة النسبية (al-‘adālah al-nisbiyyah): Relative uprightness: accepting the best available witness among the people of the locality, even if not fully upright by the strict standard.
المتأخرون (al-muta’akhkhirūn): The late jurists (usually from around the 7th century AH onward) who adapted rulings to changed circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did late Hanafi jurists accept the testimony of 'the best then the best' (al-amthal fa-al-amthal) rather than requiring fully upright witnesses?
A) Because the Quran explicitly allows this.
B) Because complete uprightness became extremely rare due to corruption of times, and insisting on it would cause loss of rights.
C) Because the Prophet's Companions did so.
D) Because the Hanafi school changed its principles.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurisprudence permits modification of ijtihadi rulings when the moral condition of society deteriorates, as long as the change upholds the Sharia's purposes of justice and prevention of harm.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Change of Rulings: The Case of Stray Camels
Legal-philosophical example of ruling change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-zaman' to identify when a ruling can change due to corrupt times, and evaluate the precedent of Caliph Uthman.

Core Idea
Rulings based on social conditions change when those conditions change — even if they originate from a prophetic command.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Hanafi legal theory: that ijtihadi rulings, and even some rulings derived from prophetic texts, may change when the social and moral conditions that underpin them deteriorate. We examine the famous example of stray camels, where the Prophet prohibited picking them up, but Caliph Uthman later allowed it due to widespread dishonesty. This case illustrates how the Shariah's higher objectives (maqasid) can override the literal application of a text when circumstances shift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling and its basis
State the prophetic prohibition on picking up stray camels and the reason given: camels can survive on their own, so it was better to leave them until the owner found them.
The ruling was based on the assumption of trustworthiness and safety of property.
2
Identify the changed condition
Describe the moral decay that occurred by the time of Uthman: theft, dishonesty, and exploitation became widespread.
This change made the original ruling counterproductive.
3
Identify the new ruling and its justification
State that Uthman ordered the collection and sale of stray camels, preserving the proceeds for the owner. Explain that this was not a rejection of the Prophet's command but an application of its true purpose: protecting property.
The new ruling aligns with the maqsad of hifz al-mal.
4
Extract the governing qa'ida
Articulate the maxim: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الزمان' (It is not denied that rulings change with change of times).
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, complementing 'العادة محكمة'.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Consider how this principle applies to contemporary mu'amalat, such as lost property in a high-crime area or digital assets in a corrupt society.
The mufti must assess whether the underlying 'illah (cause) still holds.

Source Text

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
۹/۷۷ - هذه أمثلة قليل من كثير من المسائل التي تغيرت فيها الآراء الفقهية والفتاوى وعمل القضاء، لا لاختلاف في الأنظار والمبادىء الفقهية التي بُنيت عليها الأحكام الأولى، بل بسبب تغير الزمان وفساد الأخلاق العامة، كقعود الهمم عن الواجبات وفساد الذمم في المعاملات، وفشو الظلم، وضعف الوازع الديني عن أكل الحقوق بالباطل إلخ...
حتى إن الأحكام الواردة في السنة النبوية نفسها إذا كان منها شيء مبنياً على رعاية أحوال الناس وأخلاقهم في عصر النبوة، ثم تبدلت أحوالهم وفسَدَت أخلاقهم، وجب تبديل الحكم النبوي تبعاً لذلك إلى ما يوافق غرض الشارع في جلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد وصيانة الحقوق. وعلى هذا المبدأ سار الصحابة الكرام بعد عصر النبوة.
فقد ورد في صحيح البخاري وغيره (۱) أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام سُئِلَ عن ضالَّةِ الإبل هل يلتقطها من يراها لتعريفها وردها على صاحبها متى ظهر كضالة الغنم ونحوها من الأشياء الصغيرة التي يخشى عليها)، فنهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن التقاطها لأنها لا يخشى عليها ما يخشى على غيرها من الضياع، وأمر بتركها تَرِدُ الماء وترعى الكلاً حتى يلقاها ربها. وقد ظل هذا الحكم محافظاً عليه إلى آخر عهد عمر.
فلما كان عهد عثمان بن عفان أمَرَ بالتقاط ضوال الإبل وبيعها، على خلاف ما أمر به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فإذا جاء صاحبها أعطي ثمنها، روى ذلك مالك عن ابن شهاب الزهري ذلك لأنَّ عثمان رأى أنَّ الناس قد دبَّ إليهم فساد الأخلاق والذمم وامتدت أيديهم إلى الحرام، فهذا التدبير أصْوَن لضالة الإبل وأحفظ لحق صاحبها خوفاً من أن تنالها يد سارق أو طامع . فهو بذلك - وإن خالف أمر رسول الله في الظاهر - إنما هو موافق المقصوده، إذ لو بقي العمل على موجب ذلك الأمر بعد فساد الزمان لآل إلى عكس مراد النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في صيانة الأموال، وكانت نتيجته ضرراً در تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي إخراج كلية الشريعة في الأزهر طبعة مطبعة وادي الملوك ص / ٤٨) .
(۱) رواه مالك في الموطأ ٧٥٩/٢ في الأقضية أنه سمع ابن شهاب يقول : كانت ضوال الإبل في زمن عمر رضي الله عنه : إبلاً مؤبلة تناتج، لا يمسها أحد حتى إذا كان زمن عثمان بن عفان، أمر بتعريفها ثم تباع فإذا جاء صاحبها أعطي ثمنها. قال ابن الأثير في جامع الأصول» ۷۱۱/۱۰: إبلاً مؤئلة، إذا كانت الإبل مهملة .
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom. 9/77 - These are a few examples among many issues where juristic opinions, fatwas, and judicial practice changed, not due to differences in the juristic views and principles upon which the initial rulings were built, but due to change of time and corruption of public morals, such as laziness in fulfilling duties, corruption of consciences in transactions, prevalence of injustice, weakness of religious deterrent from consuming rights unjustly, etc. Even the rulings found in the Prophetic Sunnah themselves, if any of them were built on consideration of people's conditions and morals in the prophetic era, then their conditions changed and their morals became corrupt, it becomes necessary to change the prophetic ruling accordingly to what agrees with the Lawgiver's purpose of bringing benefits, repelling harms, and safeguarding rights. The noble Companions followed this principle after the prophetic era. It is reported in Sahih al-Bukhari and others that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about stray camels: should the one who sees them pick them up to identify them and return them to their owner when he appears, like stray sheep and other small items that are feared for? The Prophet forbade picking them up because they are not feared for what is feared for other things, and he ordered to leave them: they go to water and graze grass until their owner meets them. This ruling remained preserved until the end of Umar's caliphate. When it was the time of Uthman ibn Affan, he ordered the picking up of stray camels and their sale, contrary to what the Messenger of God commanded, and when their owner came, he would be given their price. Malik narrated that from Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri, because Uthman saw that corruption of morals and consciences had crept into people, and their hands had extended to the unlawful. This measure was more protective for the stray camel and more safeguarding of its owner's right, for fear that it might fall into the hand of a thief or a greedy person. Thus, by doing so - although he apparently opposed the command of the Messenger of God - he was actually in agreement with his purpose, for if action had continued according to that command after the corruption of time, it would have led to the opposite of what the Prophet intended regarding the protection of property, and its result would have been harm.
Source pages p. 889

Commentary
This passage presents a legal-philosophical principle: ijtihadi rulings can change when the underlying social conditions and moral fabric of society deteriorate. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, illustrates this with the example of stray camels. Initially, the Prophet (ﷺ) prohibited picking up stray camels because they could survive on their own with their hooves and ability to find water and pasture. This ruling was based on the assumption that people were trustworthy and the camels were safe from theft. During the caliphate of Umar, the ruling remained unchanged. However, by the time of Uthman, public morals had decayed: people became dishonest, theft increased. Uthman observed that leaving camels unattended now led to their loss, contrary to the Prophet's objective of preserving property. Therefore, he ordered that stray camels be collected, identified, and sold, with the proceeds kept for the owner. Although this contradicted the explicit prophetic command, it was in harmony with the Shariah's higher purpose of protecting wealth. The author emphasizes that this change was not due to differing juristic principles but due to the change in time and morals. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that fatwas may change with changing customs and times, as long as the underlying objectives (maqasid) remain intact.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom or prevailing practice that influences legal rulings.
ضالة الإبل (daallat al-ibil): Stray camels that have wandered away from their owner.
تغير الأحكام (taghayyur al-ahkam): Change of legal rulings due to change of circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Caliph Uthman change the ruling on stray camels from the Prophet's prohibition to order picking and selling?
A) He forgot the Prophet's command.
B) He wanted to increase state revenue.
C) People's morals became corrupt and camels were at risk of theft.
D) The camel population increased too much.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihadi rulings may change when the underlying social conditions that they were based on change, even if the ruling originates from a prophetic command, as long as the change aligns with the Shariah's objectives.

Change of Ijtihad Rulings Due to Corruption of Time
Historical example illustrating the principle of changing rulings due to fasād al-zamān

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi' to identify when a change in societal conditions justifies a change in a fatwa ruling.

Core Idea
When the reason for a ruling no longer exists due to moral decay, the ruling may change to preserve the Shari'ah's objective.

This lesson examines a classical example from the time of Caliph Uthman to illustrate a fundamental principle of iftāʾ: ijtihad rulings may change when the underlying cause (ʿilla) disappears due to corruption of time (fasād al-zamān). You will learn how to identify such situations and apply the relevant maxim in your own fatwa work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling and its cause
In the time of the Prophet and Caliph Umar, stray camels were left untouched because people were trustworthy. The ruling was based on trust (amāna).
2
Identify the change in society
Caliph Uthman observed moral corruption and dishonesty; people began stealing. The cause (ʿilla) of trust no longer existed.
3
Identify the new ruling
Uthman ordered the camels to be identified, sold, and the proceeds kept for the owner. This outward change contradicted the earlier command.
4
Explain how the new ruling achieves the original objective
Both rulings aim to protect the owner's property. The first did so by leaving them; the second did so by selling them to prevent theft.
5
State the maxim and its implications for modern fatwa
The maxim 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi wujūdan wa ʿadaman' applies. A mufti must consider societal changes and may modify rulings when the cause changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) الفصل (۷۷) تغير الأحكام الاجتهادية بفساد الزمان
خلاف ما أمر به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فإذا جاء صاحبها أعطي ثمنها، روى ذلك مالك عن ابن شهاب الزهري ذلك لأنَّ عثمان رأى أنَّ الناس قد دبَّ إليهم فساد الأخلاق والذمم وامتدت أيديهم إلى الحرام، فهذا التدبير أصْوَن لضالة الإبل وأحفظ لحق صاحبها خوفاً من أن تنالها يد سارق أو طامع . فهو بذلك - وإن خالف أمر رسول الله في الظاهر - إنما هو موافق المقصوده، إذ لو بقي العمل على موجب ذلك الأمر بعد فساد الزمان لآل إلى عكس مراد النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في صيانة الأموال، وكانت نتيجته ضرراً در تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي إخراج كلية الشريعة في الأزهر

Chapter 77: Change of Ijtihad Rulings Due to Corruption of Time. Contrary to what the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) commanded... So when its owner came, he was given its price. Malik narrated that from Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri: that Uthman saw that moral corruption and dishonesty had crept among people and their hands had extended to the unlawful. So this measure is safer for the stray camel and more protective of its owner's right, for fear that a thief or greedy person might take it. So by this, even though it outwardly contradicts the command of the Messenger of Allah, it is actually in agreement with its purpose. For if the practice had continued according to that command after the corruption of time, it would have led to the opposite of what the Prophet (ﷺ) intended in safeguarding property, and the result would have been harm. (From: History of Islamic Fiqh, College of Sharia, al-Azhar, Wadi al-Muluk Press, p. 48). (1) Malik narrated in al-Muwatta 2/759 in al-Aqdiya that he heard Ibn Shihab say: The stray camels in the time of Umar (رضي الله عنه) were left alone, breeding freely, no one touched them, until the time of Uthman ibn Affan, who ordered them to be identified, then sold, and when the owner came, he was given their price. Ibn al-Athir in Jami' al-Usul 10/711 said: 'Iblan mu'abbala' means camels left abandoned.
Source pages p. 890

Commentary
This passage presents a historical example of how a ruling based on ijtihad can change due to corruption of time (fasād al-zamān). The original ruling, from the Prophet's time, was that stray camels should not be touched or sold; they were left free until their owner came. This was based on trustworthiness of people. However, when society became corrupt, Caliph Uthman changed the ruling: he ordered stray camels to be publicly announced and then sold, with the proceeds kept for the owner. This outwardly contradicted the Prophet's command, but the author explains it actually fulfilled the Prophet's objective of protecting property. The key point: when the reason (ʿilla) for a ruling disappears due to changed circumstances, the ruling itself may change to preserve the maqṣad (objective). The author uses the maxim 'the ruling revolves with its cause' (al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi) implicitly. This is a classic Hanafi principle in iftāʾ: a mufti must consider societal changes when issuing fatwas.

Key Terms
فساد الزمان (fasād al-zamān): Moral and ethical corruption of society that undermines trust and honesty.
العلة (al-ʿilla): The effective cause or rationale upon which a ruling is based. When the ʿilla changes, the ruling may change.
المقصد (al-maqṣad): The objective or purpose of a ruling. In this context, the maqṣad of protecting property is achieved even when the outward ruling changes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Caliph Uthman change the ruling on stray camels?
A) He wanted to increase state revenue.
B) He saw that moral corruption made the original ruling counterproductive.
C) He received a new revelation.
D) He followed the opinion of another companion.

Key Takeaway
Rulings based on custom or prevalent morals may change when those morals deteriorate, as long as the Shari'ah objective is preserved.

Change of Rulings Due to Evolution of Means: The Example of Writing Hadith
Example of prohibition of writing hadith and later decision to write due to changed circumstances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qāʿida 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi' to explain the change in ruling on writing hadith and identify when a change in circumstances justifies a new fatwa.

Core Idea
When the 'illa (cause) changes, the ruling changes – the prohibition on writing hadith became an obligation once the fear of mixing with Quran vanished.

This passage uses a pivotal historical example – the Prophet's prohibition on writing hadith and its later reversal – to illustrate how ijtihādī rulings evolve with changing conditions. As muftīs, you must master this principle to issue sound fatwās in new contexts. The maxim 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi' is one of the five major qawāʿid kubrā.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and its cause
Pinpoint the original ruling (prohibition of writing hadith) and the specific 'illa (fear of mixing with the Quran) as stated in the hadith and historical context.
The 'illa is explicitly mentioned: 'khashyatu an takhtalita bi al-Qur'ān'.
2
Identify the change in circumstance
Recognize that by the end of the first century, the Quran was widely memorized and recorded, eliminating the earlier danger of confusion. This is the objective change that triggers re-evaluation.
The text notes: 'lammā ʿamma al-Qur'ān shāʿa ḥifẓan wa kitābatan'.
3
Apply the qāʿida to the scenario
State the maxim: 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi thubūtan wa intifā'an'. Since the 'illa has ceased, the ruling cannot remain. The prohibition is lifted.
4
Derive the new ruling
With the 'illa gone, the new ruling is determined by the new need: preserving the Sunnah. Writing hadith becomes wajib (obligatory) as the only means to prevent loss.
The text says: 'bal aṣbaḥat kitābatuha wājibatan'.
5
Generalize the principle for fatwa
Understand that any ijtihādī ruling based on a specific 'illa is subject to change when that 'illa changes. The mufti must continuously assess the ongoing relevance of causes.
This is a core tool in the mufti's methodology for navigating nawāzil.

Chapter 78: Change of Ijtihad Rulings Due to Evolution of Means and Difference of Conditions. 1/78 - a - In the past: It is established that the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade writing his hadith and said to his companions: 'Whoever writes from me other than the Quran, let him erase it.' The Companions and Followers continued to transmit the Sunnah orally, not writing it, until the end of the first century AH, acting on this prohibition. Then at the beginning of the second century, scholars turned to compiling the Sunnah by order of the just caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (رضي الله عنه), because they feared it would be lost with the death of its memorizers. They saw that the reason for the Prophet's prohibition was his fear that it would be mixed with the Quran, since the Companions used to write what was revealed on pieces of leather. When the Quran became widespread in memorization and writing, and there was no longer fear of mixing it with hadith, there remained no reason not to write the Sunnah; rather, its writing became obligatory because it was the only way to preserve it from loss. It has preceded that the ruling revolves with its cause in existence and non-existence. 2/78 - b - In the present era: 1 - Before the establishment of official land registries that define properties... (1) Narrated by Muslim in al-Zuhd (3004), Ahmad in al-Musnad 1/171, and al-Darimi in al-Muqaddima 1/98. 953.
Source pages p. 891

Commentary
This passage discusses another scenario where an ijtihad ruling changes due to evolution of means (taṭawwur al-wasā'il). Initially, the Prophet forbade writing his hadith to prevent confusion with the Quran. As long as the Quran was still being recorded on materials and risked mixing with hadith, the prohibition remained. When the Quran became firmly established and written down widely, the 'illa (reason) for the prohibition disappeared. Therefore, the ruling changed: it became obligatory to write the Sunnah to preserve it. The author explicitly cites the legal maxim: 'al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi thubūtan wa-intifā'an' (the ruling revolves with its cause in existence and non-existence). This is a fundamental Hanafi qāʿida (kubra) used in fatwa methodology. The second part begins to discuss modern issues like official land registries, showing how conditions change, but the passage cuts off.

Key Terms
الحكم يدور مع علته (al-ḥukm yadūru maʿa ʿillatihi): A legal ruling is contingent upon its effective cause; when the cause exists the ruling exists, and when it ceases the ruling ceases.
تطور الوسائل (taṭawwur al-wasā'il): Evolution of means and tools that affect the application of rulings.

A) To preserve oral tradition.
B) Fear that it would be mixed with the Quran.
C) Lack of writing materials.
D) Hadith was not important enough to write.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on writing hadith demonstrates how changes in circumstances can transform a prohibition into an obligation when the underlying cause shifts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Change of Ijtihadi Rulings: The Qawida 'al-Hukm Yaduru Ma'a 'Illatihi'
Exposition of the principle that rulings change with changing means and circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-hukm yaduru ma'a 'illatihi' to identify when an ijtihadi ruling changes due to changing circumstances and evaluate the implications for fatwa.

Core Idea
Ijtihadi rulings change when the underlying cause ('illah) changes — the ruling rotates with its cause.

Legal rulings derived through ijtihad are not static — they respond to the 'illah (cause) that underpins them. When the cause disappears or new means arise, the ruling may transform. This lesson examines two classic examples: the prohibition of writing hadith became obligatory when the fear of mixing with the Quran ceased, and the requirement to mention property boundaries in sale contracts became unnecessary with land registries. These illustrate the powerful Hanafi maxim that the ruling rotates with its cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Governing Qawida
Begin by reading the passage and locating the maxim 'al-hukm yaduru ma'a 'illatihi thubutan wa intifa'an'. Understand that this is a major (kubra) qawida governing ijtihadi rulings.
This qawida is also expressed as 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (rulings are not denied change with changing times).
2
Analyze the First Example: Hadith Writing
Trace the original ruling (prohibition of writing hadith) and its 'illah (fear of mixing with the Quran). Identify when the 'illah ceased (Quran established in writing and memory) and how the ruling changed to obligatory (wajib).
The ruling changed from haram to wajib because the 'illah for prohibition was removed.
3
Analyze the Second Example: Property Boundaries
Examine the requirement to mention boundaries in sale contracts. Identify the 'illah (need to distinguish the property to avoid uncertainty). Recognize that land registries created a new, more effective means of identification, making boundaries unnecessary.
The ruling changed from required (shart) to optional because the 'illah was addressed more completely by registration.
4
Synthesize the Pattern
Generalize: rulings based on temporary causes or specific circumstances change when those causes disappear or when more effective means achieve the same aim. The qawida applies across mu'amalat but not to fixed 'ibadat.
This distinction is critical: only ijtihadi rulings change, not rulings based on definitive texts (nusus qat'iyyah).
5
Apply to Fatwa Practice
Consider how a mufti uses this qawida: when faced with a novel situation, identify the 'illah of the original ruling, verify if it still exists, and determine whether the ruling should update. The burden of proof is to establish the change of 'illah firmly.
The mufti must also consider the madhhab's hierarchy: zahir al-riwayah takes precedence unless the changed circumstance is universally accepted.

Chapter Seventy-Eight: Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Development of Means and Differences of Circumstances. 1/78 - A - In the Past: It is established that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade writing his hadith and said to his companions: 'Whoever writes from me other than the Quran, let him erase it.' The companions and followers continued to transmit the prophetic Sunnah orally, not writing it, until the end of the first century Hijri, acting upon this prohibition. Then scholars at the beginning of the second century, by order of the just Caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (may Allah be pleased with him), turned to compiling the prophetic Sunnah because they feared its loss with the death of its memorizers, and they saw that the reason for the Prophet's prohibition of writing it was only his fear that it would be mixed with the Quran, since the companions used to write what was revealed of it on scraps. When the Quran became widespread in memorization and writing, and there was no longer fear of its mixing with prophetic hadith, there remained no reason for not writing the Sunnah; rather, its writing became obligatory because it is the only way to preserve it from loss. And it has preceded that the ruling rotates with its cause, in existence and non-existence. 2/78 - B - In the Present Age: 1 - Before the establishment of official land registries that define properties and give each a unique number, a contract on a property absent from the contract session required, for its validity, mention of the boundaries of the property, i.e., what adjoins it from the four directions, so that the contracted property is distinguished from others according to what the general rules require of the object of contract being known. But after the establishment of land registries in many kingdoms and countries today, the law suffices in contracts with mention of the property's file number without mentioning its boundaries. And this is what the fiqh of the Sharia requires, because temporal circumstances and regulations have created a new means easier and more complete in specifying and distinguishing the property than mentioning boundaries in property contracts. So stipulating the mention of boundaries became futile, and we have stated that there is no futility in the Sharia. 2 - Likewise, delivery of a sold property to the buyer used to be completed only by vacating the property and actually handing it over to the buyer, or enabling him by handing over its key and the like. If this delivery was not completed, the property remained considered in the hands of the seller, so its destruction would be at his risk and responsibility according to the general fiqh rulings on liability of the sold item before delivery. But after the existence of legal rules that subject property contracts to registration in the land registry, judicial ijtihad has recently settled in our country on considering delivery to have occurred merely by registering the contract in the land registry. So from the date of registration, the liability for destruction of the sold item transfers from the seller to the buyer, because the registration of the sale involves enabling the buyer more than actual delivery, since the criterion in property ownership by law is the entries in the land registry, not possession and dispositions. And by registering the sale, the seller is no longer able to dispose of the sold property by another contract based on its being in his hand, and all rights and claims arising from ownership, such as eviction claims and claims for rent and others, transfer to the buyer upon registration.
Source pages p. 891

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am illustrates the principle that ijtihadi rulings change when the underlying causes and circumstances change. The author provides two historical examples. First, the prohibition on writing hadith: initially the Prophet forbade writing his sayings to prevent confusion with the Quran. When the Quran was safely established in writing and memory, the 'illah (cause) for prohibition ceased, so the ruling changed; writing became obligatory to preserve the Sunnah. Second, in contemporary property law, the requirement to mention boundaries in a sale contract was necessary to identify the property. After the establishment of official land registries with unique property numbers, mentioning boundaries became superfluous, so the ruling changed to allow only the file number. Similarly, actual physical delivery was required for transfer of risk. With registration, delivery is deemed to occur upon recording the contract, because registration provides more complete control to the buyer than physical possession. The author explicitly invokes the maxim 'the ruling rotates with its cause, in existence and non-existence' (al-hukm yaduru ma'a 'illatihi thubutan wa intifa'an). This passage is a clear application of legal philosophy regarding 'urf and changing circumstances in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الأحكام الاجتهادية (al-ahkam al-ijtihadiyyah): Rulings derived through ijtihad based on evidence and reasoning, subject to change with circumstances
علة الحكم (illat al-hukm): The effective cause or reason behind a ruling, which if nonexistent, the ruling changes

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary reason for the Prophet's prohibition of writing hadith, according to this passage?
A) To preserve the Quran from being mixed with hadith
B) To prevent hadith from becoming a written book
C) To encourage oral memorization only
D) Because the companions were illiterate

Key Takeaway
When the 'illah (cause) of a ruling disappears or new means eliminate the need for a condition, the ruling itself changes — a core principle for fatwa in evolving contexts.

Change of Ijtihadi Rulings: Custom vs. Maslaha Mursala
Explaining that change of ijtihadi rulings is from maslaha mursala not custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi view that change of ijtihadi rulings over time is grounded in maslaha mursala, not custom, and apply this to contemporary judicial and worship scenarios.

Core Idea
Many rulings change with time due to necessity or corruption—not custom, but public interest.

Scholars often attribute the evolution of fiqh rulings to changing customs, but this passage offers a deeper analysis. Dr. al-Zarqa argues that the true driver is public interest (maslaha mursala), especially when moral decay or new circumstances render old rulings ineffective. You will explore a key example: the iddah of a woman in an appealed divorce, which reveals why rulings must adapt to achieve Shariah objectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Locate the author's central claim about the basis for changing ijtihadi rulings. Is it custom or something else?
Thesis: 'لا يصح تعتبر من صميم نظرية العرف... بل هي من نظرية المصالح المرسلة'.
2
Analyze the iddah example
Understand why the iddah cannot begin from the initial divorce decree. Trace the logic linking the example to maslaha mursala.
Central analogy: revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) where marriage continues during iddah.
3
Examine Ibn Abidin's maxim
Read the quote from 'Nashr al-Urf' and identify the three reasons for changing rulings: change of custom, necessity, or corruption.
Maxim: 'كثير من الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الزمان...'
4
Evaluate the Hanbali fan example
See how the cause (illah) determines the ruling: manual fan is disliked because it involves movement, electric fan lacks that cause.
This demonstrates that new circumstances require fresh ijtihad.
5
Synthesize the principle
Formulate the operative rule: rulings founded on ijtihad can change when the underlying purpose (maqsad) is no longer served, even if custom hasn't changed.
This is a key tool for fatwa in evolving contexts.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
المرأة في العدة ويبدأ حسابها، لا من وقت صدور الحكم الابتدائي، لأنها لو اعتدت منذ صدور الحكم الابتدائي لربما تنقضي عدتها وتتحرر من آثار الزوجية قبل الفصل في الطعن المرفوع على حكم القاضي الأول بانحلال الزوجية، ثم ينقض هذا الحكم لخلل تراه المحكمة العليا فيه. وهذا النقض يرفع الحكم السابق ويوجب عودة الزوجية.
فكيف يمكن ذلك بعد أن تصبح المرأة متحررة من آثار الزواج بانقضاء عدتها وقد ساغ لها أن تتزوج زوجاً آخر، وقد تكون تزوّجت فعلاً؟! .
لذلك يجب أن يعتبر الحكم الابتدائي الأول بالفرقة كحكم معلق على الإبرام أي يعتبر مشروع (فرقة لا تسري نتائجه، وخاصة منها العدة إلا من بعد صيرورته مبرماً.
وقبل ذلك تبقى الزوجية قائمة بكل نتائجها رغم قضاء القاضي بالفرقة نظراً لتبدل الأوضاع القضائية عما كانت عليه في الماضي عندما كان قضاء القاضي الشرعي يصدر على درجة واحدة مبرماً لا معقب عليه.
ولا محذور في اعتبار الزوجية قائمة قبل انبرام الحكم بالفرقة، بل إنَّ لذلك نظيراً في الشرع يشبهه من هذا الوجه وهو الطلاق الرجعي الذي يوقعه الزوج نفسه. فإن الزوجية تبقى مع الطلاق الرجعي قائمة من كل وجه بين الزوجين رغم الطلاق ما دامت المرأة في العدة، حتى إن المتعة الزوجية تظل حلالاً بينهما وتقع بها الرجعة، وكذا لو مات أحدهما خلال العدة يرثه الآخر. فالطلاق الرجعي في النظام الإسلامي هو كما يقال بلغة العصر: مشروع فرقة قابل للرفع والإلغاء بالمراجعة. فإذا انقضت عدة المرأة دون رجعة من الزوج المطلق فعندئذ تقع البينونة (الفرقة).
فيجب أن يعتبر نظير ذلك بالنسبة إلى بدء عدة المرأة في حالة التطليق القضائي اليوم (١).
٣/۷۸ - فمن مجموع هذه الأمثلة وأشباهها يتضح أن قضية تغير الأحكام لتغير الزمان لا أن يصح تعتبر من صميم نظرية العرف كما يعتبرها بعض الباحثين، بل هي من نظرية المصالح المرسلة. فإن قعود الهمم، وفساد الذمم وقلة الورع، وكثرة الطمع والمستحدثات الجديدة، ليست أعرافاً يتعارفها الناس ويبنون عليها أعمالهم ومعاملاتهم، وإنما هي انحلال في الأخلاق يضعف الثقة أو هي اختلاف في وسائل التنظيم الزمني. وكل ذلك يجعل الأحكام التي أسسها الاجتهاد في ظروف مختلفة عن الظروف الجديدة غير صالحة لتحقيق الغاية الشرعية من تطبيقها فيجب أن تتغير إلى الشكل الذي يتناسب مع الأوضاع القائمة، ويحقق الغاية الشرعية من الحكم الأصلي. وذلك نظير السفينة الشراعية التي تقصد اتجاهاً معيناً في ريح شمالية مثلاً، فإن شراعها يقام على شكل يسير بالسفينة في الاتجاه المطلوب. فإذا انحرف مهب الريح وجب تعديل وضع الشراع إلى شكل يضمن سير السفينة في اتجاهها المقصود وإلا انحرفت أو توقفت .
وقد قال العلامة ابن عابدين رحمه الله في رسالته «نشر العرف ما نصه: كثير من الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الزمان لتغير عرف أهله، أو لحدوث ضرورة أو لفساد أهل الزمان، بحيث لو بقي الحكم على ما كان عليه أولاً للزم منه المشقة والضرر بالناس ولخالف قواعد الشريعة المبنية على التخفيف والتيسير ودفع الضرر والفساد ولهذا ترى مشايخ المذهب خالفوا ما نص عليه المجتهد في مواضع كثيرة بناها على ما كان في زمنه،
(۱) ومثل آخر من العبادات أن الحنابلة قد نصوا على كراهة التروح (أي استعمال المروحة لتلطيف حرارة الجو في الصلاة. فهذا لا يشمل اليوم المرواح الكهربائية التي تحرك الهواء بدون عمل من المصلي ينافي حال الصلاة وهذا مثل جيد لتخلف علة الحكم وهي هنا حركة المصلي.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. The woman in the waiting period (iddah) and its calculation begins not from the time of the initial judgment, because if she observed iddah from the initial judgment, perhaps her iddah would end and she would be free from the effects of marriage before the decision on the appeal against the first judge's ruling of dissolution, then this ruling is overturned due to a defect seen by the higher court. This overturning revokes the previous ruling and necessitates the return of marriage. How can that be after the woman becomes free from the effects of marriage by completing her iddah and it becomes permissible for her to marry another, and she might have actually married? Therefore, the initial judgment of separation must be considered as a judgment suspended on finalization, i.e., considered a 'projected separation' whose effects, especially the iddah, do not take effect except after it becomes final. Before that, the marriage remains in effect with all its consequences despite the judge's ruling of separation, due to the change in judicial circumstances from the past when the religious judge's ruling was issued on one level, final with no appeal. There is no harm in considering the marriage as existing before the finalization of the separation ruling; indeed, there is a parallel in Shariah similar in this regard: revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) issued by the husband himself. For the marriage remains fully in effect between the spouses with revocable divorce despite the divorce as long as the woman is in iddah, even marital relations remain permissible between them and return (raj'ah) occurs through it, and also if one of them dies during iddah, the other inherits from him. Revocable divorce in the Islamic system is, as said in modern language: a projected separation subject to lifting and cancellation by reconciliation. When the woman's iddah ends without return by the divorcing husband, then irrevocable separation occurs. So the parallel must be considered regarding the start of the woman's iddah in the case of judicial divorce today (1). 3/78 - From all these examples and their likes, it becomes clear that the issue of change of rulings due to change of time should not rightly be considered part of the theory of custom as some researchers consider it; rather, it is from the theory of maslaha mursala (unrestricted public interest). For the decline of ambitions, corruption of consciences, lack of piety, abundance of greed, and modern innovations are not customs that people know and base their actions and transactions upon; rather, they are moral decay that weakens trust, or differences in temporal organizational means. All of that makes the rulings founded by ijtihad in circumstances different from the new circumstances unsuitable for achieving the Shariah purpose of their application, so they must change to a form that suits the existing conditions and achieves the Shariah purpose of the original ruling. That is similar to a sailing ship aiming for a certain direction in a north wind, for example: its sail is placed in a form that makes the ship move in the desired direction. If the wind direction shifts, the sail's position must be adjusted to a form that ensures the ship's movement in its intended direction; otherwise, it will deviate or stop. Imam Ibn Abidin (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his treatise 'Nashr al-Urf': 'Many rulings differ with difference of time due to change of the custom of its people, or due to occurrence of necessity, or due to corruption of the people of the time, such that if the ruling remained as it was initially, it would cause hardship and harm to people and would contradict the rules of Shariah built on alleviation, facilitation, and repelling harm and corruption. For this reason, you see the senior scholars of the madhhab opposed what the mujtahid stipulated in many places, based on what was in his time.' (1) And another example from acts of worship: the Hanbalis ruled the dislike of using a fan (i.e., using a fan to cool the air in prayer). This does not include today electric fans that move the air without any action from the praying person that contradicts the state of prayer. This is a good example of the cause (illah) of the ruling being absent, which here is the movement of the praying person.
Source pages p. 894

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam discusses the relationship between custom (urf) and the change of ijtihadi rulings. The author argues that the change of rulings due to changing circumstances is not part of the theory of custom, but rather falls under the theory of maslaha mursala (unrestricted public interest). He illustrates this with the example of a woman's iddah in the case of a divorce decree that is appealed. The initial decree of separation is considered suspended until finalization, so the iddah does not begin until the ruling is final, to avoid the problem of the woman having already completed her iddah and remarried if the decree is later overturned. This is analogous to revocable divorce where the marriage continues during iddah. The author emphasizes that the reasons for changing rulings—such as moral decay, greed, and new inventions—are not customs but factors that necessitate adjusting ijtihadi rulings to achieve the Shariah's objectives. He supports this with a quote from Ibn Abidin stating that many rulings change with time due to changes in custom, necessity, or corruption of the people. The final footnote gives a Hanbali example of using a fan in prayer: the old ruling of dislike does not apply to electric fans because the cause (movement of the worshipper) is absent. This passage is a key exposition of the legal philosophical principle that ijtihadi rulings are subject to change to maintain their effectiveness in achieving Shariah goals.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom; established practice among people in their transactions and dealings
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursalah): Unrestricted public interest; considerations that serve the objectives of Shariah without specific textual evidence
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce during which the husband can take back his wife without a new contract within the iddah period

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author argue that the change of ijtihadi rulings is not based on custom?
A) Because custom (urf) is not a recognized source in the Hanafi school.
B) Because the factors causing change—moral decay, necessity, innovations—are not practices that people voluntarily adopt as customs.
C) Because ijtihadi rulings never change; only explicit texts change.
D) Because custom only applies to transactions (muamalat), not to worship (ibadat).

Key Takeaway
The change of ijtihadi rulings over time is driven by public interest (maslaha mursala) to achieve Shariah purposes, even when custom hasn't changed.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Change of Ijtihadi Rulings: Masalih Mursalah over Urf
Legal philosophy: arguing that rulings change due to masalih mursalah, not just urf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct theoretical basis for the change of ijtihadi rulings over time and apply it to a contemporary case.

Core Idea
Rulings change not just because customs change, but because achieving Shariah's objectives demands adaptation.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges a common assumption: that the evolution of legal rulings over time is simply a matter of changing customs. Using the metaphor of a sailing ship adjusting its sail to the wind, al-Zarqa argues the real driver is the need to fulfill the higher objectives of Shariah (masalih mursalah). Through quotes from Hanafi masters, we see that later jurists adapted rulings to prevent hardship and corruption, even when earlier texts seemed clear. Your goal is to internalize this principle and use it to assess contemporary fatwa applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing views
Note the two theories: some researchers say change is based on urf; al-Zarqa says it's masalih mursalah. Recognize the distinction: urf is habitual practice, while maslaha is a purposive principle aimed at achieving Shariah's goals.
2
Analyze the evidence from Hanafi authorities
Read the quotes from Ibn Abidin, al-Qarafi, and Ibn al-Qayyim. Each emphasizes that rulings must adapt to avoid hardship, corruption, and stagnation, and that this is rooted in the objectives of Shariah, not merely local custom.
Ibn Abidin's 'Nashr al-Urf' explicitly links change to 'corruption of the people of the time' and 'necessity.'
3
Trace the reasoning through the sailing ship metaphor
Al-Zarqa likens the Shariah's objective to the ship's destination, and the ruling to the sail. When the wind (circumstances) changes, the sail must be adjusted (ruling changed) to keep the ship on course. This shows that the ruling is a means, not an end.
The objective (destination) remains fixed; only the means (sail angle/ruling) is adjusted.
4
Extract the underlying legal maxim
From the arguments, formulate the Hanafi maxim: 'Ijtihadi rulings change with change of time, place, conditions, and customs to prevent hardship and achieve public interests.' This is a qawida kubra or wusta governing ijtihad.
See also the known principle: 'It is not denied that rulings change with changing times.'
5
Apply to a modern example
Take the case of electric fans vs hand fans in prayer (Hanbali example in the footnote). The original ruling (karahah for hand fan) was based on the worshipper's movement; electric fans lack that illa, so the ruling changes. Similarly, consider online contracts vs in-person sessions.
This method prevents mechanical application of historical rulings without considering changed means.

Chapter (78): Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Development of Means
3/78 - From the totality of these examples and their likes, it becomes clear that the issue of changing rulings due to change of time should not be considered as part of the theory of custom (urf) as some researchers consider it, but rather it is part of the theory of public interests (masalih mursalah). If the wind direction changes, the position of the sail must be adjusted to a shape that ensures the ship's progress in its intended direction, otherwise it would deviate or stop.

: Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom
Knowing that if he were in their time, he would have said what they said, taking from the rules of his madhhab. End. R. Majmu'at Rasail Ibn Abidin 125/2 and the examples he brought... And Shihab al-Qarafi also said in 'al-Furuq' under al-Farq 28/ the third issue 177/1: Stagnation upon transmitted texts forever is misguidance in religion and ignorance of the objectives of the scholars of the Muslims and the righteous predecessors. End.
And Ibn al-Qayyim (may Allah have mercy on him) said in the chapter 'Change of Fatwa and its Difference with Changing Times, Places, Conditions, Intentions, and Customs': This chapter is extremely beneficial, and due to ignorance of it, great error has occurred against the Shariah, causing hardship, difficulty, and burdening with what is not possible, which the brilliant Shariah that is at the highest rank of interests does not bring. Indeed, the Shariah is built and founded upon wisdom and the interests of the servants in this life and the Hereafter, it is all justice, all mercy, all interests, all wisdom. Every case that departs from justice to oppression, from mercy to its opposite, from interest to (corruption), and from wisdom to absurdity is not from the Shariah, even if it is introduced into it by interpretation, etc.
(27/3) I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, al-Muniriyya edition 1/3 and Faraj Allah Zaki al-Kurdi edition.
4/78 - This is the end of the second part of this introduction, in which we presented those five basic juristic theories that are the major pillars of fiqh and its bridges upon which its structure stands in its various chapters, namely: the theory of ownership, the theory of contracts, the theory of securities, the theory of legal capacity and guardianship, and the theory of custom.
Rarely does a juristic branch exist that is not related to one or more of these major theories, for if it involves the establishment of ownership over an object or a benefit, it is subject...

Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the change of ijtihadi rulings over time. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, establishes that the phenomenon of rulings changing due to time is not merely a matter of custom (urf) as some researchers think, but rather belongs to the theory of masalih mursalah (public interests). He explains that factors such as moral decay, loss of trust, and new inventions are not customs but changes in circumstances that render previous ijtihadi rulings ineffective in achieving the Shariah's objectives. Therefore, rulings must adapt to new conditions to fulfill the original Shariah purposes. He illustrates with the metaphor of a sailing ship adjusting its sail when the wind changes direction.

The author supports his argument with quotes from Hanafi scholar Ibn Abidin (from his treatise 'Nashr al-Urf'), stating that many rulings change due to custom, necessity, or corruption of the times, and that later scholars of the madhhab would contradict earlier rulings if conditions changed, based on the principles of the madhhab. A footnote gives an example: Hanbalis once ruled that using a hand fan in prayer is disliked (because it involves movement), but this does not apply to electric fans today since they require no action from the worshipper. Then al-Qarafi is quoted, warning that rigid adherence to transmitted texts without considering objectives is misguidance. Finally, Ibn al-Qayyim emphasizes that the Shariah is built on justice, mercy, interest, and wisdom, and any ruling that deviates from these is not truly from the Shariah even if claimed otherwise.

The passage concludes by noting that this chapter is the end of the second part of the book, which covered five major juristic theories: ownership, contracts, securities, legal capacity/guardianship, and custom. These are the foundational pillars of fiqh.

Key Terms
الأحكام الاجتهادية (al-ahkam al-ijtihadiyya): Rulings derived through ijtihad by mujtahids based on evidence and principles; these rulings are subject to change over time.
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursalah): Public interests that are not explicitly mentioned in the primary sources but are in harmony with the general objectives of Shariah; used as a basis for rulings when necessary.
العرف (al-urf): Customary practices of people that are sound and do not contradict Shariah; considered a secondary source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct theoretical basis for the change of ijtihadi rulings over time?
A) Change of custom (urf) alone
B) Change of public interests (masalih mursalah)
C) Change of scholarly opinions
D) Change of political authority

Key Takeaway
The change of ijtihadi rulings over time is fundamentally driven by the necessity to fulfill the Shariah's objectives (maqasid), not merely by changes in custom.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
The Theory of Custom and Changing Rulings
Conclusion of five legal theories with emphasis on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that custom (urf) is a legitimate source for legal rulings and explain how it justifies the change of fatwa with changing circumstances.

Core Idea
Strict adherence to transmitted texts without considering custom is ignorance; Shari'ah is based on justice, mercy, and welfare.

This passage concludes the author's presentation of five major legal theories. It emphasizes that Islamic law is not static; custom (urf) and changing circumstances are integral to ijtihad. The author quotes Ibn Abidin, al-Qarafi, and Ibn al-Qayyim to demonstrate that rigidity contradicts the objectives of Shari'ah. Understanding this theory is essential for any mufti or student of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scholarly authorities cited
List the three scholars quoted: Ibn Abidin, al-Qarafi, and Ibn al-Qayyim. Note their key statements about custom and change.
2
Extract the principle from each statement
From Ibn Abidin: if a mujtahid were in the present time, he would adopt current custom. From al-Qarafi: adherence to transmitted texts forever is misguidance. From Ibn al-Qayyim: Shari'ah is based on justice, mercy, benefit, and wisdom; any ruling departing from these is not from Shari'ah.
3
Relate to Hanafi theory of urf
These statements collectively support the Hanafi position that custom (urf) is a valid source for legal rulings when not contradicted by a clear text. Custom can specify general texts and sometimes override qiyas.
See the key term 'al-urf' for more details.
4
Apply to a hypothetical change in custom
If a custom that formed the basis of an earlier ruling changes significantly, a new ijtihad is needed, provided the new custom does not violate a clear nass. This is summed in the maxim 'taghayyur al-fatwa bi taghayyur al-azman'.
For example, changes in trade practices may affect rulings on contracts.
5
Recognize the warning against rigid adherence
Al-Qarafi's warning and Ibn al-Qayyim's criterion ensure that the maqasid (higher objectives) of Shari'ah are upheld. Any ruling that causes hardship or injustice must be re-examined in light of custom and context.
This is not an invitation to disregard texts but to apply their spirit correctly.

Source Text
