الباب التاسع : نظرية العُرف
لعلمهم بأنه لو كان في زمنهم لقال بما قالوا به أخذاً من قواعد مذهبه .اهـ. ر مجموعة رسائل ابن عابدين ۱۲۵/۲ والأمثلة التي أتى بها ... وقال الشهاب القرافي أيضاً في «الفروق» تحت الفرق /٢٨/ المسألة الثالثة ۱۷۷/۱ ما نصه:
الجمود على المنقولات أبداً ضلال في الدين وجهل بمقاصد علماء المسلمين والسلف الماضين اهـ.
ويقول ابن القيم رحمه الله في فصل «تغير الفتوى واختلافها تغير الأزمنة والأمكنة والأحوال والنيات والعوائد :
هذا فصل عظيم النفع جداً، وقد وقع بسبب الجهل به غلط عظيم على الشريعة أوجب من الحرج والمشقة وتكليف ما لا سبيل إليه، ما يعلم أن الشريعة الباهرة التي في أعلى رتب المصالح لا تأتي به. فإن الشريعة مبناها وأساسها على الحكم ومصالح العباد في المعاش والمعاد عدل كلها؛ ورحمة كلها، ومصالح كلها وحكمة كلها. فكل مسألة خرجت من العدل إلى الجور، وعن الرحمة إلى ضدها وعن المصلحة إلى المفسدة، وعن الحكمة إلى العبث فليست من الشريعة وإن أدخلت فيها بالتأويل إلخ .(۲۷/۳ ر إعلام الموقعين طبعة المنيرية ۱/۳ وطبعة فرج الله زكي الكردي ٤/٧٨ - هذا آخر القسم الثاني من هذا المدخل عرضنا فيه تلك النظريات الخمس الفقهية الأساسية التي هي دعائم الفقه الكبرى، وقناطره التي يقوم عليها بنيانه في شتى أبوابه وهي نظرية الملكية، ونظرية العقود، ونظرية المؤيدات، ونظرية الأهلية والولاية، ونظرية العرف.
فقلما يوجد فرع فقهي لا يكون لواحدة فأكثر من هذه النظريات الكبرى علاقة به، فإنه إذا كان فيه ثبوت ملكية في عين أو منفعة خضع لنظرية الملكية وقواعدها. وإذا كان فيه التزام عقدي خضع لنظرية العقود. وإذا كان فيه صحة أو فساد أو بطلان أو توقف خضع لنظرية المؤيدات ونظرية الأهليات وإذا كان في حكمه تأثير للعرف خضع لنظرية العرف. وإذا كان فيه تصرُّف بفعل أو قول أو فيه إثبات حق أو التزام خضع أيضاً لنظرية الأهلية والولاية والعقد.
·
فهذه النظريات الأساسية الكبرى تضرب نطاقاً حول أبواب الفقه وفصوله كلها يحيط بها ويهيمن عليها ويوجه أحكامها، وتمسك كل جهة منه بطرف من هذه الأحكام.
أن ۷۸/٥ - وقد بقيت نظرية سادسة هامة قد كان في مخطط بحثي أعالجها وأتمم بها عقد النظريات الأساسية الكبرى. وتلك هي نظرية الضمانات تعرض فيها المبادىء الفقهية في ضمان الأمول والحقوق والتزاماتها في الذمم وأسباب التضمين ،الشرعية وأنواع الضمان من مستقر ومتحول (وهو الذي يستوجب للضامن الغارم حقاً في أن يرجع بما ضمنه على غيره وعوامل كل نوع وضوابطه وما إلى ذلك من مباحث. ولكني تركت معالجة هذه النظرية الآن نظراً لضيق وقتي ولضيق نطاق هذا الجزء عنها، فقد اتسعت مباحث هذا الجزء إلى أكثر من الحد الذي كنت قدرته لها. والأمر إذا اتسع ضاق !!)، كما انه إذا ضاق اتسع.
والآن ننتقل إلى القسم الثالث هذا المدخل، وهو القسم الأخير من المخصص لعرض القواعد الكلية في الفقه الإسلامي.
(۱) على أنني قد يسر الله لي أن أصدرت قبل هذا الإخراج الجديد للمدخل الفقهي العام) نظرية الفعل الضار ونشرتها في كتاب مستقل دار القلم - دمشق ١٤٠٩ هـ / ۱۹۸۸م)، وهي تغطي جانباً كبيراً من نظرية الضمانات التي لها أسباب أخرى غير الفعل الضار. وربما أعيد نشر نظرية الفعل الضار ضمن المجلد التالي (الثالث) من هذه السلسلة الفقهية .
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. Because they knew that if he (the mujtahid) were in their time, he would have said what they said, taking from the principles of his madhhab. End quote from Majmu'at Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 125/2 and the examples he brought... And Shihab al-Qarafi also said in al-Furuq under the difference /28/ issue three 177/1, his text: 'Adherence to transmitted texts forever is misguidance in religion and ignorance of the objectives of Muslim scholars and the past predecessors.' End quote. And Ibn al-Qayyim (may Allah have mercy on him) says in the chapter 'Change of Fatwa and Its Variation with Change of Times, Places, Circumstances, Intentions, and Customs': 'This chapter is extremely beneficial. Due to ignorance of it, a great error has occurred against the Shari'ah, causing hardship, difficulty, and burdening with what is impossible, while knowing that the brilliant Shari'ah, which is at the highest level of benefits, would not bring such. Indeed, the Shari'ah is built and founded upon wisdom and the welfare of the servants in their worldly and afterlife affairs: it is all justice, all mercy, all interests, and all wisdom. So any issue that departs from justice to injustice, from mercy to its opposite, from benefit to harm, from wisdom to foolishness is not from the Shari'ah, even if it is introduced into it through interpretation, etc.' (I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in, al-Muniriyya edition 1/3, and Farajallah Zaki al-Kurdi edition 4/78). This is the end of the second section of this introduction, in which we presented those five basic legal theories that are the major pillars of fiqh and the bridges upon which its structure stands in its various chapters: the theory of ownership, the theory of contracts, the theory of nullifications (mu'ayyidat), the theory of capacity and guardianship, and the theory of custom. Rarely is there a legal branch that is not related to one or more of these major theories. For if it involves the establishment of ownership in a specific asset or benefit, it is subject to the theory of ownership and its rules. If it involves a contractual obligation, it is subject to the theory of contracts. If it involves validity, corruption, nullity, or suspension, it is subject to the theory of nullifications and the theory of capacities. If its ruling is influenced by custom, it is subject to the theory of custom. If it involves an action or statement or the establishment of a right or obligation, it is also subject to the theory of capacity, guardianship, and contract. These fundamental major theories cast a circle around all the chapters and sections of fiqh, encompassing them, dominating them, directing their rulings, and each aspect of fiqh is held by a part of these rulings. 78/5 - There remained a sixth important theory that I had planned to treat and complete the series of major fundamental theories. That is the theory of guarantees (damanat), in which the legal principles regarding the guarantee of property and rights, their obligations in the dhimmah, the legal causes of liability, and the types of guarantee (stable and transferable) are discussed... But I have left the treatment of this theory now due to lack of time and the limited scope of this part for it, as the discussions of this part have expanded beyond the limit I had set for it. And when things expand, they contract! And also when they contract, they expand. Now we move to the third section of this introduction, which is the last part devoted to presenting the universal principles (qawa'id kulliyya) in Islamic jurisprudence. (1) However, Allah facilitated that before this new edition of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam, I published the Theory of Harmful Act (Nazariyyat al-Fi'l al-Darr) in a separate book (Dar al-Qalam, Damascus 1409 AH/1988 CE), which covers a large part of the theory of guarantees that have other causes besides the harmful act. Perhaps I will republish the Theory of Harmful Act within the following (third) volume of this fiqh series.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage concludes the second section of the book, which presented five major legal theories: ownership, contracts, nullifications, capacity and guardianship, and custom. The author emphasizes that these theories are foundational to all fiqh. He begins with a discussion of the theory of custom (urf), citing three key authorities. First, Ibn Abidin notes that previous scholars would have followed the current custom if they were alive. Second, al-Qarafi warns that rigid adherence to transmitted texts without considering context is misguidance. Third, Ibn al-Qayyim expands on this, stating that Shari'ah is fundamentally based on justice, mercy, benefit, and wisdom; any ruling that violates these principles is not truly from Shari'ah, even if claimed through interpretation. This establishes that custom and changing circumstances are legitimate factors in ijtihad. The author then lists the five theories and how they apply to various fiqh issues. He mentions a planned sixth theory on guarantees (damanat) but defers it due to space constraints, noting that he has already published a related work on the theory of harmful act. Finally, he transitions to the third section, which will present universal qawa'id (legal maxims) in Islamic jurisprudence. This passage is a classic Hanafi statement on the role of custom and the flexibility of Islamic law.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom or customary practice that influences legal rulings when not contradicted by clear text.
تغير الفتوى (taghayyur al-fatwa): Change of legal opinion due to change in times, places, circumstances, intentions, or customs.
نظرية العرف (nazariyyat al-urf): The legal theory that custom is a source of law and a factor in interpreting and applying Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Qayyim as quoted in the passage, what is the basis of Shari'ah?
A) Adherence to transmitted texts
B) Justice, mercy, benefit, and wisdom
C) Following the majority opinion
D) Avoiding innovation at all costs

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is a valid source for legal rulings in Hanafi thought, and fatwa must adapt to changing circumstances to uphold the maqasid of Shari'ah.

The Concept of Khilaf al-Qiyas (Rulings Contrary to Analogy)
Definition and explanation of khilaf al-qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khilaf al-qiyas and identify its place in Hanafi legal methodology.

Core Idea
Khilaf al-qiyas: an exceptional ruling that deviates from the general analogy due to a special legislative reason.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi concept of rulings that are established contrary to analogy (qiyas). Such rulings are exceptions to the general rules that would normally apply by analogy. Recognizing these exceptions is crucial for understanding how Hanafi jurists apply istihsan and other principles.

Guided Path
1
Define khilaf al-qiyas
Extract the definition from the text: a ruling that is given an exceptional hukm contrary to the general rules applicable to its likes due to special legislative considerations.
The term 'خلاف القياس' literally means 'contrary to analogy'.
2
Identify the legislative basis
Note that the exception is not arbitrary; it is based on a specific legislative consideration (اعتبار تشريعي خاص).
This consideration is the illa for the exception.
3
Distinguish from regular qiyas
Understand that khilaf al-qiyas overrides the standard qiyas that would otherwise apply to similar cases.
Standard qiyas would give a different ruling if applied.
4
Recognize its role in Hanafi usul
Khilaf al-qiyas is often linked to istihsan, where a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) is preferred over the apparent analogy (qiyas jali).
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه رأينا في مناسبات عدة أن كثيراً من الأحكام تقرره الشريعة على خلاف القياس، أي تعطيه حكماً استثنائياً على خلاف مقتضى القواعد العامة السارية على أمثاله لوجود اعتبارات تشريعية خاصة به. فالوصية مثلاً تمليك مضاف لحالة ما بعد الموت، وهي الحالة التي تنقطع فيها حقوق الشخص في أمواله وتتعلق بها حقوق ورثته. فالوصية ثابتة على خلاف القياس، لأنَّ الشارع أراد أن يفتح بها للشخص باباً يتدارك به ما فاته من أعمال البر والإحسان التي قد تشحُ بها نفسه حال الحياة خشية من الاحتياج. فلا يجوز أن يقاس عليها تجويز إضافة غيرها من التصرفات إلى ما بعد الموت كالهبة والإعارة، والبيع والإجارة مثلاً؛ بأن يعقدها الشخص في حياته عقداً مضافاً إلى ما بعد الموت أما لو أوصى بأن يباع بعد موته شيء معين من تركته لفلان بنصف قيمته مثلاً، أو أن يعار له، فهذا سائغ لأنه وصية بالبيع أو الإعارة، لا عقد حاضر مضاف الأثر إلى ما بعد الموت. ملاحظة : ويجب أن يلحظ أن ما ثبت على خلاف القياس يمكن أن يقاس عليه في العلة التي خالف فيها القياس كدفع الحرج وسد الذرائع حيثما دعت الحاجة إليهما . هذا وقد قالوا إن أحكام الوقف تستقى بطريق القياس من أحكام الوصية، فتقاس مثلاً مسؤولية متولي الوقف على مسؤولية الأوصياء في تنفيذ الوصية .
Chapter Ten: General Maxims in Fiqh. We have seen on several occasions that many rulings are established by the Sharia contrary to analogy (qiyas), i.e., it gives them an exceptional ruling contrary to the general rules applying to similar cases due to special legislative considerations. For example, a bequest (wasiyyah) is a transfer of ownership attached to a state after death, a state in which a person's rights in his property cease and the rights of his heirs attach to it. So the bequest is established contrary to analogy, because the Lawgiver wanted to open for the person a door through which he can make up for acts of piety and goodness that his soul might have been stingy with during life for fear of need. It is not permissible, therefore, to analogize upon it the permissibility of attaching other dispositions to after death, such as gift (hiba), loan (i'arah), sale, and lease, by the person making a contract during his life attached to after death. However, if he gives instructions (wasiyyah) that after his death a specific item from his estate be sold to so-and-so at half its value, for example, or that it be lent to him, then this is permissible because it is a bequest of sale or loan, not a present contract whose effect is attached to after death. Note: It must be observed that what is established contrary to analogy can be analogized upon in the cause (illa) for which it contradicted analogy, such as removing hardship and blocking means, wherever need arises. And they have said that the rulings of waqf are derived through analogy from the rulings of wasiyyah, so for example, the responsibility of the mutawalli (administrator of waqf) is compared to the responsibility of executors (awsiya') in executing the will.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of rulings that are established contrary to qiyas (analogy) in Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that many Sharia rulings are exceptional and not derivable by general analogy. He uses the example of wasiyyah (bequest) to illustrate: normally, a transfer of property after death is invalid because ownership rights cease upon death, but the Sharia allows bequests as an exception to facilitate acts of charity. This exception cannot be extended by analogy to other contracts like gift or sale, because the rationale (illa) is specific. However, he notes an important principle: the exceptional ruling itself can be used as a precedent for other cases that share the same specific cause (illa), such as removing hardship or blocking means to harm. He also mentions that waqf (endowment) rulings are analogically derived from wasiyyah rulings. This passage is foundational for understanding the role of qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): A ruling that deviates from the general analogy due to a special cause (illa).
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A transfer of ownership attached to death, valid for up to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for a ruling being classified as 'khilaf al-qiyas'?
A) The absence of any textual evidence.
B) The presence of a special legislative consideration.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) The need to follow public interest (maslahah).

Key Takeaway
Khilaf al-qiyas is an exceptional ruling that overrides standard analogy due to a special legislative cause, and it is a key concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Wasiyyah as an Example of Khilaf al-Qiyas
Example of wasiyyah as khilaf al-qiyas and limits on analogizing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why wasiyyah is considered khilaf al-qiyas and state which other contracts cannot be analogized to it.

Core Idea
Wasiyyah is an exception because it transfers ownership after death, a time when rights normally cease.

This lesson focuses on the example of wasiyyah (bequest) provided in the passage. Wasiyyah is a classic example of a ruling contrary to qiyas. The Sharia allows it as a means for charity despite the general rule that ownership transfers require the owner's presence. We will also learn why other contracts like gift and sale cannot be attached to death by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of wasiyyah
Wasiyyah is a transfer of ownership attached to after death. At death, the person's rights in property cease and heirs' rights attach.
This makes it normally impossible to transfer ownership.
2
Identify the 'contrary to qiyas' element
The general rule is that ownership transfers require the owner to be alive and capable. Wasiyyah contradicts this by being effective after death.
The exception is granted for charitable purposes.
3
Recognize the prohibition of analogizing other contracts
The passage explicitly forbids extending this exception to other contracts like hiba, i'arah, bai', and ijarah. They cannot be made conditional on death.
These contracts require immediate or deliverable effect.
4
Distinguish between a bequest and a deferred contract
If a person merely directs after-death sale or loan of an item, that is a valid bequest of that action (وصية بالبيع), not a present contract deferred.
The difference is crucial for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه رأينا في مناسبات عدة أن كثيراً من الأحكام تقرره الشريعة على خلاف القياس، أي تعطيه حكماً استثنائياً على خلاف مقتضى القواعد العامة السارية على أمثاله لوجود اعتبارات تشريعية خاصة به. فالوصية مثلاً تمليك مضاف لحالة ما بعد الموت، وهي الحالة التي تنقطع فيها حقوق الشخص في أمواله وتتعلق بها حقوق ورثته. فالوصية ثابتة على خلاف القياس، لأنَّ الشارع أراد أن يفتح بها للشخص باباً يتدارك به ما فاته من أعمال البر والإحسان التي قد تشحُ بها نفسه حال الحياة خشية من الاحتياج. فلا يجوز أن يقاس عليها تجويز إضافة غيرها من التصرفات إلى ما بعد الموت كالهبة والإعارة، والبيع والإجارة مثلاً؛ بأن يعقدها الشخص في حياته عقداً مضافاً إلى ما بعد الموت أما لو أوصى بأن يباع بعد موته شيء معين من تركته لفلان بنصف قيمته مثلاً، أو أن يعار له، فهذا سائغ لأنه وصية بالبيع أو الإعارة، لا عقد حاضر مضاف الأثر إلى ما بعد الموت. ملاحظة : ويجب أن يلحظ أن ما ثبت على خلاف القياس يمكن أن يقاس عليه في العلة التي خالف فيها القياس كدفع الحرج وسد الذرائع حيثما دعت الحاجة إليهما . هذا وقد قالوا إن أحكام الوقف تستقى بطريق القياس من أحكام الوصية، فتقاس مثلاً مسؤولية متولي الوقف على مسؤولية الأوصياء في تنفيذ الوصية .
Chapter Ten: General Maxims in Fiqh. We have seen on several occasions that many rulings are established by the Sharia contrary to analogy (qiyas), i.e., it gives them an exceptional ruling contrary to the general rules applying to similar cases due to special legislative considerations. For example, a bequest (wasiyyah) is a transfer of ownership attached to a state after death, a state in which a person's rights in his property cease and the rights of his heirs attach to it. So the bequest is established contrary to analogy, because the Lawgiver wanted to open for the person a door through which he can make up for acts of piety and goodness that his soul might have been stingy with during life for fear of need. It is not permissible, therefore, to analogize upon it the permissibility of attaching other dispositions to after death, such as gift (hiba), loan (i'arah), sale, and lease, by the person making a contract during his life attached to after death. However, if he gives instructions (wasiyyah) that after his death a specific item from his estate be sold to so-and-so at half its value, for example, or that it be lent to him, then this is permissible because it is a bequest of sale or loan, not a present contract whose effect is attached to after death. And they have said that the rulings of waqf are derived through analogy from the rulings of wasiyyah, so for example, the responsibility of the mutawalli (administrator of waqf) is compared to the responsibility of executors (awsiya') in executing the will.

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of rulings that are established contrary to qiyas (analogy) in Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that many Sharia rulings are exceptional and not derivable by general analogy. He uses the example of wasiyyah (bequest) to illustrate: normally, a transfer of property after death is invalid because ownership rights cease upon death, but the Sharia allows bequests as an exception to facilitate acts of charity. Key Terms
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): A ruling that deviates from the general analogy due to a special cause (illa).
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest; transfer of ownership attached to death, valid for up to one-third of the estate without heir consent.
الهبة (al-hibah): Gift; a contract that requires immediate delivery and cannot be attached to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT permissible as a bequest (wasiyyah) according to the passage?
A) Instructing that a specific item be sold after death.

Key Takeaway
Wasiyyah is an exceptional ruling contrary to qiyas, and its exception cannot be extended to other contracts like gift or sale.

Analogizing from Exceptional Rulings: The Illa and Waqf
Principle of analogizing from exceptional rulings and waqf example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that rulings established contrary to qiyas can be analogized based on their specific illa, using the example of waqf derived from wasiyyah.

Core Idea
Exceptional rulings can be extended to new cases if they share the same underlying cause (illa).

In this lesson, we examine the important note that Dr. al-Zarqa adds: rulings that are khilaf al-qiyas are not isolated; they can serve as precedents for other cases that share the same illa (cause) for which the exception was made. The passage then gives the example of waqf (endowment), whose rulings are derived by analogy from wasiyyah. This illustrates how exceptions can generate new rulings through qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
The text states that what is established contrary to qiyas can be analogized upon in the cause (illa) for which it contradicted qiyas.
The illa is the reason for the exception, like removing hardship or blocking means.
2
Identify the illa for wasiyyah
The illa for the exception of wasiyyah is to allow a person to make up for missed charitable acts, removing hardship.
This is a form of daf' al-haraj (removing hardship).
3
Apply the principle to waqf
Waqf shares the same illa of enabling ongoing charity (sadaqah jariyah). Therefore, waqf rulings are derived by analogy from wasiyyah.
For example, the responsibility of the mutawalli is analogized to that of an executor (wasi).
4
Recognize the limits
Analogizing from exceptional rulings is restricted to the specific illa; it does not extend to other aspects.
One cannot analogize from wasiyyah to all deferred contracts indiscriminately.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه رأينا في مناسبات عدة أن كثيراً من الأحكام تقرره الشريعة على خلاف القياس، أي تعطيه حكماً استثنائياً على خلاف مقتضى القواعد العامة السارية على أمثاله لوجود اعتبارات تشريعية خاصة به. فالوصية مثلاً تمليك مضاف لحالة ما بعد الموت، وهي الحالة التي تنقطع فيها حقوق الشخص في أمواله وتتعلق بها حقوق ورثته. فالوصية ثابتة على خلاف القياس، لأنَّ الشارع أراد أن يفتح بها للشخص باباً يتدارك به ما فاته من أعمال البر والإحسان التي قد تشحُ بها نفسه حال الحياة خشية من الاحتياج. فلا يجوز أن يقاس عليها تجويز إضافة غيرها من التصرفات إلى ما بعد الموت كالهبة والإعارة، والبيع والإجارة مثلاً؛ بأن يعقدها الشخص في حياته عقداً مضافاً إلى ما بعد الموت أما لو أوصى بأن يباع بعد موته شيء معين من تركته لفلان بنصف قيمته مثلاً، أو أن يعار له، فهذا سائغ لأنه وصية بالبيع أو الإعارة، لا عقد حاضر مضاف الأثر إلى ما بعد الموت. ملاحظة : ويجب أن يلحظ أن ما ثبت على خلاف القياس يمكن أن يقاس عليه في العلة التي خالف فيها القياس كدفع الحرج وسد الذرائع حيثما دعت الحاجة إليهما . هذا وقد قالوا إن أحكام الوقف تستقى بطريق القياس من أحكام الوصية، فتقاس مثلاً مسؤولية متولي الوقف على مسؤولية الأوصياء في تنفيذ الوصية .
Chapter Ten: General Maxims in Fiqh. We have seen on several occasions that many rulings are established by the Sharia contrary to analogy (qiyas), i.e., it gives them an exceptional ruling contrary to the general rules applying to similar cases due to special legislative considerations. For example, a bequest (wasiyyah) is a transfer of ownership attached to a state after death, a state in which a person's rights in his property cease and the rights of his heirs attach to it. So the bequest is established contrary to analogy, because the Lawgiver wanted to open for the person a door through which he can make up for acts of piety and goodness that his soul might have been stingy with during life for fear of need. It is not permissible, therefore, to analogize upon it the permissibility of attaching other dispositions to after death, such as gift (hiba), loan (i'arah), sale, and lease, by the person making a contract during his life attached to after death. However, if he gives instructions (wasiyyah) that after his death a specific item from his estate be sold to so-and-so at half its value, for example, or that it be lent to him, then this is permissible because it is a bequest of sale or loan, not a present contract whose effect is attached to after death. Note: It must be observed that what is established contrary to analogy can be analogized upon in the cause (illa) for which it contradicted analogy, such as removing hardship and blocking means, wherever need arises. And they have said that the rulings of waqf are derived through analogy from the rulings of wasiyyah, so for example, the responsibility of the mutawalli (administrator of waqf) is compared to the responsibility of executors (awsiya') in executing the will.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of rulings that are established contrary to qiyas (analogy) in Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that many Sharia rulings are exceptional and not derivable by general analogy. He uses the example of wasiyyah (bequest) to illustrate: normally, a transfer of property after death is invalid because ownership rights cease upon death, but the Sharia allows bequests as an exception to facilitate acts of charity. This exception cannot be extended by analogy to other contracts like gift or sale, because the rationale (illa) is specific. However, he notes an important principle: the exceptional ruling itself can be used as a precedent for other cases that share the same specific cause (illa), such as removing hardship or blocking means to harm. He also mentions that waqf (endowment) rulings are analogically derived from wasiyyah rulings. This passage is foundational for understanding the role of qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): A ruling that deviates from the general analogy due to a special cause (illa).
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest; transfer of ownership attached to death.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment; permanent dedication of property for charitable purposes.
العلة (al-illah): The effective cause or reason behind a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the note in the passage, what can we do with rulings that are khilaf al-qiyas?
A) They cannot be used as precedents at all.
B) They can be analogized in the cause (illa) for which they contradicted qiyas.
C) They can be extended to all similar cases without restriction.
D) They can be used only for wasiyyah and waqf.

Key Takeaway
Rulings contrary to qiyas can be extended by analogy to new cases sharing the same cause (illa), as demonstrated by waqf being analogized from wasiyyah.

The Distinction Between Religious Obligation and Judicial Enforceability
Presenting examples where Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between religious duty and court enforceability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where the Hanafi school distinguishes between ḥukm al-diyānah and ḥukm al-qaḍā' and apply this distinction in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a ruling may be binding religiously but not enforceable in court — the realm of conscience is wider than the realm of the judge.

As a mufti, you will encounter cases where what is morally and religiously required differs from what a court can enforce. The Hanafi school has developed a clear distinction between the ruling of religious obligation (ḥukm al-diyānah) and the ruling of judicial enforcement (ḥukm al-qaḍā'). In this lesson, we will parse several examples from the text to understand when and why these two realms diverge, equipping you to give sound fatwas that respect both the letter of the law and the realities of human behaviour.

Guided Path
1
Identify Each Legal Case
Read the passage and list the four distinct scenarios: mistaken payment, picking up a lost item, prescription (taqādum), and creditor self-help. Note the key facts of each.
The cases are presented consecutively; you may need to separate them mentally.
2
Determine the Ruling Diyānatan
For each case, extract what the passage says about the religious obligation (diyānah). What is the person required to do in terms of conscience and before Allah?
Look for phrases like 'يجب', 'سبيلها الصدقة', 'يرد في حكم الديانة'.
3
Determine the Ruling Qaḍā'an
For each case, identify whether the court would enforce the ruling or not. The passage often states what the judicial system does not approve or only prevents hearing.
Pay attention to 'إنما يمنع سماع الدعوى', 'نظام القضاء لا يقر'.
4
Extract the General Principle
From the cases, articulate the overarching principle that explains when and why diyānah and qaḍā' diverge. Consider the reasons given: 'فساد الذمم', 'ليس سبباً مملكاً', intention behind the action.
The principle is that diyānah is broader and considers inner conscience and actual rights, while qaḍā' is limited by procedural and evidentiary rules.
5
Apply to a New Scenario
Given a novel situation (e.g., a debtor who has hidden assets but the creditor cannot prove it in court), apply the diyānah/qaḍā' distinction to formulate a fatwa response.
Use the creditor self-help case as a model analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يجب رد ما دفعه الإنسان على ظن أنه مدين ثم تبين خلافه كما تقدم (ر: ف ١٦/٨١).

وإذا التقط الشخص اللقطة لنفسه كان غاصباً ضامناً. فيجب أن يلتقطها بقصد حفظها وتعريفها وردها لصاحبها متى ظهر. وإن لم يظهر صاحبها فسبيلها الصدقة.

- وعلى هذا أيضاً كان التقادم (أي مرور الزمان) إنما يمنع فقط سماع الدعوى بالحق أو بالعين أمام القضاء. ولكن المدعى عليه لا يسقط عنه الحق ولا يتملك الشيء بالتقادم، بل عليه رده في حكم الديانة، لأن التقادم ليس سبباً مملكاً من أسباب الملكية، وإنما هو حاجز قضائي كما تقدم بيانه (ر ف ٤/٢٣) .

أما إذا كان أخذ مال الغير مستنداً إلى حق ثابت فإنه يجوز ولو دون رضى صاحب المال.
بل قد أفتى المتأخرون بأنه يجوز من جهة الديانة أخذ الدائن حقه الصحيح الثابت من مال المدين المماطل أو الجاحد إذا دخل في يد الدائن ولو بدون علم المدين ومن غير جنس الدين وليس مقضياً به، وذلك لفساد الذمم كما تقدم وإن كان نظام القضاء في الشريعة لا يقر له هذا الأخذ. وهذا مما يختلف فيه حكم الديانة عن القضاء (ر: ف ٤/٢ و ٢٣/ ۱۰ الحاشية الأخيرة).
Likewise, it is obligatory to return what a person paid under the assumption that he was a debtor, then it became clear otherwise, as previously (ref: 16/81). And if a person picks up a lost item for himself, he is a usurper liable for compensation. Rather, he must pick it up with the intention of preserving it, announcing it, and returning it to its owner when he appears. If its owner does not appear, then its course is charity. And also on this basis, prescription (i.e., passage of time) only prevents the hearing of a claim regarding a right or a property in court. But the defendant does not become free from the right nor does he own the thing by prescription; rather he must return it in terms of religious obligation (ḥukm al-diyānah), because prescription is not a cause of ownership among the causes of ownership; it is only a judicial barrier, as previously explained (ref: 4/23). As for taking the property of another based on an established right, it is permissible even without the consent of the owner. Indeed, the later jurists have issued fatwa that it is permissible from the perspective of religious obligation for a creditor to take his established correct right from the property of a delaying or denying debtor if it comes into the creditor's hand, even without the debtor's knowledge, and even if it is not of the same type as the debt and is not adjudicated, due to the corruption of consciences as previously, even though the judicial system in Sharia does not approve this taking. And this is one of the matters in which the ruling of religious obligation differs from that of judiciary (ref: 4/2 and 23/10, the last footnote).
Source pages p. 978

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion presenting multiple cases where the Hanafi school distinguishes between what is binding religiously (ḥukm al-diyānah) and what is enforceable in a court (ḥukm al-qaḍā'). The author follows a pattern: first, the case of a mistaken payment – even though the payee believed he was a debtor, he must return the money religiously, but the court may not enforce it after a certain time. Second, a lost item (luqṭah) – if one picks it up for himself, he is a usurper and liable, but if he picks it up with the right intention, he must preserve and announce it, and if the owner never appears, he must give it in charity. Third, prescription (taqādum) – the passage of time only prevents the court from hearing the case, but the right itself remains, and the defendant must still return the property religiously. Finally, the most significant example: a creditor may take his debt from a debtor who is delaying or denying, if the creditor gets hold of the debtor's property, even without the debtor's knowledge and even if the debt is of a different type – this is permissible religiously (diyānatan) but the court will not uphold it. This illustrates a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the realm of religious conscience (diyānah) is broader than the realm of judicial enforcement (qaḍā').

Key Terms
حكم الديانة (ḥukm al-diyānah): The ruling according to religious obligation and conscience, binding before Allah but not necessarily enforceable in court.
حكم القضاء (ḥukm al-qaḍā'): The ruling according to judicial system, enforceable by court, though may differ from religious obligation.
التقادم (al-taqādum): Prescription; passage of time that bars court hearing but does not extinguish the right or transfer ownership.
اللقطة (al-luqṭah): A lost item found by someone; the finder must preserve and announce it, not take it for himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a creditor who takes his due from a delaying debtor's property without court permission is:
A) Considered a thief and must return it.
B) Permitted to do so religiously (diyānatan) but may face judicial consequences.
C) Considered the rightful owner if the debt is proven.
D) Only allowed if the debtor agrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between diyānah and qaḍā' allows muftis to give practical guidance that respects both religious conscience and judicial reality, often resulting in different rulings for the same case.

Reversed Istishab and the Hierarchy of Proofs
Explains reversed istishab and proof hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy when a muthbit (primary proof) conflicts with a murajjih ibtida'i (preliminary preferrer) and identify cases of reversed istishab.

Core Idea
Present existence can prove past continuity, and primary proofs always override preliminary presumptions.

In this passage, we explore two intertwined evidentiary concepts: reversed istishab, where a present state is used to infer a past state, and the fixed hierarchy between the four decisive proofs (muthbitat) and the preliminary indicators (murajjihat). Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti when weighing evidence in litigation or fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ordinary and reversed istishab
Identify both directions: ordinary istishab traces past to present; reversed istishab (istishab maqlub) uses the present to indicate the past. Note the example of the father's wealth at the time of litigation being used to infer his wealth at the time of spending.
Reversed istishab is a distinct tool in Hanafi usul, not universally accepted by other schools.
2
Identify the two categories of evidentiary tools
Classify muthbitat (bayyina, iqrar, yamin, nukul) as final preferrers (murajjihat niha'iyya) and murajjihat ibtida'iyya (asl and dalaalat al-hal) as preliminary preferrers. Understand that the former are decisive proofs, the latter merely presumptive.
The passage states: 'when one of the four muthbitat conflicts with a preliminary preferrer, the muthbit prevails without doubt.'
3
Apply the hierarchy to a concrete scenario
Take the creditor-debtor example: if the debtor denies, and the creditor produces bayyina, or debtor confesses (iqrar), or the debtor refuses the oath (nukul), the muthbit prevails. Reason: it is 'clear proof of the invalidity of what the other party holds onto from a presumption or circumstance.'
The debtor's denial is supported by the original presumption (asl) of non-liability, but the muthbit overrides it.
4
Extract and explain the underlying maxim
The passage concludes with the base maxim: 'Evidence is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the presumption.' Understand that bayyina is used to overturn the apparent (zahir), while yamin is used to confirm the original presumption when no evidence is brought.
This maxim governs the allocation of burden of proof in Hanafi law.

١٠٦٩
Chapter (82) Analytical View of the Rules of Proof: Here, when the indication of the circumstance (qarina hal) testified for the son regarding the father's wealth at the time of litigation, it outweighed the presumption (asl) that the father held onto, which is non-wealth. The same is said in all branches of adjudication by circumstance, which are called: cases of reversed presumption (istishab maqlub). It is called 'reversed' because the ordinary istishab is considering past existence as extending to the present time. As for this, it involves considering the present existence as based on the past. So the father's wealth at the time of litigation was extended to the past because it was considered evidence of his wealth at the time of spending. An example of this is seen in the earlier case of dispute over the interruption of the mill's water. 7/82 - Secondly: If one of the four previously mentioned proofs (muthbitat) conflicts with one of the preliminary preferrers (murajjihat ibtida'iyya), then without doubt the substantive evidence (dalil muthbit) is taken, because it is clear proof of the invalidity of what the other party holds onto from a presumption (asl) or circumstance (dalaalat hal). Examples of this are abundant: if a creditor sues a debtor, and the debtor denies the debt, and the plaintiff produces evidence (bayyina), or the debtor confesses (iqrar), then judgment is given against him. Likewise, if the plaintiff requests an oath (yamin) and the defendant refuses (nukul), then judgment is given against him. This is because each of confession, refusal, and evidence is a final preferrer (murajjih niha'i) that is resorted to in order to rebut the apparent (dhahir) and prove the occurrence of its opposite. Upon this basis, the legal maxim was laid down: 'Evidence (bayyina) is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the presumption.' (Ref: 100/81). 1069
Source pages p. 1003

Commentary
This passage opens with an analytical discussion of evidentiary principles, specifically the concept of 'reversed istishab' (الاستصحاب المقلوب). Ordinary istishab traces past existence into the present, but reversed istishab does the opposite: present existence is taken as proof that the same state existed in the past. The author illustrates with the case of a father's wealth: the father's current wealth at litigation time is used to infer he was wealthy when obliged to spend, overriding his claim of poverty based on the original absence of wealth. Then the author moves to a second scenario: when one of the four primary proofs (bayyina, iqrar, yamin, nukul) – called 'muthbitat' – conflicts with a preliminary preferrer (like the original presumption or circumstantial indication), the muthbit prevails. This is because the muthbit is a clear proof that overturns the preliminary appearance. Examples include a creditor's evidence (bayyina), the debtor's confession (iqrar), or the debtor's refusal to take an oath (nukul); all result in judgment against the debtor. This leads to the famous Hanafi maxim: 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the presumption.'

Key Terms
الاستصحاب المقلوب (al-istishab al-maqlub): Considering present existence as indicative of past existence (reversed presumption of continuity).
المثبتات (al-muthbitat): The four primary proofs: bayyina (evidence), iqrar (confession), yamin (oath), nukul (refusal to swear).
المرجحات الابتدائية (al-murajjihat al-ibtida'iyya): Preliminary preferrers: asl (presumption of continuity) and dalaalat al-hal (circumstantial indications).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when one of the four muthbitat (e.g., bayyina) conflicts with a preliminary preferrer (e.g., asl)?
A) The preliminary preferrer prevails because it is based on original presumption.
B) Neither prevails; the judge must seek additional evidence.
C) The muthbit prevails because it is a clear proof that invalidates the preliminary appearance.
D) The case is decided according to the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi evidence theory, reversed istishab allows present circumstances to indicate past states, and the four muthbitat (bayyina, iqrar, yamin, nukul) always override preliminary presumptions.
